HEADLINE: Rezoning the Sex Shops

 BODY:
As might have been predicted, Mayor Rudolph Giuliani's plan to impose strict zoning regulations on sex-related businesses is being criticized by opponents who fear that the new rules will drive such establishments into their neighborhoods. Council Speaker Peter Vallone and Councilman Walter McCaffrey of Queens, the plan's key supporters when the Council approved the plan in draft form late last year, are standing firm. They are right to do so because the Mayor's strategy of regulating the location and concentration of sex-oriented adult businesses is sound.
Under the current proposal, which the Council must vote on again if, as expected, it is approved by the City Planning Commission in September, sex-related businesses could operate only in manufacturing districts and "commercial zones of high density" in Manhattan, including parts of this newspaper's Times Square neighborhood. To prevent any area from becoming an unappealing "red light" district, the plan pursues a dispersal strategy. It would prevent sex-related businesses from operating within 500 feet of residences, schools and houses of worship and within 500 feet of one another. 
 Though it appears that many opponents would be unhappy with anything short of an all-out ban, Mr. Vallone and Mr. McCaffrey warn correctly that tougher limits would probably be found to trespass on the constitutional rights of the affected businesses. Clearly, the city must proceed with care. Sexually explicit videos, books and dancing are forms of constitutionally protected free expression. While courts have allowed municipalities to limit sex businesses upon a showing of harmful impact on the economy and public safety, local governments may not unduly restrict access. Too severe a crackdown could invite a successful legal challenge that would leave neighborhoods completely unprotected from the spread of X-rated video stores and topless bars.
Indeed, as the commission reviews all of the testimony from its two days of hearings this week, it needs to be especially attentive to criticism that the proposal is too restrictive and would unfairly impinge on free expression. There is still a chance to review the zoning maps in detail and to make sure the final version of the plan is adequately respectful of both neighborhoods and free expression. The worst result would be a zoning plan that makes people feel good and politicians look good momentarily and then fails to withstand a court challenge.

LENGTH: 238 words

HEADLINE: Use Real Trains for New York Airport Rail Link;
Lower Manhattan Ferry

 BODY:
To the Editor:
The loss of some 5,000 jobs connected with the coffee and cotton exchanges has dealt yet another blow to the viability of Lower Manhattan as an important commercial center. Thousands of jobs in banks, brokerage houses, insurance companies and law firms have been lost to midtown Manhattan, the outer boroughs and other states. 
 There are many reasons for the steady flow of jobs away from the downtown area, but surely one of the most important is the lack of good commuting options from suburban areas. The best solution would be the construction of a downtown rail station with connections to New Jersey Transit, Metro-North and Long Island Rail Road lines.
Unfortunately, there are no rail lines in Lower Manhattan and it is difficult to imagine that in these budget-conscious times funds for their construction will be found.
A decent and far less costly alternative would be to construct a Hoboken-style rail-ferry terminal at Hunters Point in Queens. The terminal could be reached by diesel Long Island Rail Road trains and even by Metro-North trains rerouted over Hell Gate. From the terminal, ferries could transport commuters directly to the Wall Street area.
Commuting by ferry has proved popular with New Jersey commuters; there is every reason to believe it would be popular with commuters from Westchester, Connecticut and Long Island.
 
SCOTT N. SINGER
New York, July 21, 1995

SECTION: Section 7; Page 20; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 939 words

HEADLINE: New & Noteworthy Paperbacks

BYLINE: By Laurel Graeber 

 BODY:

THE COLD WAR: A History
By Martin Walker. John Macrae/Owl/Holt, $14.
The author looks back on the conflict with some nostalgia: it helped maintain global stability, he argues, and encouraged economic growth. "With lively prose and well-chosen anecdotes, he deftly traverses a half-century," David Callahan said here last year.
THE COLLECTED STORIES
By Grace Paley.
Noonday/Farrar, Straus & Giroux, $14.
Moments of recognition, love, remembrance and regret fill these 45 stories, which span more than 25 years in the author's career. The characters, often Jewish New Yorkers, are revealed mostly through dialogue, recorded with an unfailing ear for the vagaries of self-expression. Ms. Paley's "distinctive, sure-footed command of the short-story form brings to fictional material a quality that must be called poetry," our reviewer, Robert Pinsky, wrote in 1994. He added, "By this I mean not mere verbal sauce . . . but the ability of language to dance with playful, lordly authority about the contours of reality." 

THE ROOSEVELTS: An American Saga
By Peter Collier with David Horowitz.
Touchstone/Simon & Schuster, $15.
Theodore and Franklin were both progressive political leaders, but they could not have been more different in their familial relations. The authors portray Teddy as authoritative but deeply devoted, Franklin as aloof and Eleanor as just plain cold. The book "offers vivid and persuasive analyses of their personalities," John A. Garraty said in these pages last year, calling it "truly first-rate popular history." Henry F. Graff, writing in the Book Review in 1979, found "history on the grandest scale" in FRANKLIN D. ROOSEVELT AND AMERICAN FOREIGN POLICY, 1932-1945, by Robert Dallek (Oxford University, $18.95). Reissued with a new afterword, this study shows how F. D. R. guided international relations in a way unsurpassed by any other modern President. Mr. Graff praised its "scope, clarity and judiciousness."
SHEAR
By Tim Parks. Grove, $11.
A British geological consultant investigating the cause of a construction accident rocks the foundations of his own life through two unscrupulous love affairs. The result is an "eerie, engrossing and beautifully written suspense novel," David Sacks said here last year.
THE CIVILIZATION OF EUROPE IN THE RENAISSANCE
By John Hale. Touchstone/Simon & Schuster, $18.
Drawing on and enlarging upon the scholarship in Jacob Burckhardt's 19th-century study "The Civilization of the Renaissance in Italy," the author describes the sweeping social changes and emergent European identity that characterized the entire Continent between 1450 and 1620. Last year our reviewer, Theodore K. Rabb, called the book "a sweeping and learned traversal of the age, full of choice details." The scope is even wider in A HISTORY OF CIVILIZATIONS, by Fernand Braudel, translated by Richard Mayne (Penguin, $14.95), which gives an overview of both Eastern and Western cultures. This "effective and readable" study is "enlivened by Braudel's supple prose," David Walton wrote in the Book Review in 1994.
BLACKER THAN A THOUSAND MIDNIGHTS
By Susan Straight. Anchor/Doubleday, $11.
Darnell Tucker, a young black man in the author's fictional community of Rio Seco, Calif., doesn't have extravagant ambitions -- his love is fighting brush fires -- but even his modest dreams are imperiled by his impending fatherhood. "Darnell's painstaking struggle to do the ordinary things becomes extraordinary in Ms. Straight's gifted hands," Rosemary L. Bray said in these pages in 1994. Anchor/Doubleday has also reissued UNDERSTAND THIS, by Jervey Tervalon ($10.95), a first novel that explores the underside of black life in Los Angeles's urban sprawl just after a drive-by shooting. The story moves "with spirit, energy and truthfulness," Michael E. Ross wrote here last year.
BALSAMROOT: A Memoir
By Mary Clearman Blew. Penguin, $9.95.
The history of the women in the author's family is bound up with the history of their land: the rough terrain of central Montana, where homesteaders led an often lonely existence. Ms. Blew reflects on the choices made by her Aunt Imogene, now elderly and senile, as well as on her own choices and her daughters'. In 1994 Susan Lowell said in the Book Review, "It's hard to imagine a novel casting a longer shadow than this personal testament." A sometimes harsh life is also examined in FLAT ROCK JOURNAL: A Day in the Ozark Mountains, by Ken Carey (HarperSanFrancisco, $10), which records 24 hours of the seven years the author and his family spent without plumbing, electricity or any of the conventional links to the wider world. "You have to admire Mr. Carey for living so independently," Dulcie Leimbach wrote in the Book Review last year.
THE END OF VANDALISM
By Tom Drury. Fawcett Columbine, $11.
What begins as an ironic look at working-class lives in the Midwest becomes something far more poignant, our reviewer, Eric Kraft, said in 1994. This novel's characters, who include a local sheriff and his new wife, achieve a kind of grace through pain. It is as if the author "had discovered the kind of informed sympathy for the living that the visitor to hell feels upon returning to the light," Mr. Kraft remarked.
AIR & FIRE
By Rupert Thomson. Vintage Contemporaries, $12.
Southern California in the 1890's is like a colonial outpost. It is here that Theophile Valence, a fictional disciple of Gustave Eiffel, tries to erect a prefabricated iron church. "This often surreal, evanescent story takes careful reading, but the effort is well worth it," David Murray said here last year. LAUREL GRAEBER

HEADLINE: CONNECTICUT GUIDE

BYLINE: By ELEANOR CHARLES

 BODY:

SLAPSTICK AND MUSIC
 "Music and Merriment" is the title of an entertainment featuring Julian Sperry, flutist, and Graham Lea-Cox, pianist, composer and director of the English Performing Arts Ensemble. They will appear at the Klein Memorial Auditorium in Bridgeport on Saturday at 8 P.M. 
 Their act is anchored by music from a variety of periods and genres, spiced with British wit, the occasional donning of masks, and the screening of a 1925 silent slapstick film underscored by a musical race through the works of Tchaikowsky, Offenbach, Ravel, Gershwin, Saint-Saens and Rimsky-Korsakov. In another segment the songs of Noel Coward, selections from grand opera and American ragtime are juxtaposed with lighthearted abandon.
Tickets are $10, and the theater is at 910 Fairfield Avenue. Call 333-5000 for reservations and directions.
 
'MOBY DICK' MARATHON
 The birthday of Herman Melville, Aug. 1, 1819, is to be be celebrated for the 10th consecutive year at Mystic Seaport. From noon tomorrow until 11:45 A.M. on Tuesday, the seaport staff and visitors will take turns in a marathon reading of "Moby Dick."
The event is called "Call Me Ishmael" and it takes place aboard the Charles W. Morgan, generally acknowledged to be the last surviving wooden whaling ship in the world. The vessel is permanently berthed at the seaport's Mystic River docks.
Some diehards who return for the reading year after year stay through the night, some leave after their stint is over, and many stay for a few hours or less, particularly people with children in tow.
Anyone planning to make a night of it should arrive fortified with something to sit on and something to eat.
Admission is free with seaport admission of $16, $8 for young people from 6 through 15, free to children under 6. Arrival by 11:30 A.M. is advisable in order to catch the whaleboat demonstration at 11:45, just before the reading starts at noon.
The seaport is off Exit 90 of Interstate 95, and one may call 572-0711 for more information.
 
CHAPLIN MUSICAL
 A musical about Charlie Chaplin is the featured production in a new musical theater conference at the Eugene O'Neill Theater Center, 305 Great Neck Road in Waterford, running from tomorrow through Aug. 12.
"The Little Tramp," by David Pomeranz and Steven David Horwich, will have eight readings. An additional three musicals in developmental stage will not be open to the public.
The performances will be held at 8:15 P.M. Thursdays through Saturdays with Saturday matinees at 2 P.M. Tickets are $8 and $10 and may be reserved by calling 443-1238.
 
SONO PARTY
 South Norwalk will hold its 19th annual SoNo Arts Celebration on Washington Street and throughout the surrounding area at the mouth of the Norwalk River on Friday from 7 to 10 P.M., Saturday from 10 A.M. to midnight, and Sunday from 11 to 6. Admission is free.
The streets will be crammed with booths containing the work of more than 200 juried artists and artisans from across the country. Continuous music and dance will be performed on four stages and dozens of vendors in two food courts will offer comestibles from many ethnic and regional cuisines.
Features that have become traditional are the children's art playground, the kinetic sculpture race at noon on Saturday, and the Saturday night block party with three bands providing music for dancing until midnight.
New this year are the parade of the giant puppets, handcrafted by local artists, and a beer buffet marking the opening of the New England Brewing Company on Marshall Street.
Last year's celebration attracted 100,000 visitors to the area, which is closed to traffic for the duration. Motorists can take Exit 14 northbound or 15 southbound off Interstate 95 and follow the signs. The number to call for more information is 866-7916.
 
STAGED READINGS
 The National Theater of the Deaf, based in Chester, has scheduled an international conference at Choate Rosemary Hall in Wallingford.
New plays will be presented and conference participants will attend seminars, lectures and workshops in which the development of playwriting by deaf authors, and marketing to a wider audience will be explored.
Three spoken and signed readings will be performed on Wednesday, Thursday and Friday at 7 P.M. in the Paul Mellon Center for the Arts on the Choate Rosemary Hall campus.
"I See the Moon," by Michele Maureen Verhoosky; "The Water Falls," by Willy Conley, and "Fish Nor Fowl," by Eugene Bergman, will run consecutively.
The National Theater of the Deaf, founded in 1967, entertains hearing and hearing-impaired audiences by combining the spoken and the signed word.
Tickets are $8 at the door or one may call 526-4971 for reservations or more information. The phone number for people with impaired hearing is 526-4974.
 
STRANGE DOINGS
 In producing his full-length color film "Oddities, Oddities: In the Footsteps of Robert Ripley," William Stockdale has trekked through underground churches at Lalibela, Ethiopia; spent some quality time in Hell, Mich.; filmed the tourist trade that pumps the economy of Arles, France, where van Gogh was once the resident lunatic, and recorded the doings of strange animals, fish, bodies of water, Turkish ice cream that stretches like warm mozarella, and other Ripleyesque phenomena.
His movie will be shown on Saturday at 8 P.M. at the Willimantic Camp Meeting Association grounds, one mile south of Threadmill Square in Willimantic on Route 32, or coming from the other direction, 2 to 3 miles north of the stoplight in South Windham on Route 32. Admission is $3.
 
MARBLES AND JACKS
 Hoop rolling, ninepins, bilboquet, croquet, top spinning, marbles, jacks and other games from the sometimes distant past will be played at the Children's Annual Lawn Party at Roseland Cottage in Woodstock from 11 to 4 on Saturday.
The rose-colored mansion at 566 Route 169 is owned by the Society for the Preservation of New England Antiquities. It is a National Historic Landmark and the site of many Victorian-inspired events throughout the year.
Families may take along their own picnics and spend the day being entertained by a 1870's costumed magician, joining in a peanut hunt and getting involved in the making of kazoos, fans, small parachutes and similar crafts. Admission is $2 and the phone number for more information is 928-4074.
 
HAITIAN MUSIC
 The fiery Haitian and reggae music of Boukan Ginen will be heard at the Globe Theater in Norwalk on Thursday at 8 P.M. Now five years old, the 10-member ensemble is an offshoot of Boukman Eksperyans, an established group of Haitian musicians.
Boukan Ginen won Haiti's Carnival Champions Prize in 1991, and has since toured the United States, Canada and Europe. A second recording, for Xenophile, is in the works for release next year.
The theater is at 71 Wall Street and tickets are $10 at the box office, through Ticketmaster, or call 866-2999. ELEANOR CHARLES

GRAPHIC: Photo: The Haitian ensemble Boukan Ginen will perform Thursday night in Norwalk. (Stephen Homer)

SECTION: Section 9; Page 9; Column 1; Real Estate Desk

HEADLINE: Commercial Property/Fifth Avenue in the 50's;
As Troubles Ebb, It's Becoming The Avenue Again

BYLINE: By CLAUDIA H. DEUTSCH 

 BODY:
OUTSIDE of New York, few people realized there had been a change. Out there, a Fifth Avenue address still conjured up an image of an elegant street where an uninterrupted line of expensive stores like Tiffany blended with tourist-pleasers like St. Patrick's Cathedral.
But recent reality has been far less pretty. Over the last decade even Fifth Avenue in the 50's, the avenue's "best" -- translation: priciest -- section, has been splotched by electronics and rug stores. High-fashion windows were eclipsed by seemingly permanent "going out of business" signs, and the ubiquitous street peddlers were turning the avenue into an obstacle course. 
 "It was like a flea market," said Tom Cusick, president of the Fifth Avenue Association. "Many good retailers took one look and said, 'I don't really want to be here.' "
Now, Fifth Avenue is again living up to its posh reputation. The shoe store A. Testoni and St. John's Knits, both upscale retailers, have moved to Fifth Avenue and 53d Street, replacing electronics stores. Liz Claiborne is building a big store on the southwest corner of 52d Street, where persistently vacant space coexisted with an electronics store and a low-end men's clothing store. The Ferragamo family, which owns the building next door, has evicted an electronics store and a rug store, and is said to be negotiating with a high-fashion retailer.
In other spaces, retail excitement is replacing respectable dullness. Banana Republic is at 53d Street, in Japan Airlines' former space. Gianni Versace is building a huge emporium, complete with roof garden, where Olympic Airlines once sat. And the Warner Brothers Studio Store is in a long-vacant bank branch at 57th Street.
Some retailers are even choosing Fifth over Madison, reversing a flight of quality that reached a fever pitch when Barney's opened at 61st and Madison in 1993. One reason is the many tourists-cum-impulse-buyers that Fifth Avenue attracts.
"On Madison people shop specific stores, but on Fifth they shop the street," said H. Gene Silverberg, chief executive of Bigsby & Kruthers, which recently opened the Knot Shop, a tie store, across from St. Patrick's.
"Tourists definitely go to Fifth Avenue; everything else is optional," agreed Efraim Grinberg, president of North American Watch, which opened a Movado store at 52d Street last year.
That idea keeps getting converts. Faconnable, the men's clothing store, had always believed that their customer base was between 57th and 72d on Madison, said Alan Victor, an executive vice president of the Lansco brokerage who persuaded Faconnable to move to 54th and Fifth instead. Do they have regrets? "They're already talking about expanding," Mr. Victor said.
Fifth Avenue landlords, of course, could not be more delighted. Sumitomo Realty, which owns 666 Fifth Avenue, at 53d Street, is looking to replace the B. Dalton bookstore, which wants to move, and to fill space once occupied by Pastrami & Things.
"We will certainly get more for the space than we would have gotten if the area hadn't redeveloped," said Arthur L. Bocchi, a Sumitomo vice president.
Even landlords who say they were unaffected by the avenue's deterioration concede that new tenants have fewer qualms about moving in. "When you buy a house, you care about the neighborhood," said Kate D. Coburn, Rockefeller Center Management's vice president of retail leasing.
Fifth Avenue's landlords are trying to make sure the neighborhood stays classy. According to Mr. Victor, Olympic Tower Associates has turned down several mass-market retailers for space it has available at 51st Street. "They want a tenant in keeping with H. Stern, Mark Cross and Versace, the others on that block," he said.
The owners of the Crown Building at 57th have refused to renew the lease of a low-end jeweler, while the owners of the Scribner Building at 49th, where Brentano's is about to vacate 12,000 square feet, are taking their time negotiating deals.
"We are marketing the Crown space to tenants who attract the same type of shopper as Tiffany or Bulgari nearby," said Kenneth M. Krasnow, an associate director at Cushman & Wakefield who handles leasing at both buildings. "We'll go for a more popular-priced store at Scribner, but we still want a quality tenant."
(Since the Brentano's interior is landmarked, Mr. Krasnow is marketing it primarily to European retailers, since they are more used to making their own retail images conform to unchangeable contours.)
Such pickiness is terribly exciting to members of the Fifth Avenue Association, which has spent much of the last five years getting the avenue back in shape.
"It's hard, walking down Fifth today, to relate to what was going on in 1990," Mr. Cusick recalled. "Liquidation signs were all over, and peddling was out of control."
Moreover, many of the tackier stores had ironclad leases that economically pressed landlords felt forced to sign. "When the landlords were renting to the schlock stores, national chains weren't in the market," said Benjamin Fox, a partner at the New Spectrum store brokerage.
Getting rid of it all was no easy task. Although Fifth Avenue zoning has strict signage rules, it took several lawsuits to get all the offensive signs taken down. And it took enforcement of penalties for consumer affairs violations to purge the street of "ripoff stores," as Mr. Cusick calls the stores that sold overpriced goods to tourists.
PEDDLERS were even harder to oust. In 1990, a city court ruled that disabled veterans had a right to peddle anywhere in New York, and thousands of people applied for licenses. Many of them sold counterfeit versions of the goods being sold in the high-priced stores they parked in front of.
They were followed by book and magazine vendors who, although rarely disabled veterans, remained relatively unhassled by the courts or the police. After all, nobody wanted to be accused of being harder on sellers of constitutionally protected speech than they were on sellers of T-shirts.
The last group to arrive were the peddlers of watches and scarves. Most of them were unlicensed, and thus fair game for the law. But they usually displayed their wares in briefcases that could be shut in a second. Often they would pay lookouts to alert them if the police were anywhere near. By the time the officer arrived, the peddler was simply a person carrying a leather case.
Most of the peddlers are gone now. A new state law requires veterans to abide by the same standards as other vendors, which means Fifth Avenue's zoning rules can again prohibit street sales. Other legal changes make it possible to use witness testimony to prosecute unlicensed vendors who have folded up their briefcases by the time a police officer arrives.
"We had 1,818 encounters with vendors in the month of August 1993," Mr. Cusick said. "Eighteen months later we had one a day."
But not all is roses on Fifth Avenue. When Warner Brothers moved to 57th Street, a great hue and cry arose from people who felt that mass-market entertainment had no place on New York's most elegant shopping street. Their cries accomplished little: Niketown, another entertainment emporium, is opening across the street, in the Trump Tower space vacated by Galeries Lafayette. And Goldfeil and Bally of Switzerland, sellers of, respectively, ultraexpensive jewelry and accessories, are giving way at 55th Street to The Disney Store, seller of, well, Mickey Mouse paraphernalia.
Some brokers say such stores are better suited to Fifth Avenue's pedestrian mix. "Fifth Avenue gets the office workers and the Japanese tourists, but Madison has the wealthy East Side residents and European tourists that the quality retailers go after," said Richard Seligman, a senior managing director at Edward S. Gordon.
Others say the mass merchandisers represent a net gain for an avenue that can now be all things to all shoppers. "Fifth Avenue has become an all-around consumer friendly place," said Rob Nager, director of retail leasing at Colliers ABR, who handles leasing at 650 Fifth Avenue, at 52d Street.
The new populism has also helped revive Fifth Avenue south of Saks, where annual retail rents average $200 a square foot, compared with $400 north of 50th Street. And mass merchandisers, who normally rent huge spaces, do far better than that.
"It's less expensive to rent below that imaginary border, yet that's where a lot of the big spaces are," said Mr. Krasnow, who believes that the quality retailing zone of Fifth Avenue now extends to 42d Street.
Faith Consolo, managing director of the Garrick-Aug store brokerage, says that the lower stretch is particularly appealing to international retailers, because it offers the cachet of the Fifth Avenue address without the burden of the rents. "When they say 'Fifth Avenue,' no one asks '34th, 47th or whatever,' " Ms. Consolo said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: New Gianni Versace store, with trompe l'oeil canvas covering. (Todd Plitt for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Silent Tower On 10th Ave. Is Coming Back to Life

BYLINE: By DAVID W. DUNLAP 

 BODY:
Lewis Futterman once said he developed the 31-story Aurora apartment tower at 10th Avenue and 57th Street for "an upscale audience of people who want the convenience of being near their jobs in midtown and who want enough space for a baby grand."
No baby grands arrived, however. Neither did any residents. Like other projects that began in an expansive real estate climate but neared completion as the market was convulsing in the late 1980's, the Aurora fell into a limbo from which it is only now emerging. 
 The story of its salvage has to do in part with affordable-housing tax credits, a financing mechanism that is well used but little known outside development circles, and a 30-year abatement on all property taxes. And it has to do with an unusual alliance between a nonprofit service organization and a decidedly for-profit builder.
But it also has to do with the extra space that Mr. Futterman carved out. The oversize living-dining rooms, up to 30 feet long, will permit the subdivision of the 98 original apartments into 178 shared units for people who are elderly, disabled, under-employed or living with AIDS.
And after seven years as an empty shell, the Aurora will finally open next January, said Joseph P. Benincasa, general manager of the Actors' Fund of America, which has undertaken the project in partnership with the Related Companies, the development concern headed by Stephen M. Ross, and its financial service affiliate, the Related Capital Company.
The project has been financed largely through the sale of Federal tax credits to a "group of financial institutions with a significant presence in New York City," said Stuart J. Boesky, managing director of Related Capital. He declined to identify the purchasers in any greater detail.
The purchasers paid $34 million to get tax credits worth $46 million over the next 10 years. About one-third of the proceeds, or $10.25 million, goes to pay for the property. The rest is being used to finish construction, repay a loan, settle with creditors, reimburse Related for the money it has spent out of pocket and pay Related a development fee.
What tipped the economic scale in the project's favor, Mr. Boesky said, was the city's two-year-old Section 420c tax abatement program, usually used for the rehabilitation of single-room-occupancy hotels, under which the Aurora will not have to pay any property taxes for 30 years, amounting to a savings of about $600,000 a year.
That translates to $280 a month in forgiven taxes for every residential unit, said Commissioner Deborah C. Wright of the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development. She contrasted this with the $1,000 a month that the city spends to house single people with AIDS.
"We're getting 178 low-income units for a really small cost," Ms. Wright said. "Typically, when we use 420c, it's in the context of an almost totally subsidized project. In this case, the thing that's wonderful and fascinating is that the project was almost totally paid for by private development."
Low-income housing tax credits were established on a temporary basis by the Tax Reform Act of 1986, renewed several times after that and then made permanent two years ago. Credits are allocated to the states on a per-capita basis and administered by state housing agencies.
Typically, corporations will purchase the credits, paying less up front than the credits will be worth over time in reducing their Federal tax liability. That is how the investors make their money. Individuals also invest, but in most cases, can only offset $25,000 a year in Federal taxes.
The money paid for the credits goes to build or renovate housing for people whose income is 60 percent or less of the median income in a given area. Rents in qualifying projects can be no more than 18 percent of the area median income. (In the case of New York City, this calculation would yield a rent of roughly $450 a month.) The low-income units have to be maintained a minimum of 15 years to qualify for the credits.
As a rule, Mr. Boesky said, tax-credit equity represents about 40 to 50 percent of the financing for a project, with the rest of the money being borrowed. Investment partnerships put together by Related might involve 10 corporations that have staked $10 million each or 3,000 individuals who have each invested $25,000, he said.
To date, Mr. Boesky said, Related Capital has raised $1.2 billion in tax-credit equity, which has been used to develop 37,000 housing units around the nation. "We're probably the biggest low-income housing production machine in America," he said. "Where it used to be the Federal Government, it's now Related."
ANOTHER major operation is the National Equity Fund, which describes itself as the largest nonprofit syndicator of tax credits, having raised $1.2 billion to produce 27,000 housing units nationwide. The fund is an affiliate of the Local Initiatives Support Corporation, a community development group started by the Ford Foundation and based in Manhattan.
Locally, the New York Equity Fund has seen tax-credit investment rise to $110 million in 1993, from $49 million in 1989. The National Equity Fund is a partner in this local group, together with a subsidiary of the Enterprise Foundation of Columbia, Md., an affordable-housing development concern founded by the developer James Rouse.
"There's a more confident investment by corporations," said William R. Frey, director of the New York office of the Enterprise Foundation. What has increased confidence, he said, are tangible results. "There's a product," he said. "Housing has been built. They have received a return on their investment for seven or eight years. There's more of a comfort level that, in fact, this a good vehicle to invest in."
Given its success, Related Capital keeps searching for further investment opportunities. "We were scouring the tristate area for failed 1980's real estate deals that had never been placed in service," Mr. Boesky said. "The reason we were doing this is that we happen to think it's a very exciting way to utilize tax credits."
That brought them to 10th Avenue and 57th Street.
The Aurora was begun in 1987, using development rights from a merged zoning lot formed with the Mission Evangelical Pentecostal Church immediately to the north and the Badem Buildings, a small apartment house immediately to the east. The architect was John Harding. He designed Mr. Futterman's Bel Canto apartment building on Broadway, near 67th Street, and worked on an unrealized project that would have linked the chateau-like former Towers Nursing Home at Central Park West and 106th Street, a city landmark, to a new apartment tower.
By 1989, the Aurora was 90 percent finished. About half the units had been spoken for. Studio apartments were being offered from $154,500 and two-bedroom units were being offered from $386,200. "We were selling very well," Mr. Futterman recalled in an interview last week.
But the project unraveled quickly. "It got into trouble all at once," Mr. Futterman said. "That was at the time that real estate values were tumbling right and left." In addition, interest rates were skyrocketing. "So much money allocated for construction ended up being eaten up by the interest rates," he said.
"We tried desperately to finish the project but we weren't able to accomplish it," Mr. Futterman said. Although he was "within an inch" of making a deal in late 1989 with the lead lender, Home Savings of America, he said that Home was unable to persuade the other banks involved. All the deposits were returned to the prospective buyers, he said, and the building sat empty "while everyone banged heads in court and elsewhere."
At about the same time as the Aurora was sinking, the Actors' Fund was confronting a real estate problem of its own.
Established in 1882 to provide for the welfare of entertainers, the fund rather naturally found itself in the business of helping pay rent for those who were disabled or down on their luck. By 1990, rent subsidies were consuming $2 million a year, Mr. Benincasa said, about half of all the financial assistance offered by the fund.
And the demand showed no sign of waning, particularly with the closing of one of the city's largest low-rent hostels, the William Sloane House, a 1,400-room Y.M.C.A. residence at 34th Street and Ninth Avenue.
Moreover, there was an ever increasing demand for housing and services from people with AIDS and H.I.V., the virus that causes AIDS. "There is a high incidence of it in the entertainment industry," said Cyril Brosnan, public affairs director of the fund.
All of that suggested that the Actors' Fund develop its own affordable housing, on a scale much larger than its existing nursing and retirement homes in Englewood, N.J. The search began in 1990.
Reaching out for experienced developers, the fund turned to Housing and Services Inc., whose projects include the renovation of the Narragansett, a single-room-occupancy hotel at Broadway and 93d Street; the conversion of the former Speyer School at 514 West 126th Street into an AIDS residence known as St. Mary's Episcopal Center and the conversion of a former orphanage at Woodycrest Avenue and 161st Street in the Bronx into an AIDS residence called the Highbridge-Woodycrest Center.
The Actors' Fund had several criteria. "We focused on areas where entertainment professionals live," Mr. Benincasa said, "so the West Side was very important." Also, Mr. Brosnan said, the fund wanted a building that it could occupy 12 to 15 months after purchase, which ruled out new construction in favor of renovation.
Some of the buildings that were briefly under consideration included the Holland Hotel, 351 West 42d Street; the Howard Johnson Plaza Hotel, 851 Eighth Avenue, between 51st and 52d Streets and a 22-story apartment tower at 255 West 85th Street.
But when Housing and Services brought the Aurora to the attention of the Actors' Fund in 1993, the search pretty much came to a halt. "After I saw it," Mr. Benincasa said, "it was the only serious contender."
THAT was when the Actors' Fund crossed paths with Related.
"We learned that Housing and Services Inc. had entered into a contract with the owner," Mr. Boesky said. "We reached out with the idea of buying out Housing and Services or doing a joint venture. We learned quickly that they were representing the Actors' Fund, which we thought was very exciting."
In the summer of 1993, negotiations began to resuscitate the Aurora. "It was a difficult deal to pull together," Mr. Boesky said, "because there was so much litigation and so many zoning issues around it." The new development team was required not only to buy the mortgage but also to settle all the outstanding lawsuits.
Complicating matters was the fact that the church had gone into court to detach its zoning lot from that of the Aurora, on the ground that it had not been paid by Mr. Futterman. The merger of the zoning lots had yielded about 20,000 square feet of extra development rights for the Aurora (roughly equal to 20 apartments).
"The judge gave the air rights back to the church," Mr. Boesky said, "so you had a situation where the building was no longer in compliance." Jeff T. Blau, vice president of the Related Companies, said the developers negotiated with the church to re-merge the lots and paid for the development rights.
There was another problem with the storefront church. Since it was part of the merged zoning lot, it was subject to the same requirement as the Aurora: that it be set back 10 feet from the sidewalk. Rather than move the building or chop off its front, the developers had to obtain a variance from the Board of Standards and Appeals.
All of the owners of the merged zoning lot had to sign the application before the board, and that occasioned a further delay when the landlord of the Badem Buildings balked.
Last year, the developers filed for their Section 420c abatement. Commissioner Wright confessed some initial skepticism. "I got a phone call about this project maybe six weeks into the Giuliani administration," she said. "It sounded like found money. And you know how fearful we bureaucrats are of something that looks too good to be true."
"But the tone the Mayor set was to be aggressive, try new ideas and find private investment," Ms. Wright said. "We took a deep swallow and, against all history, decided to be bold."
Marketing of the tax credits began in the fourth quarter of 1994, Mr. Boesky said, and construction began in early May. The closing occurred this month.
The architect of the renovation is Marvin H. Meltzer of Manhattan, whose other projects include two ambitious developments in the Bronx: the 265-unit Melrose Court, a new residential project on St. Ann's Avenue, between 156th and 159th Streets, and Crotona Park West, the rehabilitation of 563 units in 20 buildings, most on Fulton Avenue, south of the Cross-Bronx Expressway. The general contractor is Constantine Designers and Builders of Long Island City, Queens.
As built, the Aurora has four apartments on a typical floor: three one-bedroom units and a studio. Under the plans drawn by Mr. Meltzer, an extra bedroom is being added to each of the one-bedroom units and the studios are being converted into one-bedroom apartments. Some residents will share bathrooms; others will have their own.
On the second floor, the Actors' Fund will set up a community center, social workers' and volunteers' offices, a library and a large kitchen. The presence of these services enables the building to qualify as a "community facility with sleeping accommodations" rather than a residential development, Mr. Blau said, which permitted the new owners to increase the room count significantly.
The three chief criteria for admission to the Aurora will be age (a cut-off has not yet been set but it may 62 years and older), income (less than $20,000 a year) or health (symptoms of AIDS). As many as half the residents may come from outside the entertainment industry, Mr. Benincasa said. Monthly rents should run from $450 to $480.
One thing that isn't being changed -- at least for now -- is the building's name. Board members of the Actors' Fund came up with other ideas. For example, Rocco Landesman, president of Jujamcyn Theaters, suggested the Tommy.
"We like 'Aurora,' " Mr. Benincasa said. "There's continuity. But we're going to reserve judgment." He paused to contemplate the naming opportunity before asking, "Do you know anyone who wants to establish a $5 million reserve?"

SECTION: Section 13CN; Page 1; Column 1; Connecticut Weekly Desk

HEADLINE: Expanding System Metes Out Mashantucket Justice

BYLINE: By JULIE MILLER 

 BODY:
A MEDICINE wheel decorated with buckskin, trade beads, hackle feathers and the face of a fox hangs on the wall of the Mashantucket Pequot tribal courtroom. The medicine wheel and a judge's gavel, also embellished with beads and feathers, symbolize the traditions and customs that make the newly created tribal court different from non-Indian courts of law.
The opening of Foxwoods High Stakes Bingo and Casino and the breathtaking pace of its expansion -- nearly 10,000 employees have been hired since the casino opened in 1992 -- have made legal issues come to the forefront on the Mashantucket Pequot Reservation. 
 "Any time you have major economic development and an influx of employees and visitors, it makes sense that there will be more issues," said Melody L. McCoy, a lawyer with the Native American Rights Fund in Boulder, Colo. "The tribal court system provides a viable means of resolving conflicts."
The Mashantucket civil court was established in 1992. The criminal court was created a year later, and the juvenile court opened last March. The Mashantuckets have enacted criminal court ordinances and a children's code and are developing a civil code, said Henry Sockbeson, a lawyer for the Mashantuckets. Before the tribal court was established, the Tribal Council heard and decided cases for the tribe.
Native American tribal courts as a whole have become more active in recent years. Of the more than 500 American Indian tribes and Alaskan native villages, about 180 operate courts today, almost double the number 20 years ago, Ms. McCoy wrote in an article in Human Rights, a publication of the American Bar Association. The tribes vary widely in size, from the Navajo Nation of more than 200,000 members to bands with fewer than 50 members. There are 340 members of the Mashantucket Pequot Tribe.
A broad range also exists in the number of cases handled by tribal courts. In 1992 the Navajo courts processed more than 85,000 cases; some smaller tribes heard fewer than 10. It is not unusual for medium-sized tribes to handle 200 to 300 civil, criminal and juvenile cases yearly, Ms. McCoy said. One of the cases currently before the Mashantucket court involves employees at Foxwoods who were fired or asked to resign after an investigation by casino management of poker games off the premises.
Jill E. Shibles was hired last year as the court's chief judge. A member of the Penobscot Indian Nation in Maine, Ms. Shibles is one of only a handful of Native American judges in the eastern United States who are also lawyers. She earned a bachelor's degree in 1985 from King's College in Briarcliff Manor, N.Y., and a law degree in 1989 from the University of Maine.
Ms. Shibles became director of the Penobscot tribe's judicial system in 1990 and a judge two years later in the Passamaquoddy Tribal Court. She also had a private law practice in Maine, where she specialized in American Indian issues, criminal defense and family law.
Ms. Shibles, who is 31, worked first as part-time chief judge for the Mashantuckets. Last spring, she moved with her husband and two sons to Connecticut and began full-time work for the tribe.
"It's thrilling to be on the ground level," she said, "developing the tribal court and exploring new ways of dealing with the administration of criminal justice.,"
Lawyers who practice before the court are members of the tribal bar familiar with tribal law and court proceedings, Ms. Shibles said.
Michael Kurs, a partner in Pullman and Comley in Hartford, is among several lawyers who were recently sworn into the tribal bar. Mr. Kurs had experience with Indian law in two cases involving the Navajos, who live in Arizona, New Mexico and Utah. "The tribal court is like any other court in that a lawyer isn't allowed to practice there unless he is admitted," Mr. Kurs said. "Being a member made sense to me as an outgrowth of my work with the Navajo Nation."
Criminal jurisdiction is an important issue to tribal courts. Crimes committed on the Mashantucket Pequot Reservation fall under Federal, state, tribal or concurrent Federal and tribal jurisdiction, depending on the type of crime, whether or not there were victims and whether the person charged with the offense is Indian or non-Indian, Ms. Shibles said. A gaming compact between the state and the tribe allows the state to prosecute crimes committed in the gaming rooms of the casino.
Under a Supreme Court decision, Indian tribes do not have jurisdiction over non-Indians charged with offenses committed on their reservations, Ms. Shibles said. This does not sit well with the chief judge, who argues that since the Mashantuckets are like any other sovereign nation, non-Indians should be tried in the tribal court.
"I think the concern is that there might be some bizarre punishments," she said. "But I don't think you'll see those kinds of things. It should be up to the tribe to decide what punishment there will be under their laws."
The Mashantuckets consider rehabilitation, rather than detention, the main objective in criminal and juvenile cases. The court's intent is to get to the cause of a person's behavior problem with the help of social services, including mental health professionals and social workers, available on the reservation at no cost, Ms. Shibles said. But violations of the law can carry penalties. A breach of peace violation, for example, may be punished by up to six months in jail and a fine of up to $1,000.
In one recent case, a defendant was sentenced to 30 days in jail, which was suspended on the condition that he make restitution, get counseling and treatment focusing on substance abuse, domestic violence and anger management. If the defendant failed to comply, a hearing would be held and the portion of the sentence that had been suspended would be reimposed.
Teen-agers up to 18 fall under the tribe's juvenile code. (Connecticut courts classify teen-agers under 16 as juveniles and those 18 and over as adults.) Defendants who are 16 or 17 are considered adults. However, they may apply to the court for youthful offender status, which means they are treated as adults, but their records are sealed from the public.
The tribe shares the close ties of an extended family, and children receive strong support from other members. A new community center on the reservation offers a variety of activities for children during the summer. "We keep them really busy," Ms. Shibles said.
The only complaints against juveniles so far have concerned criminal mischief, Ms. Shibles said, and they were settled out of court in meetings between the tribal probation officer and the people involved in the incident. A resolution may require a youngster to do community service or make good for any loss or damage.
The Mashantuckets are exploring home detention as an alternative in severe juvenile cases. Rather than being in detention with other troubled youngsters, the child would leave home only to go to school, Ms. Shibles said. "For the underlying behavior problem, the child's family has resources available to help them," she explained. "There's little excuse."
Despite some problems, tribal courts are a flourishing and promising system, a unique blend of the traditional and the contemporary, Ms. McCoy wrote. But for their potential to be fully realized, steps must be taken to resolve the legal issues surrounding their jurisdiction and relationships with non-tribal governments.

HEADLINE: ROAD AND RAIL;
Bumpy Ride: A Faster Rail Link to Manhattan Runs Into Parking Problems

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN 

DATELINE: MAPLEWOOD

 BODY:
Like many residents here, Vicki Herzfeld Arlein is excited about the Kearny Connection, the rail link that will offer direct access to midtown Manhattan when it opens next spring.
But like many residents here and in other communities along New Jersey Transit's Morris and Essex Line, she is far from thrilled with the idea of a giant parking garage to accommodate new commuters, right in the heart of town. 
 "This was the mother of all issues recently, and there was a lot of broad-based opposition," said Ms. Herzfeld Arlein, who has been active in trying to stop the deck. "We were feeling that this would change what we like about Maplewood."
From Maplewood to Summit to Bernards Township, there has been a lively debate over New Jersey Transit's quest for additional parking. The agency expects 6,500 more riders a day over the next five years -- an increase of 50 percent for the Morris and Essex Line -- and it wants to accommodate some of them by adding 1,500 parking spaces, either in new garages or in expanded lots. By law, any parking built by the state transit agency must be open to all, not just local residents.
But when the connection opens in May, only 200 of the 1,500 spots, in Bernardsville and Far Hills, are expected to be available. By the end of 1996, the agency hopes to have 900 more. It has been a tough sell.
"We have run into some rough sledding," said Alfred Harf, assistant executive director of planning for New Jersey Transit. "I think there is a not-in-my-backyard sentiment that says we should be taking care of our own residents and have residents from other towns go somewhere else."
The transit agency says its proposed parking decks and lots would lure people to the train and contribute to local economies. Residents say the parking plans -- particularly those that involve new decks -- are too grand and too intrusive. And while the discussions have generally been civil, they make it clear how strongly people feel about physical changes to their communities.
This has been true even with the popular and much-anticipated $69 million Kearny Connection, which is expected to shave an average of 17 minutes off the commute to Manhattan when it opens in May. Until now, commuters have had to take the Morris and Essex Lines into Hoboken and transfer to the PATH. With the Kearny Connection, they will be able to ride the train right into Penn Station via the Northeast Corridor Line. (Trains will still run to Hoboken, for those traveling there or to lower Manhattan.)
Many local merchants favor the extra parking, saying their towns do not have enough as it is.
Residents who oppose it do so for several reasons. They say selling land to New Jersey Transit would take valuable property off the tax rolls. They fear the extra traffic. And they don't want to spend extra money to patrol and clean garages.
Nowhere, perhaps, have those feelings been more adamant than in Maplewood, where more than 2,000 people signed a petition this year to protest plans for a four-story, 400-car deck within a block of a park, a children's playground, the library and the middle school.
In March, the township formed a committee on parking and traffic to study alternatives to the deck. Its report is not yet complete, but already there are suggestions of a shuttle bus service and a reconfiguration of existing parking spaces.
In Summit, residents protested New Jersey Transit's plan to build a 600-space, four-level deck. Last fall, a group called the Committee to Save Summit collected 1,200 signatures, forced local officials to postpone acceptance of the deck and helped create a task force on the subject, said Marty Van Allen, a founder of the group.
Like Maplewood's, Summit's report should soon be ready. But some residents say the passion has subsided somewhat. Parking was an issue in the Republican mayoral primary in June, and Walter Long, a downtown businessman, defeated Judy McLendon, a prominent member of the Committee to Save Summit.
The idea of a deck was also floated in Millburn, but it was quickly dismissed after residents said they feared a deck would clog narrow roads leading to the station and raise serious safety concerns.
In Bernards Township, it was not a deck but a platform ramp and increased parking for the Lyons station that generated controversy, said H. Steven Wood, the township administrator. New Jersey Transit wanted 300 spaces; the township wanted 200. New Jersey Transit wanted a 300-foot elevated ramp to get to the train station; the township wanted only 100 feet. Negotiations are continuing.
And in South Orange, residents objected to a tiered parking deck for 470 cars but seem amenable to a proposal for two smaller garages attached to new stores to help revitalize its downtown, said Barbara Sacks, village administrator.
Back in Maplewood, even the most ardent of anti-deck residents agree that compromise is possible.
"We want to work with New Jersey Transit because they're great for the community," said Barbara Novak. "But let's not go for major surgery. Let's take an incremental approach."

GRAPHIC: Photos: The first in a series of rail improvements -- a connection that will shunt trains from the Morris and Essex Line directly to midtown Manhattan --is nearing completion in Kearny. (Sam D'Amico for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: CHATTER;
Whose Beach Is It?

 BODY:
Should New Jersey's beaches be open to everyone, or should towns have the right to restrict access?
 
A Question of Fees
 The coastal areas should be accessible to everyone as a national resource. But there is an economic element to this controversy: the question of access fees. While undeveloped coastal areas should be open and free, developed sections such as maintained sand beach facilities and boardwalks can justify a fee to recoup expenses on a nonprofit basis.
 
KENNETH A. CROSSNER
Somerset
 
Don't Drive Tourists Away
 Hundreds of thousands of New Jerseyans and out-of-staters visit our beaches every year. If we take away their access, they will spend their money elsewhere. And let us not forget that the state of our waters and beaches was not solely created by the beachgoers but also by lax corporate and Government standards of years gone by.
 
MARY BUYOFSKI
Perth Amboy
 
High Cost of Visiting
 I am in absolute agreement that the New Jersey coast should be off limits to those of us who are not privileged enough to own a half-million-dollar beach house or drop 3 grand in rent for two weeks "down the shore."
 
TRISH VANNI
Ewing
 
The Best Beaches
 I just returned from a stay in Ocean City, Md., and I have stayed at many beaches along the East Coast. The Wildwood beaches are the absolute best. What are they doing right down there?
 
ERIC HOWARD
Somerset
 
Sharing the Shore
 While I can understand the concerns of those who live in beach towns, there are many more towns that do not have the availability of beaches. Garden State residents should have the absolute right to enjoy all the wonderful beaches throughout the state. If this access is prohibited, what's next, a law that only residents, not visitors, can travel through or be in their town?
 
SHIRLEY FRAZIER
Paterson
 
Share the Burden
 Tax money needs to be allocated to bear the costs of maintenance, but that burden should be shared, as should the sands. Legislators who promote tax money for beach replenishing should also work for equal access.
 
JUDITH OWENS
Brick
 
Fees Are Too High
 One of the things I absolutely hate about New Jersey is the concept of having to pay for access to the ocean or beaches. When I lived in California, I could go to any beach any time for no fee because the beach is all public land. The pay beaches have lost my tourist dollars forever.
 
STEPHEN JARVIS
South River

LENGTH: 2355 words

HEADLINE: The Long, Shared Summer

BYLINE: By BARBARA STEWART 

DATELINE: SEA GIRT

 BODY:
TERRY McGUCKIN was one of the originals in the big, boxy cedar-shake Colonial house a few hundred feet from the shore in Sea Girt. That was summer 1990.
 Mary Bridget Schmid knew Terry from Bayonne High School and got into the house three or four summers ago. Patty Hardina knew Mary Bridget from the University of Dayton, a Marianist Catholic school in Ohio. 
 Patty had long known Sea Girt, a gracious resort town a mile square with block after block of fine old gabled houses with cupolas and wide lawns and wraparound porches. Four or five summers ago, she answered a Village Voice ad and bought a share in another big seaside house in nearby Manasquan.
 "I wasn't thrilled," Patty said of the Manasquan house. "People had their names on their jelly. Once I took a guy's salt and he got upset."
 When Mary Bridget and Patty got together during the winter, Mary Bridget would tell stories about the good times and the great people she had met at 3 Brooklyn Boulevard, Sea Girt -- the bicycling, the barbecues, the beer parties, the long days on the sand, blanket-hopping from one friend to another. The friendly young men, many Roman Catholic like them, with good jobs. The friendships, the engagements, the subsequent marriages.
 "That's all I'd heard about -- the shore," said Patty. "I really wanted to meet people and stuff." That first weekend at 3 Brooklyn three summers ago, she lugged her suitcase up the steps and into a house where she knew hardly anyone. "I walked into a strange house. To this day I could be in the wrong house."
 But it wouldn't much matter. At any of the big seaside houses that rent to groups of adults, Patty would be part of a long summer tradition, like sunscreen, sweet corn and heavy Friday afternoon traffic. For Patty and thousands of adults -- especially those in their 20's, and 30's -- summer began months ago with a down payment on a bed and a corner of the refrigerator. In return, the space between Memorial Day and Labor Day becomes an unbroken series of weekends by the sea, where the house, the roommates, the routine are comforting and familiar, where the days are taken up with sports or nothing at all, and the nights are filled with rounds of parties and always the hope of meeting somebody nice, somebody really right.
 Patty sits upright in a porch chair as she talks. Around her are a good handful of the 17 summer renters -- 14 full shares, who can come every weekend, and 3 half shares, who are allowed to come every other weekend. The porch is wide and inviting, as big as a living room, with open spaces between the pillars through which the beach is visible. An Irish flag flies in front of the house across the street. Sea Girt and Spring Lake are known as the Irish Riviera, towns where American Irish Catholics traditionally congregate. Each little town of about 2,000 has its own Catholic church.
 The air is oppressively hot. Mary Bridget, 29, an account manager for a hazardous-waste removal company, and Patty, also 29 and a vice-president in sales for Salomon Brothers, had a late lunch of salads with bottled low-fat dressing. Now they are passing around fat-free nacho Doritos, critiquing them and comparing them with the full-fat kind. Mary Bridget is outgoing, with dark hair down her back. Patty is demure and tidy, with neatly cut straight blond hair. Russ Mollica, who's 35, with an easygoing demeanor, digs out a handful.
 Before coming to 3 Brooklyn, Russ, who owns a Hoboken computer-import company, had spent a few summers at the Manasquan house where Patty was. He didn't like it much, either. "People came home at 4 in the morning," he said. "They were screaming and jumping off the roof." After a struggle, he says, this house has a "good group of people with similar living values."

IN other words, people in their 30's or close to it. People with good jobs, many in sales or accounting. People without sharp edges, who'll blend with the others. People who, for economic and philosophical reasons, prefer the Jersey Shore to the Hamptons. The shore is closer for New Jersey and New York City people, and cheaper; dinner out is a cheeseburger, not an arugula salad with nasturtiums, and party dress is clean shorts and sneakers, not silk and heels.
 Most important, "people with similar living values" are people who are single.
 "Ultimately everybody wants to meet somebody and stop doing the singles thing," said Patty.
 Of course, the immediate purpose of a seaside house is to play and see friends and rest after the winter and the workweek. But an underlying purpose is for men to meet women and women to meet men. Sea Girt in the summer is a great place to do this. The routine is easygoing. People who, in the city, would have to schedule a meeting far in advance drop by unannounced at one another's houses. The entire weekend is a big get-together, at the beach, on the porch, at bars and restaurants. It's a lot easier to connect with people lounging in shorts on a porch than sitting upright on an ergonomic office chair dressed in a business suit. Last year three couples, friends of the people at 3 Brooklyn, married.
 At summer's end, the friendships continue. The 3 Brooklyn housemates get together for drinks and dinner once a month. In the fall, they gather for the Far Hills Hunt. On New Year's Eve they party. Last year 10 women vacationed together at Newport.
 "Everybody I've dated for the past five or six years I've met here," Patty said. "I can't remember when I didn't meet at least one guy."
 Naturally there are customs, if not actual rules, governing behavior between men and women. If two people from the same house are attracted, they should wait until Labor Day to date. Housemates tend to treat one another as buddies, family members, people to whom they can confide while wearing old bathrobes. A man and a woman who were dating lived at 3 Brooklyn last summer; their fights made the others squirm.
 In the hierarchy of summer places, Sea Girt and Spring Lake are classy, while the nearby beach towns of Belmar and Manasquan attract a younger, wilder, messier and more impecunious crowd. At a summer house in Belmar, for instance, a group of at least 15 young men -- all of whom appeared to be in their late teens or early 20's and none of whom wanted their names used -- could be seen one overcast afternoon hunched around the tube, chanting at the set, swigging beer and smoking marijuana. The dishes piled in the sink, wet towels on the floor, seemed to create no tension, for there must be the concept of cleanliness for the lack of it to be troublesome.
 These young men would not be welcome at houses in Sea Girt or Spring Lake, nor could they afford it. The average summer rental in Sea Girt -- from Memorial Day to Labor Day -- costs $12,000 to $15,000. Three Brooklyn, a five-bedroom house built in the early 1900's, rents for $25,000 for the summer. It is owned by William Doyle, a Vermont State Senator who grew up in Sea Girt and whose father was Mayor there. Renting the grand old house lets him keep a presence in Sea Girt. He rents it for the winter, as well, and visits it with his wife and children in fall and spring. He's pleased with the current renters, though some in Sea Girt would like to do away with summer groups, all summer groups. Virginia Zientek, president of the Sea Girt Chamber of Commerce, opposed the group rentals, echoing the feeling of some longtime residents who regard summer people as noisy, messy and irresponsible.
 Terry Hemmer, the real estate agent who rents 3 Brooklyn, called her summer renters "professional adults."
 "We sincerely love them," she said. "They treat it like it's their own. The house is always left in immaculate condition."
 The rent is about $1,800 for a full share. The extra money goes for toilet paper, mustard, electric bills. Terry McGuckin and another housemate take care of the business at 3 Brooklyn. They talk to the real estate agent, recruit and approve new renters and collect the money, which must be paid in full by the preceding January. They tote up the phone bills and buy such essentials as coffee and hot dogs and margarita mix.

EACH renter buys his own food. The kitchen, which is as big as a Manhattan studio and has counter space and cupboards around its perimeter, is divided into sections. They keep their cans of soup in the cupboard, their milk and butter sticks in the extremely jammed refrigerator, and their snacks and liquor on the counter. Lined up are bottles of zinfandel, vodka, peach schnapps, tequila and Diet Pepsi. Meals don't warrant much attention. Breakfast is coffee and cereal or a muffin, eaten on the porch. Lunch is peanut butter or a Big Mac. Dinner is hot dogs or burgers or chicken on the grill or a sub sandwich or whatever can be scrounged at the Saturday night keg parties around town.
 An upstairs room has five beds, a downstairs one has four. The beds are narrow and the rooms have a dormitory look. In adjacent living rooms are three pull-out sofas on which guests -- or renters, when the house is full -- sleep. The renters pay for a maid to clean each week.
The weekend days in Sea Girt, as in any summer community, have an easy, holiday rhythm. Mornings, get a cup of coffee, sit sleepily on the porch in shortie pajamas or shorts and a T-shirt. Drive to the golf range. Bicycle or roller-blade. Lie on the beach, swim in the sea.
 Saturday nights mean big parties, and each of about 15 group houses, all with 10 to 20 single working people in their 30's, takes a turn. Every renter in the host house contributes $50 to $75 for beer, sandwiches and salads. Hundreds of people come: summer renters, their friends, weekend visitors staying at seaside inns. Many have come to Sea Girt summer after summer and have gotten to know most everyone. The big group houses are within blocks of one another, so guests can drink without worrying about driving. The hosts warn the neighbors and promise to clean up. They tell the police, who will come at 9:45 and make sure the party's closed by 10, when Sea Girt, by law, must be quiet.
 Saturdays build up to the big event, the party, and Sundays, in the party's aftermath, are a palpable lull. At the end of the summer, people dress up in long gowns and tuxedos for the Seashell Ball, with profits donated to St. Dennis's parochial school. The rest of the summer are T-shirt-and-beer parties.
 On one recent Saturday, party time is drawing near. Terry, Patty, Mary Bridget and Kristine Tedesco, who handles insurance for a New York City company, with a few others, crowd into the bedroom with four narrow beds and one bureau. They pull out baskets of shampoo, Nivea, razors, toothbrushes, loofas.

TERRY helps Mary Bridget lace up the back of a swingy, washed-demin dress. Patty sits cross-legged in front of the door mirror, blow-drying her hair, pulling it around the brush, blowing it into fine soft waves. Nonnie Narin, a TWA flight attendant, paints dark lines across her eyelids. It's a scene from Saturday night at a college dorm, which summer at Sea Girt greatly resembles, except, as one renter remarks, people have money and don't have to study.
 Patty, hair smooth, face pink with make-up, is neat in bright white shorts, a striped sleeveless shirt, and gold earrings and a gold chain necklace.
 "That's what you're wearing?" she asks Michael Chinque, her voice incredulous.
 "Yeah," he replied, looking down at his worn T-shirt. "The Daffy Duck."
 Patty turns to Brian Quirke, a Paterson accountant, eyes his cotton shirt and says, "You need to be ironed." He smiles.
 The party is a block away at a big Victorian house with a three-sided porch. People holding paper cups of beer are crowded onto the porch and spread in clumps across the lawn, around the beer kegs and the food. Some stand and cheer at the four-foot high slanted ice block with three tracks. One person, hunched at the bottom, mouth open, is trying to catch streams of vodka another pours from the top.
 Snatches of talk flow and fade. People approach and smile and hover, keeping intent watch on the movements of others. A self-described M.B.A., who one of the 3 Brooklyn Boulevard women thought was cute, walks off in the middle of her sentence.
 By 10, the party is over and the 3 Brooklyn men and women are back on their own porch. The evening hasn't panned out. Nobody's connected with anybody. But the housemates have one another. Several, including Russ Mollica, gather for a game of Pictionary. Others, however tired they may be, soldier on. The scene is moving to Gilly's, a Sea Girt bar.
 "I feel like going to bed," says Mary Bridget. "But I should go to Gilly's. There's not time to wallow in self-pity. You have to maximize your opportunities. That's why I should go to Gilly's."
 "Dave asked me where my tan was," says Patty morosely.
 "Bummer," says Russ.
 "I dated him once in the city -- he's kind of a wacko," Patty says.
 "Not a fun night," says Mary Bridget.
 Gilly's is packed. People squeeze by and consider themselves well settled with enough open space to stand. Fetching beer through the crowd is an ordeal. Talk is shouted. The 3 Brooklyn men and women stand together, eyeing acquaintances and strangers.
 Back home after an hour or so of this, at 1 or 2 in the morning, 3 Brooklyn rattles with noise and action. The Pictionary game is rousing, exciting the players far more than the keg party did. One woman is nuzzling a friend in the living room. Patty settles into a porch chair for a while and then rises to go to bed. The house is too noisy to easily sleep, so she'll turn on a machine that makes sounds like ocean waves.
 In the morning, the friends sleepily make their way to porch chairs, balancing coffee cups and critiquing the party. Russ brings back the newspapers. Brian heads out to bicycle. Mary Bridget and Terry drive to 10 o'clock Mass. Shortly, in just a few hours, everyone will head back to apartments in Bayonne or Paterson or New York City, apartments where most live alone. Only five more working days before they'll be back together again.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Part of the crew of 3 Brooklyn Boulevard in Sea Girt, a rambling old beach house that doubles as a vacation dorm for 17 young professionals, who carry on the tradition of summer as shared responsibility. SECTION: Section 13WC; Page 15; Column 1; Westchester Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 1020 words

HEADLINE: Together, 2 Magazines See Brighter Future

BYLINE: By ANN COSTELLO 

DATELINE: HARRISON

 BODY:
IT was a marriage of convenience between two little-known magazines, both aimed at international business managers. Separately, each was losing money. Together, they hoped to share staff in several departments and settle down to a long and happy -- and profitable -- life together.
Few Westchester residents were aware that one of the magazines, International Business, with a circulation of 62,000, had been published in Westchester for seven years and had been based for the last three in a shimmering glass box at 500 Mamaroneck Avenue in Harrison. Fewer still knew that the other magazine, Foreign Trade, was in McLean, Va. 
 Quietly, the British owners of Foreign Trade bought International Business on June 15 and immediately set about downsizing and restructuring it. International Magazine's staff members are now preparing to move from their spacious suite with its views of lushly landscaped grounds and adequate parking to a smaller space in Rockefeller Center in Manhattan.
The 10 or so remaining staff members are optimistic about the move. Especially upbeat is David E. Moore, a Rye resident for 43 years and the founder, editorial director and former chairman of International Business.
"A magazine is an extension of one's ego, an extension of myself," Mr. Moore said, "My staff has made this a top-quality magazine, but I sold it because I wanted it to grow."
The new owners, Labyrinth Group, a British company involved in books, videos and magazines, announced the sale in London on June 19. Labyrinth bought the magazine from American International Publishing Corporation for 1.5 million shares of Labyrinth stock now, plus a two-year note for $120,000 and the right to buy 600,000 shares at a later date.
The day after the sale, the new owners set about to further reduce staff size, which Mr. Moore had already whittled down over the last year. They also combined several departments with identical departments at Foreign Trade, which has a circulation of 33,000. Mr. Moore will be staying on at International Business as editorial director and will sit on the Labyrinth board. He said he hoped to continue his monthly column. He and his wife, Katherine, vice president of the Katonah Museum of Art, love London, he said, and look forward to making four trips a year there for Labyrinth board meetings. In the meantime, he will commute to the magazine's new offices in midtown Manhattan.
The new editor in chief, Lori Ioannou, had been a senior editor at the magazine for three years and enjoyed her 25-minute reverse commute by car from Bayside, Queens. The trip by train to midtown Manhattan will be considerably longer, but Ms. Ioannou's attention is focused less on commuting than on the magazine's future.
As she put together the August issue, her first, she said her hope for the success of International Business lay in her belief that its niche, the "international arena, is the wave of the future for business journalism."
The chairman of Labyrinth Group, Gerard Mizrahi, who lives in London, appeared in the Harrison offices the day after the sale and began the restructuring. He was joined by Labyrinth's executive director, Peter Campbell, who will supervise the marriage of the two magazines in their production, editorial design and advertising staffs over the next few months.
"We intend to keep the two magazines operating as two parallel publications," Mr. Mizrahi said, describing their two audiences as "non-overlapping." He explained that Foreign Trade "talks to smaller companies, and is focused on trade -- a practical approach," while International Business aims at larger companies' managers and covers "global issues and strategies."
The marriage of the two magazines and their cost-saving move out of Westchester raise questions about the commercial vacancy rate in Westchester and the health of the magazine industry.
Seven years is left on International Business's 10-year lease on the lush 7,917-square-foot suite at 500 Mamaroneck Avenue. The building is managed by Legacy Management, whose president, Tom Julius, said he felt that the space would be quickly sublet. "The building is 100 percent leased, and we have many inquiries about space every day," he said.
Gilbert Mercurio, executive vice president of the Westchester County Board of Realtors, estimated that the overall commercial vacancy rate in the county was 25 to 35 percent. He cautioned that some management companies count space as leased even if the tenants have moved out, so that true vacancy rates are difficult to determine.
"Fifteen percent used to be considered high," he said. "But, since the recession in 1990, the commercial sector vacancy rate has been much higher."
As to the overall health of the magazine industry, James R. Guthrie, executive vice president at Magazine Publishers of America, said that "a strong first quarter" had resulted in "a 10-year record for first half ad page and revenue gains." Translation: the magazine industry is experiencing a boom in advertising sales. But several New York advertising agencies said that the increase in the price of paper by at least 35 percent since the middle of last year had hurt many magazines, causing some to take "drastic measures."
Paul Schnabel, who worked as a salesman at International Business for four years before becoming publisher of Foreign Trade in February, was named publisher of both magazines after the sale. Mr. Schnabel said that Foreign Trade had grown "75 percent in ad sales in the first six months this year" but that International Business had experienced flat sales over the last year.
He said the move to Manhattan would be completed by the end of July and would serve to "better position International Business as a mainstream business magazine." He pointed out that before the purchase the sales staff spent 40 percent of its time taking trains to midtown Manhattan to meet with clients.
"The consolidation has been challenging," he said, "but it was needed to better compete in the market. Divided, we were in the red. Combined, we're in the black."
