SECTION: Section 3; Page 1; Column 3; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 2517 words

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Retirement's Worried Face

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

 BODY:
LIKE so many of his generation, Ronald McPherson switched careers in his 40's, going from social work to computers. But the job switching and then illness shrank his once-generous pensions. And now at age 56, Mr. McPherson finds himself scrambling in part-time work to save enough for retirement. "I never thought this would happen to me," he said.
Or take Fern Marx, who is 59. She quit college to marry and raise children, but at 40 found herself divorced. Like so many women her age in the same fix, Ms. Marx managed to build a late-in-life career, earning $60,000 as a researcher in Wellesley, Mass. In retirement, that will shrink to $20,000. "I have no choice but to work into my 70's," she said. 
 The great retirement crisis, so widely anticipated for the Baby Boomers who begin to reach retirement age in 2010, is not waiting for the millenium. Already it is quietly engulfing millions of Americans now in their last few years on the job. They had counted on golden years in retirement, and this may not be.
Since World War II, Social Security and company-paid pensions have been the two main pillars of the American retirement system. But the pensions are disappearing, and Social Security, while still intact, is the subject of such gloomy forecasts that the Ronald McPhersons and Fern Marxes of America try not to count on it in planning for retirement. In the transition to a new system -- one that requires people to pay for much of their own retirement from their own savings -- they see themselves caught short, without enough reliable income from pensions or savings or Social Security to maintain their old living standards.
Their stories, and their struggles, are giving younger workers an advance look at a future that is scary because it is still so opaque. Will some of the old system be preserved? If not, what in particular will happen to the huge middle class now earning $30,000 to $100,000 a year? Millions in this wage bracket still count on Social Security and company pensions for the bulk of their retirement income. If they now have to pay their own way, how much must they save? Is it $1 million, $3 million, $6 million?
"No one really knows," said Olena Berg, the Labor Department's assistant secretary for pensions and welfare benefits. "But any amount you save is going to leave you better off than if you don't save that amount. We are getting tens of thousands of inquiries about these issues. Clearly there is anxiety out there."
Clearly. The Baby Boomers are shedding an earlier silver-spoon optimism about their future, surveys show. And behind the Boomers, Mr. McPherson's children and other young people in their 20's are watching their parents' distress with growing apprehension, wondering if they, too, will be forced into harder times in old age.
The spreading nervousness is pushing millions of American workers toward a reluctant self-reliance. "Whether we like it or not, we are heading de facto to a system that has to depend on private savings," said James P. Smith, a labor economist at the Rand Corporation and the author of a just-published study with some unusual findings.
Self-financed retirement may be the wave of the future. But analyzing data from a survey of 7,600 households, Mr. Smith found that even many wealthy families lack enough savings to maintain their lifestyles in retirement, without considerable help from the old standbys, company-paid pensions and Social Security. Saving money is hard. By Mr. Smith's estimate, a four-person household needs $70,000 in annual income to begin saving significantly -- quite a threshold for a nation with a median household income of only $37,000.
"Many Americans, despite this threshold, have started to save, particularly those over 45," said Diane Colasanto, president of Princeton Survey Research. "But very few think this will lead to a comfortable lifestyle in retirement. They feel crunched."
 
What It Takes to Retire
 Wall Street fosters the anxiety. Brokerage houses and mutual funds are deeply engaged in the profitable business of investing the billions building up in retirement accounts. And their estimates of how much people must accumulate for retirement are often open ended.
Merrill Lynch, for example, estimates that a husband and wife earning $85,000 apiece when they retire together at age 65 will need at least $1.5 million in savings to come away with $100,000 a year in retirement income. But that estimate assumes that inflation will be mild and that $26,000 of the $100,000 will still come from Social Security.
Maybe. The Social Security fund is expected to run dry by 2029, and in anticipation, various plans are already afoot to scale back. One proposal would skew the payments toward the poor while cutting back what the wealthy receive, including Merrill's couple. Or perhaps in the 21st century, the Federal Government will halt the inflation adjustments in Social Security payments that since the 1960's have lifted the elderly out of poverty.
A similar cutback is well under way for company-paid pensions. These had their origin in World War II, when companies circumvented a wage freeze by offering pensions to their workers. What evolved was a system of guaranteeing the payment of a pension that often exceeded 50 percent of a worker's income, if he or she stayed on the job long enough.
These pensions, called "defined benefit" plans, reached their peak in 1985, when they covered 30 million people, and then went into a continuing decline. New rules raised their cost, and many corporations, engaged in downsizing, no longer wanted to encourage workers to stay for decades. A new system of "defined contribution" plans came into existence instead, and mushroomed.
Under the new system, workers themselves pay for retirement by depositing a percentage of their wages into special savings accounts, often called 401(k) plans, after the section of the law that authorized tax breaks for these accounts. Employers frequently match a part of their workers' contributions, but that is the extent of their obligation. When a worker retires, his pension consists only of the savings in his 401(K) and the return those savings earn. The company is off the hook.
Early this month, the Clinton Administration seemed to encourage the shift away from company-guaranteed pensions and Social Security. At a Labor Department gathering, Labor Secretary Robert B. Reich called on Americans to save more, but he demurred at the suggestion that he had given his blessing to the new retirement system. "We are simply acknowledging," he said later, "that where we are right now, a lot of people have to take action on their own to protect themselves."
 
Covering the Shortfall
 That is what Ms. Marx is discovering. After her divorce, she went back to school, took a job, and in 1980 became a researcher for the Center for Research on Women at Wellesley College. But she was too late. Like so many women in their 50's, drawn late into jobs both by the women's movement and necessity, Ms. Marx has not accumulated enough years to build up much in the way of benefits or retirement income from the 401(k)-type savings account that both she and her employer contribute to.
"My guess is that I will get about $10,000 or so a year from my savings and roughly the same from Social Security, but who knows what will happen to Social Security," she said. "Given the finiteness of my resources, I can't think about retiring."
Whatever happens, Ms. Marx probably won't be destitute. If she can't go on working -- and a bad back already limits her hours on the job -- there is a $200,000 home that could be sold, and $200,000 in other savings, apart from her retirement account, that could be drawn down -- savings squeezed from years of spartan living. But each such step would mean a harder old age.
"It is all very confusing to be 60," Ms. Marx said, referring to her next birthday, this month. "I can't see myself as an older person. I see myself, and must see myself, as someone who is still middle aged."
Mr. McPherson still hopes to stop working at 62. By one standard, he and his wife are rich. Although he brings home only $12,000 a year from teaching computer courses at San Bernardino Valley College, his wife, Gail, who is 42, earns $80,000 a year as the manager of a waste water plant for the City of Riverside. Their combined income puts them in the upper 20 percent nationally and they own an idyllic three-bedroom house in the mountains above San Bernardino. "It is beautiful here," Mr. McPherson said.
But maintaining this lifestyle in retirement will be dicey. The McPhersons are just finishing the process of raising five children -- three hers and two his by first marriages -- and they have only $176,000 in savings today. Again, by national standards, that is high for the McPhersons' age group, Mr. Smith found in his study, but probably not high enough to pay for retirement.
The McPhersons estimate they'll need $5,000 a month, before taxes, "to live comfortably in retirement." The income from savings must bring in at least $1,000 of that total, which means the current savings, now mostly invested in mutual funds, must return a healthy 7 percent a year, and more if inflation kicks in. He had once expected to collect $2,700 a month in pensions, but this has dwindled to $1,700. There will be $800 in Social Security. Now he is counting on his wife's pension, which he hopes will be $1,500 a month. (She has no Social Security from her city Government job.)
Mr. McPherson's retirement plans began to go wrong in 1986, when he resigned after 20 years as a social worker for Orange County and, switching careers, became a computer systems executive for the State of California. "I burned out," he said. "I could no longer take the strain of dysfunctional families."
The job switch, at age 48, meant giving up the $1,700 a month pension that Mr. McPherson would have received at age 62. Instead, he accepted a reduced $1,000 a month, starting at age 50, and it is this pension that the McPhersons now use to help build up their savings.
The computer job also promised a $1,700 a month pension, if Mr. McPherson had lasted until age 62. But sudden, severe illness -- a huge threat in any retirement system based on an individual's own hard work and savings -- struck Mr. McPherson two years ago, at age 54. The illness resembled chronic fatigue syndrome and it forced him into early retirement. That meant a cut in his state pension to only $700 a month.
He is well now, but his stepson, Rex Briggs, is marked by the experience of watching his parents' retirement income dwindle. At 23, Mr. Briggs is doing quite well himself, earning nearly $40,000 a year as a researcher in Norwalk, Conn., for Yankelovich Partners, a public opinion polling firm.
Most of his contemporaries, he finds in his research, are too burdened by expenses to save, although they think constantly about it. They have college loans to repay and credit card debts stemming largely from the costs of a social life aimed not just at pleasure, but also at the important task of finding mates.
Mr. Briggs is a saver, having started last year just weeks after he married his college sweetheart, Carmel. "When I see what is happening to my parents," he said, "the thought crosses my mind, what if I have to help them? And I absolutely would. What would that do to my retirement?"
So he is putting away roughly $500 a month in a 401(k) plan. The couple lives mostly on her salary as an accountant, a salary roughly equal to his. They still have debts from her college days, but they save anyway, by scrimping. They own only one car (she drops him at work), rent a modest apartment (home ownership is far off in their minds) and give up the old pleasures.
" I am torn," Mr. Briggs said, "between enjoying my youth and being scared to death that there won't be enough saved when it comes time for me to get out of the work force."
 
A Hero of Saving
 Already, the emerging retirement system is producing its heroes. Manuel Matsos is one. During the Labor Department's gathering to encourage savings, Mr. Reich introduced Mr. Matsos, who is 40, as an example of how to save for retirement on wages of $60,000 to $70,000 a year. Mr. Matsos, who was chosen from a focus group, offered two attributes that average Americans should embrace, Mr. Reich said, "so that when 65 comes, they can retire and relax."
First, take a second job if your wife stays home with the two sons, as Mrs. Matsos does. Mr. Matsos is plant manager for a company that makes cosmetic samples, and in his off hours he is the guitar playing leader of a band that plays at weddings and festivals in Baltimore's Greek-American community, of which Mr. Matsos is a member.
"I had chosen to work two jobs so that my wife could stay home," he said, "but I wasn't channeling the income properly, and I realized that if I did not change, I would have to work in my retirement years. Now 15 percent goes into savings."
That happens because of the second attribute that Mr. Reich seeks to promote. The Matsos family shuns consumerism. Given the family's income, cutting back sharply is the only way to save. Mr. Matsos is convinced of this, although his asceticism does not always sit easily. "Showing me living in a row home and driving used cars is not the image I want to project to the Greek community," he said.
Nevertheless, the Matsos family avoids fast food restaurants; the children spend too much in them. Summer vacations in a rented condo on the Maryland shore are taken with parents and siblings, who share the cost. Impulse buying is out, and coupon shopping is de rigueur. Only lessons for the children are spared.
"My oldest son is studying music and is enrolled in martial arts," Mr. Matsos said, "and if I have to drive my car another year to do this, I will."
 
A Pension in Doubt
 Angie Rogers has no hope of living on savings in retirement. At 45, she has worked 26 years at Fieldcrest Cannon Mill in Columbus, Ga., and today she earns $8 an hour operating a machine that folds towels. Like so many low-wage Americans, Ms. Rogers is counting on a Social Security pension that would pay her roughly $12,000 a year, or 72 percent of her current $16,600 base wage.
Nevertheless, for the first time, Ms. Rogers, who is single and childless, is accumulating cash savings. Five years ago, her union negotiated a 401(k) plan to which the employees can contribute up to 10 percent of their wage. The company partly matches up to 4 percent. Ms. Rogers contributes 6 percent of her wage, cutting back on new clothes and dinners out to do this.
While the savings won't pay for retirement, Ms. Rogers said, they are a buffer against poverty, and they come at a time when she is beginning to wonder whether Social Security will really be there for her.
"I sure do worry about it," she said. "Being in the baby boom generation, plenty of us are going to draw Social Security, and if we all start drawing, then before I am very old, the money won't be there."

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
A Bubble Grows Ever Bigger

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS 

 BODY:
So much for that collapse of technology stocks.
Last week the Philadelphia stock exchange index of semiconductor stocks set three record highs. Even with a dip on Friday, the index is up 103 percent for 1995. The previous week's plunge of more than 10 percent in a couple of hours is almost forgotten. 
 The recovery shows that faith in technology lives on, especially among the people running some of the largest mutual funds in the country. It is they who bought heavily to halt the fall and get prices rising again.
The mini-panic of July 19 was largely confined to the hours between noon and 2 p.m., when the Dow Jones industrial average tumbled 100 points and tech stocks did even worse. A rally ensued, wiping out most of the losses by the close. Would this never have happened if some money managers had not gone out to lunch that day? What would happen if they took vacations?
There will be no forecast in this column of when the tech-stock mania will end. It is widely believed that no price is too high for many tech stocks, simply because technology is in the process of transforming every human activity. To a visionary, that sounds inspiring. To a contrarian, it is reminiscent of the belief 15 years ago that inflation would never stop and no price was too high for an oil stock.
Just for argument's sake, however, let's assume that Michael Brown, Microsoft's chief financial officer, has good reason to pronounce himself mystified by that stock's surge this year. And then consider what will happen to Microsoft and a lot of other tech companies if Wall Street, for reasons good or bad, does turn negative some day.
One answer is that the stock options that are now making employees and executives wealthy will suddenly look less attractive. That will create two classes of people at those companies: the ones who got in early and the ones who didn't. With the jealousies will come demands from the late arrivals to be compensated more in cash and less in paper that may never pay off. Right now, many tech companies are able to pay relatively low salaries to key employees, with options making up the difference.
It is then that those companies will regret one of their great victories of 1994, when they used political pull to force accounting regulators to back off from making companies show an expense for the value of options handed out. Having won that fight, these companies can continue to report inflated profits. But if cash salaries someday have to be raised to compensate for the fact few options are being cashed in any more, that will mean that reported profits will suffer. So the companies will see their reported costs rise even as their employees are in fact getting less and are unhappy about it. And that drag on reported profits could help to delay the eventual recovery of tech stocks.
All that is, of course, sometime in the future. Now tech is king of Wall Street, and the gambling frenzy is on. The mutual funds that bet on technology are the market leaders, and get more money from eager investors. The funds use that cash to buy the same stocks, which sends them up even higher and brings in more money. Buyers who focus on value are getting out, but that doesn't matter.
Someday, however, it will. And after the fall both those who bought the stocks -- either directly or through mutual funds -- and those who work for the tech companies themselves will wake up with long-lasting hangovers.

HEADLINE: The Fast-Track Treadmill

 BODY:
To the Editor:
"Should I Have Left an Hour Earlier?" (June 18) reconfirmed my disappointment in executives who have done nothing to make the workplace more family friendly. Sadly, baby boomers who espoused a more family-centered life have chosen accommodation with the status quo. 
 Workaholism affects both families and society. Workaholics' spouses may be precluded from seeking challenging careers, and children who feel abandoned by a frequently absent parent may develop behavioral problems.
Those who choose to make family life paramount often must work at unsatisfying or low-paying jobs because the "good" jobs go to those willing to sacrifice family life and leisure time. Unemployment rises as companies downsize, increasing the workloads of decreasing numbers of employees, who become less productive from fatigue and stress. One prescription: make Juliet B. Schor's "The Overworked American" required reading for all chief executives, managers and business students.
 
ANDREA R. BROWN
Newton, Mass., July 20
 
The writer is a professional fund-raiser.

HEADLINE: NEW YORK 1945;
The War Was Ending. Times Square Exploded. Change Was Coming.

BYLINE: By SAM ROBERTS 

 BODY:
FOR millions of New Yorkers waiting anxiously in Times Square, World War II ended at 7:03 P.M. Aug. 14, 1945. At that moment, the 15,000 light bulbs on the Times Tower's zipper sign spelled out five words that touched off a tumultuous celebration: "Official -- Truman announces Japanese surrender."
After surviving years of scarcity and sacrifice, with London bombed, Paris humbled and Rome, Berlin and Tokyo occupied by foreign troops, New York had suddenly been elevated from the nation's major metropolis to the unrivaled world capital. 
 "All City 'Lets Go,' " a New York Times front-age headline exulted the next day, merely hinting at the outpouring to come later with V-J Day and the formal signing of the surrender. If the day's jubilant headlines reported the present, some of the advertisements suggested the future.
One, aimed at executives in the nation's largest manufacturing city, exhorted: "Bring Your Factory to Texas." The ad pointedly cited Texas's tax advantages, a large, semi-skilled labor force idled by peace and the potential markets developing in the Southwest. Another ad, from the Academy of Languages, promised readers with an eye on Latin America or East Harlem that by taking advantage of "the famous Ibarra method" they could learn Spanish in record time.
And in the classifieds (in which apartment seekers and others still insistently identified themselves as "Christian"), the greatest demand for houses was in the suburbs. Typical was an ad placed by an executive with two school-age children who was looking for a place with three or four bedrooms within one hour's commuting from New York City.
Today, the youngest World War II recruit would be close to 70 years old. Of all the people living in New York City today, fewer than one in four were even born in 1945, much less remember firsthand what the city was like then.
In the summer of 1945, New York was a city riding a wave of triumph, even as the undertow of the future began to tug at its feet. It was a time of unbridled self-confidence. The city had contributed 850,000 servicemen to the war effort. The war had transformed New York into the capital of the world. By 1946, seven airlines offered regular service between Queens and Europe, and the United Nations would later settled on the East Side of Manhattan, where the Modernist headquarters of an organization devoted to peace displaced a stand of odorous slaughterhouses.
As Jan Morris recalled in her book "Manhattan '45" (Oxford University Press, 1987), a promotional brochure from Bankers Trust proclaimed that New Yorkers were a people "to whom nothing is impossible."
"There were no strangers in New York yesterday," Alexander Feinberg wrote in The Times, reporting on the celebration of Japan's surrender.
But a Depression, a world war, migrations and government policies had already laid the foundation for profound political, social and economic change.
During the war, shortages of food-ration stamps for grocery purchases led more people to eat out. (Restaurants were serving eight million meals a day during the war compared with three million before.) Women moved in droves into the work force, replacing soldiers. The dearth of housing construction, coupled with an influx of defense workers, produced another shortage whose mixed legacy endures today: rent controls. When the war ended, 75,000 New Yorkers were still waiting for telephone service because so few new instruments were being manufactured. (By choice, not necessity, Mayor Fiorello H. La Guardia did not have a telephone on his desk at City Hall). A few months after V-J Day, 25,000 women mobbed Macy's after a sales clerk posted a sign advertising nylons.
Still, New York had not let a war a continent away (but within U-boat sight of New York harbor) disrupt its routine any more than necessary. The national curfew was midnight, but Mayor La Guardia insisted that New Yorkers be allowed to drink, legally, until 1 A.M. On West 56th Street, Miyako's, a traditional Japanese restaurant, took its orders from loyal customers -- not from Tokyo -- throughout the war. Broadway was awash with blockbusters, including "Carousel," "Harvey," "Oklahoma!" "On the Town" and a play, "The Glass Menagerie," by a popular new writer, Tennessee Williams. The city's baseball teams were doing less well on Aug. 14, 1945: The Yankees and Giants each in fourth place, the Dodgers in third.
 
THE OTHER SIDE OF THE GOOD OLD DAYS
During the war years, Brooklyn still had its own daily newspaper (Queens had two, the Bronx one) and its own baseball team (run by Branch Rickey, who, a year later, broke the sport's color barrier by recruiting Jackie Robinson). Harlem was still a mecca for aristocrats of black culture. Downtown, Manhattan was still home to Augustus Van Horne, the last descendant of Peter Stuyvesant.
In the summer and fall of 1945, millions of them turned out for separate ticker tape parades to welcome Eisenhower, de Gaulle, Gen. Jonathan Mayhew Wainwright, Adm. Chester W. Nimitz and Claire Chennault. Harry S. Truman visited that fall, in what was described as the first time a sitting President paid his respects to a New York City mayor at City Hall. La Guardia read the funnies on radio during a newspaper strike that year (providing, perhaps, besides the airport that bears his name, the most durable memory of his mayoralty).
But if, apart from the anxiety about loved ones overseas, the war years seem in retrospect like the good old days, they were not that good (though, in some respects, statistically, at least, better than today). About 165,000 families were living doubled up. Welfare consumed $70 million or nearly 10 cents of every dollar of the city's budget (compared with nearly 25 cents for the Human Resources Administration today). The poor were growing so out of hand that the Mayor tightened a ban on begging in the subway. New mothers were leaving about 1,000 babies a year at the New York Foundling Hospital.
New York, as it has always been, was a changing city in the 1940's. And, as always, the instruments of that change were considered suspect -- even when they were fellow Americans lured, like newcomers before and since, by the promise of better life in the form of defense industry jobs.
Puerto Ricans, outgrowing the island's agricultural economy, began migrating to the city during the decade in record numbers -- tripling their population to nearly 190,000 by 1950. As a result of an influx from the American South, the city's black population rose by more than 60 percent.
But, much more so than today, the city was racially segregated. It was still acceptable then not only to admit it but to justify it. Sacrificing civil rights for an opportunity to lure private investment to vitally needed housing, in 1943 La Guardia reluctantly agreed to municipal tax breaks that would enable the Metropolitan Life Insurance Company to build Stuyvesant Town in Manhattan -- apartments for which blacks need not apply. As the company's president explained at the time, "Negroes and whites don't mix."
The City Council adopted a resolution prohibiting city participation in any similar projects unless the agreements with private developers included an anti-discrimination clause. La Guardia, seeking to reconcile seemingly intractable goals, wrote the insurance company that if the tenant selection process at Stuyvesant Town were ever challenged in court, the city's position would be that racial discrimination should not be tolerated. Charles V. Hamilton, researching his biography of Adam Clayton Powell Jr., later discovered a copy of the letter in the Mayor's files with a handwritten notation: "Not sent."
The following year, Metropolitan Life announced plans for another housing project, the Riverton in Harlem. That was the same year that Harlem proudly elected Powell as New York's first black Congressman, projecting his advocacy involving issues like Stuyvesant Town onto the national stage and onto the House floor, where using the word "nigger" was still tolerated.
In Brooklyn's Little Harlem, as Bedford-Stuyvesant was known, a grand jury investigation concluded that "it has become dangerous and unsafe to traverse the streets of this area before and particularly after dark." The grand jurors took pains, though, to blame the situation not on race but on lapses in law enforcement and on social conditions, including too many people illegally collecting home relief and living tripled up or worse in former one-family homes, many of which had been carved into rooming houses for Brooklyn Navy Yard workers. (As if to further confirm that crime was an equal-opportunity employer, a sign posted in the changing rooms at S. Klein warned in Italian and Yiddish, as well as English: "Do not disgrace your family. The punishment for stealing is jail.")
The Brooklyn grand jury reported that "many churches have closed completely because their parishioners do not dare attend the evening services," that "groups of young boys armed with penknives of all sizes and other weapons roam the streets at will and threaten and assault passers-by," and that "many law-abiding children and adults residing in this area are therefore forced to carry similar knives and weapons for protection."
Foreshadowing an era plagued by similar problems but later bedtimes, the grand jury suggested that at 9 every night radio stations broadcast the question: "Parents, where are your children right now?"
Wartime manpower demands had shrunk the police force, which was further challenged after V-E Day by a growing arsenal of illegal guns brought home by returning servicemen. After V-J Day, there was nearly one murder a day committed in the city, or about double the previous year's rate. The toll for all 1945 was 292.
 
LA GUARDIA STEPS ASIDE; TAMMANY HALL IN DECLINE
Early that year, after three tumultuous terms, La Guardia decided not to seek re-election. The mayor, Thomas Kessner wrote in his biography, "left no party legacy, no structure, no institutional fusion or urban reform movement." After being shut out of the mayoralty for 12 years, the Democrats were revived by the election of William J. O'Dwyer, the former Brooklyn District Attorney (whose grand jury had produced the Bedford-Stuyvesant report). The selection of O'Dwyer, who had arrived in New York City from Ireland at the age of 19, marked the 11th consecutive time (since 1909) that the Democrats had chosen a mayoral nominee of Irish descent.
But the election of New York City's 100th mayor was also a turning point, even if it took another two decades for the decline of the Democratic Party machine to play itself out. Tammany, unable to meet the mortgage payments, had to sell its Union Square headquarters. Its demise was further signaled by two developments: New Deal programs had largely supplanted the party as a provider for the poor and powerless (by 1946, more than 100,000 people were receiving relief); and, in the year of the ethnically-balanced ticket, a Hollywood producer created campaign "newsreels" for the Democratic ticket, which foreshadowed the radio and television commercials that candidates would soon regularly broadcast to appeal directly to the voters.
Moreover, Edward N. Costikyan, a former Tammany leader, remembers: "World War II broke the geographic solidity of the Democratic Party's control over the electorate. After the war, people were more mobile and their long-term relationships with their district captains and district leaders disappeared."
Winning wasn't everything. What O'Dwyer inherited, he later recalled, was a city in which "not a single hospital had seen a paintbrush in the 16 years prior to my swearing-in ceremonies.
"In the meantime," he said, "the city's population had increased by a million residents, and each department was woefully undermanned. The smoke was choking New Yorkers, and the raw sewage had long ago discouraged fish in the bay or in the North River and had rendered our beaches dangerous for bathing . . . Veterans were returning home with no place to live . . . the rapid transit system, once our pride and joy, was falling apart and badly in need of equipment and repair."
The morning after the election, he also inherited a plan. A minion of Robert Moses, whose 1939 World's Fair had made the public -- and himself -- absolutely giddy about the future, delivered the master builder's master plan for reconstructing the city. His ambitious blueprint with a $1.5 billion price tag called for completing the Brooklyn-Battery Tunnel and Idlewild Airport and for new housing, as he prepared to bulldoze existing homes to complete a network of highways, including three elevated expressways that would slice across Manhattan.
Soon after, Cleveland Rodgers, a City Planning Commissioner, and Rebecca B. Rankin, librarian of the Municipal Reference Library, echoed O'Dwyer's lament. "New York has never kept up with the demands for vitally needed facilities and services," they wrote in 1947. "It has not solved the basic problems of existence. Nowhere are there more glaring contrasts between rich and poor. Life is hard for millions and living and working conditions are deplorable in many respects."
Life in the city was also increasingly congested. (Unlike many other American cities, New York had lots of undeveloped land then, but Manhattan still had more residents than Queens.) Gas rationing during the war had slowed the migration to the suburbs. But between 1940 and 1950, for the first time, more people moved out of the city than in (over all, though, the population rose from about 7.4 million to more than 7.8 million because of higher birth rates at the beginning of the Baby Boom). While nearly 100,000 more foreigners, 230,000 nonwhites and 130,000 Puerto Ricans settled in the city than moved away, at least 340,000 more non-Hispanic whites left than arrived -- a middle-class exodus that accelerated in succeeding decades.
 
THE RISE OF THE CAR, THE SPREAD OF HIGHWAYS
Meanwhile, more and more of those who had left were returning to work in the city by car. By 1950 (the year that alternate-side parking was instituted and one year after O'Dwyer finally doubled the nickel subway fare), nearly 120,000 people were commuting to the city by car -- more than three times the number only two decades earlier.
Even more fundamental changes would soon be wrought by the deliberate and the unintended consequences of the Government's postwar policies on housing, transportation and veterans' benefits as the city's political influence waned in Washington. At the beginning of 1945 -- and not since then -- the President of the United States, Roosevelt, was a native New Yorker. More congressmen came from New York City that year than represented the entire state of California.
That an expected recession, like the one that occurred after World War 1, did not materialize reflected, in part, the Government's decision not to limit veterans' pensions to the elderly and disabled but to subsidize the education of returning soldiers and their ability to buy a home. But the criteria for Federally insured mortgages generally favored suburban developments, not urban housing -- just as Robert Moses's network of parkways had opened Long Island, Westchester and Connecticut to an influx of New York City expatriates.
Plenty of people were left, though, and more were on their way. By the end of the decade, E. B. White would write a paean to New York, with a caveat. Never, he wrote then, had the city been "so uncomfortable, so crowded, so tense."
 
 
 
The Summer of '45: A Diary
 The summer is dominated by news of the war. Many foods are in short supply. Produce store owners close their doors to protest the price ceilings set by the Office of Price Administration. Horse meat is shipped from the Midwest for people who miss beef. Advertisers ask readers to support them because they are supporting "our boys overseas."

SECTION: Section 13; Page 4; Column 2; The City Weekly Desk

HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.;
Tales From the Sky

BYLINE: By CHASTITY PRATT 

 BODY:
FROM where Frank Kind works, sidewalks look like gray chalk lines, billboards are the size of postcards and Central Park is a tidy mosaic of bonsai trees and bushes.
When he is perched on a scaffold 45 floors above the cement sidewalk, Mr. Kind, 58, doesn't think about his brother-in-law Harry Heller, who fell from a scaffold and ended up with one leg shorter than the other. And he never looks down. "I just pay attention," he said, "to what I'm doing." 
 Mr. Kind has been a window washer for 43 years, since he was 15. When he started in the trade, he used a wooden chair suspended by rope from building rooftops or a cotton belt hooked around his waist and onto the outside of the building where he worked. In those days, scaffolds were little more than wooden planks powered by rope and muscle. Sometimes a belt would fray, a hook would pull free from masonry, a plank would tip. Window washers know the horror stories: four washers who fell from a scaffold at the Equitable Building in the 60's, another killed when his cotton belt tore in 1970, two others who died in 1990 when their scaffold plunged from the 12th floor of the Holiday Inn Crowne Plaza in Times Square.
But today, nylon has replaced cotton, and hooks are bolted into window frames. New skyscrapers have customized wire scaffolds, powered by electricity and equipped with walkie-talkies and fire extinguishers.
Everyone agrees that the window washer's job is safer. Still, working conditions are not for the weak of will or stomach. On Thursday, a fierce wind smashed a scaffold into a window of 919 Third Avenue, near 56th Street. A facade repairman crawled through the broken window onto the 22d floor, but another worker dangled from a safety rope 20 floors above the sidewalk until a lawyer in the building saved him.
Other enemies of the window washer are wild birds that attack when their building-ledge nests are disturbed and windows that slam shut, leaving a washer dangling -- sometimes for hours -- with no way to climb back inside. And there is the occasional thrill seeker, be it nudist or parachutist.
"I was on the 110th floor of the Twin Towers when the guy jumped out with a parachute," Mr. Kind recalled as he and his partner, William Bubak, 61, took a break from the hundreds of windows they wash regularly at 888 Seventh Avenue, a 45-story office tower at 57th Street. "He looked at me, I looked at him and he jumped out and pulled the cord. The Port Authority called me and asked why I didn't stop him. I said, 'I didn't have a parachute.' "
Cesar Gerena still remembers the time he was washing windows at an apartment building on 58th Street. "The guys inside say, 'Finally, we can see; it's that time again,' " Mr. Gerena said. "A lady walked past naked in the window right across from the ones I just washed. She did it every day at 8 A.M. That's New York."
Window washers in the city belong to Window Cleaners Local 2. Managers of large office and apartment buildings usually contract with maintenance companies, which get their workers from the union. The local president, Arnold Geller, said "business was good" until about 1985; then the recession brought commercial construction to a near-halt and forced companies to slash maintenance. Union membership plummeted. Today, Mr. Geller said, it is rebounding. "Window cleaning," he explained, "follows construction and the economy."
Most cleaners earn about $40,000 a year. Among the job's perks: bumping into someone famous, like a movie director who needs a window washer in his next film. "I was working at 22 Broadway when Oliver Stone saw me in the lobby and says, 'Hey, how long you been doing this?' " said Patrick DeFillippo, 45, of Glendale, Queens. "I was with them all day taping 'Wall Street.' In that scene where Michael Douglas is arguing with Charlie Sheen, he goes to the window and waves at me. I got paid good for it, too."
The typical window cleaner's day begins between 5 and 6 A.M., before the sun starts its ascent, and ends before it scorches their skin and drenches their drab blue uniforms in perspiration. From their perches, they watch the city yawn its way into the working day. Their tales are told matter-of-factly; the daily monotony strengthens the gut.
"In 1969 was I working on the 19th floor, and a hook came out of the window," Mr. Bubak recalled. "I swung off the window, hit my head against the building and was out cold. I gave it up for seven years." But, he said, the pay brought him back. "Sometimes I still get butterflies if I haven't been on the scaffold for a while, but it goes away."
Mr. Kind started washing windows with his father, Jacob, in 1952. His sons, Jason and Steve, have followed him into the union. A 14-karat squeegee hanging from a gold rope chain around his neck has three diamonds -- one for each generation of window cleaners in his family.
"It's quiet up here," said Jason Kind, 21, looking toward New Jersey from the roof of 888 Seventh Avenue. "All you hear is the wind and the air-conditioner motors. You get a rush; it's like bungee jumping."
His father and other veteran washers also speak of the rush. Some say modern safety measures have taken the thrill out of the job. "Years ago, it used to be that there was no limit on how high you could go on rope," Frank Kind said. "You climbed over the roof into the chair. Twenty-five, 40 floors. The good thing with rope is you knew you were going down and you knew you were coming back up."
Bill Bubak agrees, especially about the mandatory tether from the roof to a washer in a chair or on a scaffold. "You got this lifeline connected to you that smacks you in the face," he said. "It's a pain. Besides, once you get so high, it doesn't matter anymore. It's all the same if you fall."
 
ON THE JOB
Squeezing Squeegees in the Sky
Some facts about window-washer in the city:
Numbers 800, down from peak of 3,000 in 1916.
Salary $16.91/hour, plus $11/day for scaffold work. Average annual salary: $40,000-$42,000.
Training Six months on the job, supervised by veteran cleaner. Each cleaner must be certified by U.S. Occupational Safety and Health Administration for every type of scaffold used.
Equipment Extension ladder for jobs up to 30 feet; Rope-suspended chair for up to 75 feet; Electric scaffolds and nylon belts have no height restrictions.
(Source: Window Cleaners Local 2)

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: RED HOOK;
Deep Water And 2 Cranes Revive a Port

BYLINE: By MICHAEL COOPER 

 BODY:
The Brooklyn waterfront once hummed with activity as hundreds of longshoremen hauled freight each day from dozens of ships onto trains and trucks. But in the last 30 years, most of that business fled to newer, more spacious piers in New Jersey.
In the last year the city's Economic Development Corporation moved two refurbished, 120-foot cranes from the South Brooklyn Marine Terminal in Sunset Park to the struggling Red Hook Marine Terminal, the only port in the city where cargo containers are unloaded from ships. The agency also met with shipping companies to help Red Hook snare new contracts. 
 The efforts seem to be paying off. Sal F. Catucci, the chief executive of American Stevedoring, which began managing the Red Hook terminal in 1994, said it was handling almost twice as much freight as last year.
This year Red Hook has unloaded about 20,000 cargo containers from ships and 11,000 from barges, Mr. Catucci said. It expects to unload a total of 35,000 containers from 288 ships and 20,000 from barges. In 1994 it unloaded 18,000 containers from 228 ships and nearly 7,000 from barges. Mr. Catucci said he employed 125 to 300 longshoremen a day, up 10 percent from last year.
Development agency officials said that working in Brooklyn's favor is that Buttermilk Channel, which leads to the Red Hook piers, goes down 65 feet before it hits rock. The channel is about 40 feet deep now, but its sandy bottom could be dredged to accommodate larger ships. By comparison, Kill Van Kull, which leads into Newark Bay, hits rock at 40 feet.
"Ships are getting bigger and deeper," said Representative Jerrold L. Nadler, a Manhattan Democrat who leads a private group that is lobbying the Empire State Development Corporation to study how to develop the Brooklyn port. "When you drive along the Shore Parkway and see ships idling, they are waiting for high tide so they can go." The group has also asked Mayor Giuliani to halt waterfront development until a study can be completed.
For Mr. Catucci, such a study cannot come too quickly. "Brooklyn has the deep water," he said. "This is where the port should be."
 MICHAEL COOPER

LENGTH: 1020 words

HEADLINE: BEHIND THE WHEEL/Rolls-Royce Silver Spur;
Expensive Behemoth Is Surprisingly Nimble

BYLINE: By MARSHALL SCHUON 

 BODY:
I HAD done this before, I thought -- driven a car to the ooh-oohs of the crowd. I had preened at the wheel in parades, and I had driven in races, although the ahhs then were more like gasps as I demonstrated true ineptitude in the turns.
But this was no race. This was a v-e-r-y slow crawl through New York's Central Park, leading a $1.5 million line of fancy metal, and the appreciation from the multitude of skaters, joggers and muggers ranged from waves to stared befuddlement. One woman whistled. 
 The occasion was the introduction of Rolls-Royce's 1996 models, complete with police escort. There were Silver Spurs, Silver Dawns, Bentley Turbos -- eight cars forming a train of ostentation. A day-care class in green T-shirts pointed like a drill team.
I tried to look important and to forget that I somehow forgot to wear a tie.
Rolls-Royce has invested $75 million in the redesign and enhancement of its line over the last 18 months, and while that might be goobers to Ford or General Motors, it is a whopping sum for a company that is more a boutique than a mainstream player.
Rolls sold only 335 vehicles in the United States last year, but not to fret. With its cars costing as much as condos, the company remains profitable -- and with BMW's takeover of the Rover Group, Rolls is now, amazingly, the largest solely British-owned auto maker. Sales this year also show promise -- all 30 of the $319,000 Bentley Azure convertibles were spoken for before they were produced -- and 1995 could end with more than 400 new Roll-Royces and Bentleys whispering out of the company's 47 American showrooms.
All the same, the investment for '96 was needed, and the new models are smoother and more potent than their predecessors. A new intercooler for the turbocharged Bentley V8's and a new engine-management system has improved efficiency and (dare one say) economy.
For the first time, too, Rolls has actually announced a horsepower figure, admitting that the 6.75-liter turbocharged V8 produces 385 horses and an immense 553 foot-pounds of torque. The admission, supposedly to show that Rolls is a rejuvenated company, has not spread to the nonturbo cars, however, and the word there, as always, is that power is "adequate."
That coyness, incidentally, goes back to one of the marque's two founders, Sir Henry Royce, who is quoted thus: "My motorcars are designed to transport four people in supreme comfort at speeds up to two miles per minute. How that is achieved is utterly irrelevant."
These days, any Rolls or Bentley can top 120 miles an hour, and the best guess for the nonturbo's horsepower is about 300. Also, while the turbo will propel the car's megatonnage from 0 to 60 in less than six seconds, the more sedate sedans will accomplish the feat in just three seconds more.
The fact that there is no such thing as a slow Rolls, the journey through Central Park notwithstanding, has always been one of the hard things to keep in mind about the car. Rolls-Royces have for so long been equated with hushed civility that it takes the occasional spin to note once again that this is an automobile, a very real automobile, and not a living room.
The rear-opening hood rises on counterbalanced hinges, and there is the engine, sure enough. True, most of it is hidden under a cover of plastic composite, but finned aluminum valve covers do stick up, all polished silver and black, and there is no question that they are the upper ends of serious machinery.
For '96, the handmade grille is a bit lower, and the venerable "Spirit of Ecstasy" hood ornament has lost some height, shrinkage attributable not to age but to a search for aerodynamics, according to a Rolls spokesman. "As coachwork has become more streamlined, the Flying Lady has evolved," he said in rounded tones. "Some might say that she now maintains a slightly more discreet vigil over the famous radiator."
The black Silver Spur out in the driveway (you didn't think I left it in Central Park, did you?) comes with whitewalls, a vinyl roof, tasteful scads of chrome, and a sticker that says $176,500. For $22,500 less, you can get the Silver Dawn -- the same car but without such niceties as polished wooden picnic tables in the back seat and similar foofaraw.
The Dawn is a made-for-America-only model, and its intent is to lure buyers with a price that is closer to that of upper-level Mercedes-Benzes. But the Spur is -- forgive me -- the bread-and-butter Rolls. Its formal lines, huge length and interior amenities are as right for C.E.O.'s as for sultans.
New wheels and tires, wider and bigger, help the car's stance and road performance, which is surprisingly nimble. But it is inside that the car really shines. Mine has black leather everywhere, with bright red piping on the seats in an odd sort of frolicky decor, given its otherwise formal mien. What isn't leather or lamb's wool is mirror-finished wood, and even the new passenger air bag hides behind veneer.
It goes without saying that the interior cossets you in quiet solidity. The rear seats recline, with power adjustments, and the room back there is amazing. Surprising in a different way, however, is luggage capacity for the vehicle's occupants. The boot is shallow and holds a rather stingy 12.3 cubic feet of cargo -- but it does contain his-and-hers umbrellas, blue and white with Flying Ladies on the panels. Neat.
For the driver, there is a tilt wheel that automatically trundles up and out of the way when the door is opened or the ignition key is removed. The front seats are also lower to ease entry and exit, and their foam has been softened for more comfort. The audio system is both the best and worst to be found, with great sound and impossibly small buttons.
Fortunately, Rolls provides a remote control, but you probably shouldn't try to tune the radio while you drive. What you should do is enjoy the purr under the hood and the kick in the seat when you step on the gas. You can also have fun going slowly, of course. Just smile and wave. And look important.
 
INSIDE TRACK: Say you're rich? Well, this car is the acid test.

HEADLINE: The Nation;
The Budget Knife Boomerangs Home

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
FOR many people here, this has been one of the saddest and most anxious weeks of waiting in decades. The largest county government in America, is facing a potentially disastrous budget gap, and the Los Angeles County supervisors are deciding how to dismantle not just their social, health and other services, but their ideal of what a government should be.
 It appeared that nearly 40 walk-in clinics, spread mostly in poor neighborhoods, would disappear. More than 15,000 county workers would lose their jobs and many social and welfare programs probably would be slashed beyond recognition. 
 Meanwhile, Orange County, lying on Los Angeles' southern border, was facing the realities of its bankruptcy. After slashing its operating budget by 41 percent, the county had to eliminate free school bus rides, halt some testing for bacteria at popular beaches and sentence some criminals to perform routine maintenance at the courthouse.
 Without much local money to cushion the blow, Southern California is one of the first places to feel the tough love that the new leaders in Congress have promised to deliver to those who have been dependent on the government for survival, or just for services.

 
 
The Final Chapter Begins
 Many experts say this difficult exercise is a harbinger of what counties across the country are going to be experiencing under the restrictive new budgetary and social policies in state capitals and in Congress. But it is also the final chapter in the tax revolt that started in Southern California almost two decades ago with the passage of Proposition 13 and reached final victory when anti-tax Republicans captured Congress last year.
Until now, local tax rebels have been largely shielded from the consequences of their own revolt, because Washington and the state governments increased their contribution to schools and social programs as local revenues shrank. But now, with the tax rebels ensconced in Washington, there is nowhere else to turn.
 This is a pivotal moment. What is now dawning on Southern California will soon become evident to the rest of the nation.
 "We're just the first ones to get hit by all this, said Yvonne Braithwaite Burke, a Los Angeles County supervisor and head of the Inter-governmental Affairs Committee for the National Association of Counties. "But it's going to happen all over the country."
Some would argue that this reckoning has been put off for 17 years and that counties like Los Angeles and Orange should have prepared for it long before budget cutting fever reached Washington last November.
 In 1978, California passed what was known as Proposition 13, a rebellion that slashed property taxes and put a 2 percent cap on annual increases.
 But the county governments were able to dodge the deep cuts in services and the restructurings that the rebels had hoped for. California's economy was thriving throughout the 1980's and the flush state government made up most of what the counties had lost. In addition, the Federal Government stepped in and provided billions of dollars to cover many of the health and welfare programs that counties administered on behalf of Washington.
 Even Orange County did not have to come to terms right away with the consequences of its policies. Blocked from raising local taxes to finance the services residents clamored for, the county's treasurer generated more than $150 million a year in interest income by operating a risky investment fund. It was only last year that the financial house of cards collapsed (the fund lost $1.7 billion) and Orange County became the largest municipal government ever to file for bankruptcy.
 The collapse, as it turned out, happened at an inopportune moment. Now the state has its own fiscal crisis and is in no shape to bail out any of its counties. And the Federal Government has been cutting back some of its support, and it plans to cut even more. At long last, Prop 13, as it is known, is running its course.
 Not everyone is gloomy about this. Some of the tax rebels think it is all to the good, and they are not looking for help from the state or Federal governments. "Prop 13 was supposed to limit the size of county government, and it didn't do it," said Joel Fox, president of the Howard Jarvis Taxpayers Association, which led the fight for the measure. "I don't want to see the health care system collapse. But the county has just tried to patch things over. Now they are going to have to restructure."
 He added, "What Congress is doing will put even more pressure on the counties."
 Pressure indeed. The new Congress has promised that many of the big social programs that the Federal Government used to finance will now be the responsibility of the states. That has already happened to programs to reduce child abuse, to welfare and to the justice system. Increasingly, responsibility for more and more programs will be be passed down.
 In Washington, this trend is touted as a form of populist democracy, allowing the people who use the programs to be closer to those who manage them. But for many local officials, it looks an awful lot like an evasion of responsibility; they think they, not the members of Congress, will take the political heat for slashing services.

 
 
Pass the Hatchet
 "Congress talks about passing responsibility, but what they're really doing is passing the knife," said Robert Reischauer, the former head of the Congressional Budget Office.
Already, the knife has been handed down in Southern California, and the slashing has begun. "Much of this starts with the Congress," said Ms. Burke. "It then goes to the states, and then guess who it ends up with? The buck stops with us. We're the ones who have to close the hospitals." Sally R. Reed, Los Angeles County's chief administrative officer said, "Eyeball to eyeball, all this happens at the county level. We're the ones who listen to the parents crying or face the disabled kids whose programs have just been shut down."
 But the final chapter of the revolution that began with Proposition 13 has yet to be written. Perhaps the greatest question is whether residents of Los Angeles, or any other county, will feel that the quality of life will decline if they accept deep cuts in services. Some experts have predicted that as soon as the middle class suffers the consequences of closed libraries or a more inefficient justice system it will vote for a change of course. But California's experience suggests otherwise.

 
 
Future Shock
 For instance, while most services have survived the Proposition 13 shock, education in the state has undergone a slow but dramatic decline. In the mid-1960's California had the fifth-highest rate of spending per pupil in the country and an envied education system. Today it is 42d in spending; it has one of the highest school dropout rates in the country (only two states are worse), and last year its fourth graders tied for last place in an educational assessment test that was given in 39 states.
 Is anyone balking? Yes, but not about the decline in education. According to Ms. Reed, what people are saying is what they've been saying for 17 years: "We're not going to pay any more."

LENGTH: 1022 words

HEADLINE: Ideas & Trends;
A Rage for Merit, Whatever That Is

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
OF all the questions that bubble around the issue of affirmative action -- questions of fairness, justice, discrimination or quotas -- perhaps none is more nettlesome than that of merit. 
 As an ideal, it is as American as apple pie, and its celebration is what supposedly distinguished this country from the class-bound societies of Europe. It is the magic word in the campaign against affirmative action, invoked repeatedly, for example, as the regents of the University of California voted this month to abolish preferences accorded by race and sex.

 
 
The Beholder's Eyes
 But as competition for society's benefits grows more intense than ever and the economy undergoes radical change, the definition of merit is becoming more elusive. Is it, as some say, the only basis that the society should use in bestowing benefits and rewards such as jobs, promotions and admission to college? Or is it merely a Potemkin concept behind which the ruling class maintains its position? "Fondly we think we honor merit then," Alexander Pope wrote 250 years ago, "when we but praise ourselves in other men."
 Some people, like Prof. Lani Guinier of the University of Pennsylvania law school, hope the debate over affirmative action would provide the opportunity to delve into the question of merit -- and its kissing cousin, qualifications -- and whether the means used to assess them remain relevant.
 Liberal supporters of affirmative action, including President Clinton, often say they oppose the selection of "unqualified" over "qualified" people. That, of course, treats merit in absolute rather than relative terms. Conservative opponents of affirmative action routinely wave the flag of merit without defining the term; they would rely on mechanisms like standardized tests.
 "Standardized tests give you a relatively narrow picture of a person's abilities," said Alexandra Wigdor, director of the Division of Education, Labor and Human Performance of the National Academy of Science. "These do not measure motivation, perseverance or teamwork skills. There are just so many things that they don't measure that to consider merit to be fully explained by rank order on a standardized test is such a narrow definition of merit that it's absurd."
 Those who question the value of standardized tests posit this example. Suppose a high school student whose parents are college graduates scores 1,400 on the Scholastic Assessment Test, and the median score in his high school is 1,450. And then suppose a student from a low-income family whose parents did not finish high school scores 1,200 and the median score in his school is 800. Which would make the better college student? The one who scored higher or the one who overcame disadvantage?
 The issue is not an abstract one. To the California Board of Regents, judging by merit alone means using only scores on a standardized test, such as the S.A.T., and grade-point averages. Starting in 1997, 50 to 75 percent of those admitted to the University of California at Berkeley and the University of California at Los Angeles, the system's most prestigious schools, will be admitted strictly by those numbers. (Currently, 40 to 60 percent are admitted on that basis.) For the others, factors like socioeconomic background and unusual talents -- in music or sports, for example -- may also be considered.
 Under the new scheme, the regents estimate that the number of whites would probably remain the same and the number of Asian-American students would increase. The percentage of black and Latino students would fall.
 Yet the S.A.T. is little more than a predictor of how a student will do in the freshman year of college. That is certainly a limited measure, though better than no measure at all. Benjamin Wright, a professor of psychology and education at the University of Chicago, says the differences in test scores do not accurately measure the differences in performance, either. In addition, the S.A.T. has a range of error of at least 40 points, meaning that someone scoring 900 one day could take the test the next day and score anywhere from 860 to 940.
 "When people use these numbers to make decisions, they are naturally being misled," Mr. Wright said.
 The problem of using standardized tests to predict performance becomes even more acute in the employment context, some experts say. In a service economy, creativity and interpersonal skills are important -- but hard to measure. And in the highly contentious area of civil service exams for police and fire departments, such important traits as honesty and courage are left out.
 "Those kinds of private skills are totally missed in the merit argument," said James Heckman, professor of economic and public policy at the University of Chicago.

 
 
Compared to What?
 But if the means of measuring qualifications are less than perfect, the question has to be asked, compared to what? Scrapping "objective" gauges would mean relying more on the kinds of judgments that prompted the adoption of standardized measures -- and ostensibly fairer ones -- in the first place.
 For traditional civil rights advocates, the answer to the dilemma is affirmative action, using so-called objective measures to establish a floor of standards and then encouraging -- or forcing them if necessary -- schools and companies to select a proportion of women and members of ethnic minorities who meet the minimum qualifications. Others who oppose using race or sex as a criterion for selection hate this idea, but some -- though not many -- say they have an obligation to insure that adhering to the idea of merit does not confine minorities and women to second-class status.
 "Is it necessary to have two different kinds of standards because the playing field is not level?" said Clint Bolick of the Institute for Justice, who is an outspoken critic of affirmative action. "My answer is that we should always try to adhere a single set of standards that will apply to everyone. But our challenge is to find a ways of leveling the playing field, so that more people can meet those standards."

LENGTH: 976 words

HEADLINE: The World;
Trade's Bottom Line: Business Over Politics

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
EARLIER this month Henry A. Kissinger and a delegation of American business executives and academics paid a visit to Li Peng, China's Prime Minister. As would be expected at a moment when relations between the two countries has reached a low point -- on human rights, missile proliferation and Taiwan -- the opening minutes were spent with Mr. Li complaining about Taiwan's President visiting the United States, and with the Americans pressing the case of Harry Wu, the imprisoned human rights activist.
 What surprised the visitors, however, was how quickly Mr. Li dropped those issues and jumped right into business: Power projects that need foreign capital, China's plans for buying and building new passenger aircraft, the giant Three Gorges Dam project. "He made it very, very clear that he had no intention of letting these huge diplomatic disputes affect the economic relationship," said C. Fred Bergsten, a prominent economist and adviser to the Clinton Administration, who was along on the trip. "It was as if we were talking about two completely separate relationships." 
 These days America's economic stake in China's future is far too large to leave to the vicissitudes of diplomatic relations, especially when Japanese and German competitors are hardly about to pull out of the country every time the Chinese jail another 100 dissidents. Yet "commercial diplomacy" -- a term once enthusiastically used by some in the Administration to describe engaging the Chinese in economic development, the arena they are most focused on -- has so far done nothing to modify China's political behavior. Just the opposite: Mr. Li is betting that the United States is not willing to lose deals with McDonnell Douglas and Ford in the name of protecting human rights.
 It is a taste of things to come, and one of the central dilemmas facing Secretary of State Warren Christopher next week as he tours Southeast Asia and ends up in Vietnam, the newest site of American efforts to use business as its main diplomatic vehicle.

 
 
The Best Promotion
 At its most basic level, commercial diplomacy has clearly been a success for the Administration. For the first time in this century, the Government has regularly put the promotion of business interests at the top of its foreign policy agenda. Even the Republicans who want to eliminate the Commerce Department were congratulating Secretary of Commerce Ron Brown last week for his department's advocacy of those interests in the big emerging markets from Brazil to Poland. And Mr. Brown seemed to win converts when he argued that this is no time for "unilateral disarmament" in trade.
 But when Mr. Clinton decided last year to remove the link between China's human rights record and the renewal of trade benefits, and when he extended diplomatic recognition to Vietnam two weeks ago, he suggested that economic engagement had a deeper purpose: It would be the best way to change the nature of authoritarian regimes. So far, that strategy has failed to work in China. In a widely published essay last week Mr. Kissinger himself concluded that the two countries are on "a collision course."
 Commercial diplomacy works best with small countries reaching for America's markets and capital. That is why Hanoi has been more cooperative than Beijing. Even the North Koreans have begun to make concessions regarding their nuclear program in hopes that the ultimate reward is Western investment. But China is a special case, with the luxury of many suitors. Even while assuring Americans that trade and politics are separate, China's first response in the dispute over American relations with Taiwan was to sign a deal with Mercedes-Benz.
 Jeffrey Garten, the Undersecretary of Commerce for International Trade, is among the many officials who say that while there is little political leverage in the commercial ties to China now, the power equation will change. "Over the next decade, our links could easily expand geometrically." he said. "By that time a new generation will be leading China, and their interests in economic prosperity for China are likely to rank much higher in terms of China's international priority. It may be a gamble, but it is the best shot we have."
 Even with allies, though, the Administration is increasingly recognizing that there is a fundamental complication when relations are based on business rather than security. In Southeast Asia, the relationship with America was once a matter of life and death. Today Asian countries say they want America's continued military presence primarily because it keeps shipping lanes open.
 Commercial alliances run by very different rules, in which political allies are also fierce competitors. So the question -- today in China, tomorrow elsewhere -- is whether and when America is willing to swallow its devotion to human rights or concerns over nuclear proliferation to keep Mitsubishi from closing a deal.
 "Everyone acknowledges that economics now plays a central role in foreign policy -- that battle is over," one of Mr. Clinton's top economic advisers said the other day. "What no one has really grappled with is what happens when commercial concerns push out other interests."
 It will be interesting to see whether Mr. Christopher, who has rarely gone beyond the platitudes of the new era, is ready to grapple openly with such tradeoffs. During a speech on Asian policy on Friday, someone asked him if he was concerned that Japan's banking crisis could mushroom into a worldwide financial crisis -- an issue far closer to the hearts of the Asian leaders he meets next week than the issue of how to contain the Serbs in Bosnia.
 "Where are you, Bob Rubin, when I need you?" he asked, referring to the Treasury Secretary. Then, the true diplomat, he said he hoped the Japanese would solve their own problems.

SECTION: Section 4; Page 5; Column 1; Week in Review Desk 

LENGTH: 1250 words

HEADLINE: The World;
In Japan, Chicken Little Lays the Golden Egg

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF 

DATELINE: TOKYO

 BODY:
" . . . and then in the fall of 1995, to cap an atrocious year for Japan, Mount Fuji erupted, covering Tokyo with lava and triggering a giant earthquake that sent the country's major islands sinking noislessly beneath the sea. . . ."
WELL, that hasn't happened yet. But it would be a fitting follow-up in an Annus Horribilis that is deflating Japan in its own eyes and those of the world. 
 It wasn't so long ago that the West looked upon Japan as the Great Success Story of the latter part of the 20th century -- and, perhaps, of the 21st century as well. Its economy appeared unstoppable, it was buying up golf courses and Old Masters around the world, and its youngsters were intimidating because they seemed more interested in calculus than in controlled substances.
 Those were the days when Japanese restaurants served meals sprinkled with gold dust, and the Imperial Palace grounds in Tokyo were said to be worth more than all of California. The Japanese ran the world's biggest banks and bought assets like Rockefeller Center at astronomical prices; if it was comforting that they could be fleeced, it was also scary that being fleeced didn't seem to matter to them.
 These days, though, the Japanese are feeling anything but scary; their own self-image is more like pathetic and vulnerable. It is not just the great earthquake that toppled earthquake-proof bridges and killed 6,055 people in January. Nor is it just the addition of the nerve gas attack on the Tokyo subway system.

 
 
The Blue Period
 Those episodes have been the highlights of Japan's Blue Period, but the day-to-day texture is more about economic woes. Partly because of the high yen, unemployment is at a record, and plenty of 50-year-olds fear that after a lifetime of sacrifice, they face "kubi kiri" -- or beheading, as layoffs are sardonically called here.
 "While it is only with the utmost caution that a political leader dares make statements that might heighten anxiety among citizens, I frankly believe we face very serious crises," warned Morihiro Hosokawa, the former prime minister. He added: "in this critical situation, Japan is like a ship on the verge of sinking."
 A commentator in the Yomiuri Shimbun, Japan's largest-circulation newspaper, warned that "the public vaguely shares a sense of 'doomsday.' "
 So which is the real Japan? The colossus? Or the basket case?
 There is evidence for both propositions, but experience suggests that foreigners should be wary of the hand-wringing. Japan has always been a nation of self flagellators, at a national and personal level. Japanese women have to be warned when living abroad not to complain constantly to neighbors about how awful their own husbands are, as it is only polite to do back home. Abroad, neighbors will take their complaints seriously and suggesting dumping the bum.
 One of Japan's strengths, in fact, may be its bleak outlook. The last time that Japan was this worked up, for example, was the early 1970's, after the oil shock. Japanese feared that this was the end of their boom, and even toilet paper disappeared from shelves as people stockpiled for an uncertain future. But in retrospect the gnashing of teeth seemed ridiculous, and Japan emerged stronger than ever. Instead of putting Japan out of business, the oil shock put Toyotas in carports across America.
 Will Japan be able to bounce back this time?
 "Japan in one or two years will of course bounce back," said Yukio Okamoto, an influential consultant. "But we will never again come back to the years of really strong economic growth."
 One reason is simply that as economies grow, the pace tends to slow. Japan's postwar boom, when annual economic growth rates sometimes approached 10 percent, was an anomaly. If future growth is in the neighborhood of 2 or 3 percent per year, that is still fine by the standards of industrialized countries.
 But Japan's neighboring developing countries, from China to Malaysia, are still in the boom phase. This means that Japan's share of the global economy is likely to diminish, and this is already happening: Japan's exports accounted for 11.1 percent of the world total in 1985, and now account for about 9.7 percent. For the same reasons, the American share has tumbled hugely over the last half century.
 A more fundamental long-term challenge is demographic: Japan is aging very fast. In the year 2000, for example, Japan will have one-quarter fewer youngsters aged 10 through 19 than it did in 1990. The number of people over 60 will have increased by 35 percent.
 This kind of aging tends to slow an economy down in part because the proportion of retirees increases. But some economists say the more important problem is simply that older people are less innovative and entrepreneurial than younger ones. So as Japan ages, it can expect fewer risk-takers like the ones who in the late 1940's founded Sony and Honda.
 While some foreigners are as gloomy as the Japanese, anticipating such possibilities as a banking collapse, many other experts also point to Japanese long-term strengths. They suggest that Japan will be hugely important for generations to come -- and could gain greater influence than ever.
 "Japan still has many prominent factors working in its favor," said Robert Alan Feldman, an economist at Salomon Brothers in Tokyo. "It has a labor force that is still very well educated, hard working and works up to extremely high standards. It has a social system that still works extremely well, with a crime rate that is very, very low."
 Another of Japan's advantages is location, location, location. It may be embarrassing for recession-bound Japan that its neighbors are booming along at growth rates of 7 percent or more per year, but it is also a major opportunity. Japan's experience, connections and investments in Asian countries allow it to share in some of their growth, and could help it become the focal point of the most dynamic continent in the world.
 Japan could also gain influence simply by being more hospitable to foreigners and their products. Opening up Japanese markets to Asian exports would mean a big boost, and welcoming foreign university students and faculty could make Japan an educational center for all Asia. Japan would also hugely expand its importance if it were to gain a permanent seat on the United Nations Security Council, as it hopes to do; even if that doesn't happen, it could simply choose to play a more active role in international diplomacy.
 One gauge of long-term potential is how much countries invest in plants and equipment or in areas such as health and education. Not surprisingly, as a nation of Eeyores, the Japanese have been socking away money to tide them over difficult times. The net household savings rate in Japan last year was 16.6 percent; in the United States it was 4.1 percent. Laboratories throughout Japan still hum away with research-and-development spending, though less than before.
 "The fact that many Japanese are worried, many opinion leaders are coming out and saying we've got to change course" is a reason for optimism, said Richard Koo, an American economist who is often critical of Japan from his perch at the Nomura Research Institute. The public worry in Japan is leading to widespread talk about the need for deregulation, which could be the fuel for a tremendous new wave of growth.
 "These could push Japan to a new height," Mr. Koo said. "We don't need to write off Japan."
