HEADLINE: The World;
The South American Art of Name-Calling

BYLINE: By CALVIN SIMS 

DATELINE: BUENOS AIRES

 BODY:
LATIN America isn't, as it is often portrayed in the United States, just one civilization artificially divided into different countries. Listen to the chants at soccer stadiums around South America, and you'll hear what Latin Americans really think about each other.
 At the recent America Cup tournament in Uruguay, Argentine fans belittled their Chilean counterparts as "sons of Pinochet" (translation: Chile is full of militarists). The Argentines, who are predominantly white, also urged their team on against the "dirty blacks from Brazil" (the country with the largest black population on the continent). 
 Meanwhile, in a match against Bolivia, Chilean fans yelled that no country with a standard of living equal to Uganda could beat them.
 And finally, when Uruguay took the championship here, thousands of Uruguayans took to the streets to celebrate their victory not by mocking Brazil, the closest contender, but by shouting their joy at having humiliated the continent's most prideful nation, Argentina. Gleefully, they cursed the "portenos" of Buenos Aires -- a moniker that connotes hopeless arrogance.
 Stereotypes are alive and well in Latin America, and they are not just limited to the soccer stands.
 While other societies have been cultivating taboos on crude references to ethnic origin or race or sex, Latin culture is still often surprisingly straightforward about these things.
 Latin Americans say this makes them more direct and honest than, for example, North Americans, who even while struggling to make their own society speak more tolerantly still tend to lump all Latin Americans in one stereotypical mass.
 On the other hand, it also means that Latin Americans accept stereotypes -- of their own compatriots as well as of other Latin Americans -- that would make most North Americans cringe. In doing so, they build elaborate pecking orders in their minds (usually with one's own country at the top, of course).
 Bolivians, for example, think of themselves (and are thought of by many other Latin Americans) as a kind-hearted nation. But kind-heartedness isn't what applies when many Bolivians think about Chileans, their neighbors to the southwest.
 A 10-year-old Bolivian boy returned from school recently announcing that he wanted to kill all Chileans. Why, he was asked. "They stole our coastline in the Pacific War of 1869 and left us with nothing," the child explained.
 Many Chileans, who have very distinctive accents, complain of being treated poorly in stores and restaurants in Bolivia. Bolivians view Chile as an expansionist country not only militarily but also economically and culturally -- a notion that was confirmed in many a Bolivian mind when Chile recently hired away the Bolivian soccer coach who had taken Bolivia to the World Cup competition last year.
 Latin Americans are also very frank in their dealings with others on a personal level. A fat person is openly called "gordito" or fatty, and people of dark skin color are called "negrito" or blacky ("ito" usually shows affection). Such references would be unthinkable in the United States, but they are not meant or taken as disparaging.
 In such an atmosphere, a stereotype that might otherwise be thought of as nasty can even be taken as a compliment, if the target wants to bury the sting. Argentines, for example, often revel in being referred to as haughty (the trick is to think enough of yourself that arrogance seems justified). Likewise many Chileans, when they are called militaristic, can summon up their best feelings about men in uniform by remembering the prosperity and order that Gen. Augusto Pinochet brought, along with repression and terror.
 Without a doubt, Argentines, who are mainly of European descent, are the most universally loathed group in South America. "Argentines are hated because they are arrogant," said Luigi Manzetti, a senior researcher at the North-South Center at the University of Miami. "They see themselves as an outpost of European civilization, and they think the rest of Latin America envies them because their population doesn't include Indians and mestizos."
 A favorite pastime of many South Americans is trading Argentine jokes. Here are two very popular ones:
 In the middle of a violent lightning storm, an Argentine goes outdoors and stands in the rain. "What are you doing out here?" he is asked. "I came out because God wants to take my photograph."
 Question: What is the most effective weapon against an Argentine? Answer: A needle, which can be used to deflate him.

 
 
Equal-Opportunity Disdain
 Argentine disdain for anything different is not limited to other nationalities and is often expressed within the country for the lower classes and people of mixed blood. For example, during Argentine league soccer games, fans who are rooting against the country's most popular team, Boca Juniors, like to sing that Boca fans are "all dirty Bolivians and Paraguayans who never bathe." The reference is particularly pointed these days because many Argentines resent a recent influx of peasant immigrants from those countries.
 In Argentina's neighbor, Chile, Peruvians and Bolivians are viewed as less than equal, because they are mainly mestizo (of mixed ancestry) and indigenous, rather than European, in ancestry. This is expressed by Chileans as a stereotype that the Peruvians and Bolivians are all backwoods peasants with no sophistication -- a stereotype that rankles the Andean people.
 Hernan Godoy, a sociologist and former dean of the faculty of social sciences at Catholic University in Santiago, thinks he knows why the Chileans feel that way; typically, it has something to do with Argentina and pride and pecking orders.
 "We Chileans feel that Argentines are whiter, blonder and more secure," he said. "But on the other hand, the Chileans feel blonder and whiter than Peru and Bolivia."

HEADLINE: July 23-29;
The Wobbly Ideal Called Perfection

BYLINE: By JAMES BARRON 

 BODY:
THE French novelist Emile Zola once declared that he was "little concerned with perfection." American high school students suddenly have the luxury of feeling the same way: it is now possible to miss three or four questions on the Scholastic Aptitude Test and still achieve a perfect score. 
 The College Board, which administers the all-important admissions test, has recalibrated its scoring system so the average student would once again get scores of 500 on both the math and verbal sections. The average verbal score had fallen to 424, the average math score to 478. The change redefined perfection downward a notch or two. "We don't believe what others believe, that an 800 should only go to a student who has a perfect raw score," said Bradley J. Quin, the director of admissions and enrollment services at the College Board. "We don't define perfect that way."

 
 
By Any Other Name
How to define perfect in an imperfect world? (And it is an imperfect world: it bulges around the middle, instead of being perfectly round.)
 Hollywood has had a lot to say about perfection: "Leonard Maltin's Movie and Video Guide" lists 21 films with the word "perfect" in the title, from the Clint Eastwood escaped-convict thriller, "A Perfect World," to the David Niven-Loretta Young romance, "The Perfect Marriage." In a 1985 film titled simply "Perfect," John Travolta played a reporter. Everybody knows reporters are perfect.
 Sure.
 But don't ask an economist what constitutes the perfect rate of economic growth. The Administration and the Federal Reserve seem to have decided that a 2.5 percent is perfect, although that's not all that brisk. The trouble is, if the economy starts chugging much faster than that, the result would be a situation that even a less-than-perfect student of economics could recognize as less than perfect: inflation.
 Employment statistics? Perfectly confusing. Full employment -- a job for every worker -- might strike a layman as not only perfect but an improvement on Henry IV of France, who said he wanted there to be no peasants in his realm so poor they did not have a chicken in their pot on Sunday. Economists, though, are not laymen. This may or may not be why full employment does not mean zero unemployment. Instead, full employment is considered to exist when unemployment is in the 6 percent range.
 What's perfection at the Post Office? Never mind rain, sleet and gloom of night: take a look at on-time performance. Nationally, 85 percent of first-class mail arrives perfectly on schedule. But the island of Manhattan is a black hole of imperfection. Its comparable figure drops to 76 percent, meaning that one in four letters arrives late by Postal Service standards. That adds up: Manhattan postal workers have to redeliver 400,000 to 500,000 letters every day because they did not get to their perfect destination the first time around.
 Milton wrote about Chaos and Night moving "in perfect phalanx." How perfect were the phalanxes of bombers in the Persian Gulf war? "Smart bombs" were 90 percent accurate but made up only 7 percent of all of the American explosives dropped on Iraq and Kuwait. The 81,980 tons of unguided bombs had an accuracy rating of only about 25 percent. That is, 62,137 tons missed their targets.
 Perfect pitch? Musicians who have it may be far from perfect. "There was a violist at Juilliard who had it when I was a student," said the composer Lowell Lieberman. "You could press all the keys with your forearms and she could tell you which keys were not sounding. But she was the worst musician in the school. She played totally out of tune." (What pitch is the perfect one for the A above middle C? The standard used to be 440 cycles per second, but original-instrument aficionados prefer 415, while some symphony orchestras seek a bit more brilliance at 444.)
 The perfect Madison Avenue pitch? Earlier in the year, the trade publication Advertising Age chose the greatest commercial ever made: the spectacular spot by Chiat/Day that evoked the George Orwell novel "1984" to introduce the Apple Macintosh computer during Super Bowl XVIII 11 years ago. It ran only that one time.
 The perfect baseball pitch? Some fans say it was the Yankees' Don Larsen's game-ending throw in the 1956 World Series, a called third strike against Dale Mitchell of the Brooklyn Dodgers in the first (and so far, the only) no-hitter in World Series history.

LENGTH: 1682 words

HEADLINE: D'Amato Wields the Gavel, Without the Scrapper's Style

BYLINE: By FRANCIS X. CLINES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 27

 BODY:
As he perches in his special seat of power as chairman of the Whitewater hearings, there is an uncharacteristic quietude about Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato that even he himself can find strange.
"Being able to be a free spirit, a free operator -- that's a luxury of the minority that I don't have anymore," said the New York Republican, hardly ruing his return to majority power, but conceding that his street instinct for political cut-and-thrust has been crimped. 
 "But I listen to them all -- listen, listen," he said. "Give people time, and if they're cooking things up it will come out," he said of the hearings' current question: whether the White House, on a damage-control mission, rummaged through the office of Vincent W. Foster Jr., the deputy counsel, the night he committed suicide two years ago.
Last year, Senator D'Amato raged with partisan certainty about that question when he was on the attack in the minority. But, wielding the majority gavel now, he leaves it to other Republicans on the committee to attempt roundhouse innuendo. He modulates the proceedings evenly, ruling out the more far-fetched political queries for the moment as the hearings proceed with slow certainty into the Presidential election cycle.
"I relish it," conceded the hyperkinetic, 57-year-old politician who runs the inquiry with a soft-spoken gravity that seems an almost amusing relief from the essential D'Amato political dynamic perfected in a career of upset victories: the scrapper up from steerage, the nobody with no place in the halls of power -- except for his knack of seizing the initiative in jungle-fast thrusts that even his peers find breathtaking.
"He doesn't take no for an answer, and he's got a very long memory," said a fellow Senator, speaking in cool summary of Mr. D'Amato's formidable fund-raising powers, his true cutting edge within Washington's politics.
The D'Amato image that he works so hard to keep before the public -- the gauche, lippy, brazen populist -- is hardly a full picture here, where his many strands of power are gathered easily in his restless, far-reaching hands.
As the hearings proceed with some bipartisan praise for their fairness thus far, nervous witnesses at the testimony table look across at a politician at the top of his game.
Even while running the Whitewater inquiry under a near-unanimous Senate mandate, Mr. D'Amato makes time to be a strategizing and fund-raising principal in the campaign of his friend, Bob Dole, the Senate majority leader, who hopes to topple President Clinton next year. Mr. D'Amato is very busy shaping this national power broker's role, one that would dwarf his mastery last year in the election of his chosen candidate, George E. Pataki, as Governor of New York.
At the same time, Mr. D'Amato is running one of politics' most important mother lodes of power and money, the National Republican Senatorial Committee. Then, too, he is chairman of the Senate Banking Committee, historically one of the main generators of legislative and fund-raising power under either party's control.
"To me, this could be the culmination of all the political endeavors I've ever undertaken," said Mr. D'Amato, envisioning a Dole victory as he sat back casually this week after a long day of Whitewater, his mind's eye on a dozen current endeavors: his new sass-and-tell autobiography, which burnishes his scrapper's image; banking bill arcana that leave critics suspicious; his soaring love life; Whitewater's intriguing political rapids, and his growing role in national politics.
In this, he early on used his New York party machine as the front-runner's wedge for the Dole campaign. Lately, he has been warning the candidate against a repeat of the prime-time ideological rabidness of the 1992 Republican National Convention. Even more, he cautions Mr. Dole of Mr. Clinton's extraordinary prowess as a candidate, if not as a President.
"Ronald Reagan was the second greatest politician I've ever seen," Mr. D'Amato said. "Clinton's the best. The best. Great persona. Incredible in person. They hit him with everything last time. Here he is, in the thick of the battle."
Mr. D'Amato was clearly paying homage as a fellow besmirched scrapper who has rebounded from the canvas. "Take him for granted, we pay the price," the Senator said as Mr. Dole's national steering committee chairman, making the Whitewater hearings seem even more intriguing to discuss.
But first he sang a bit into the phone when a critical call came through. "Miss Claudy! Hi, Sweetness!" Mr. D'Amato warbled in response to the anchor-desk felicities of Claudia Cohen, the wealthy television newswoman featured in the senatorial news conference he called solely to confirm that he was in love again.
"Learning is a wonderful experience," he said, updating the romance for a visitor and describing his visits to Ms. Cohen's splendid house in the Hamptons as if in a National Geographic narrative: he, a humble denizen of the Nassau County boardwalk and poker games at the Island Park Knights of Columbus, venturing far eastward to that moneyed sleekness and finding "insight into some things I generally don't deal with."
Mr. D'Amato, endlessly visceral, stood in delight at the sudden arrival into his office of Arthur J. Finkelstein. He is one of the most reclusive political consultants in the business, gifted at honing the D'Amato image in New York and branching out more now with the Senator into the lucrative national arena. They embraced like brothers. The Senator autographed his new book, "Power, Pasta and Politics: The World According to Senator Al D'Amato," for the visitor, and Mr. Finkelstein glided out with a smile, assured that "that other thing will be taken care of."
Of heartfelt hugs and dialogue is the mesh of Senator D'Amato's days. Early Monday morning, he strolled along Broadway in Manhattan in an arm-in-arm chat with another political lieutenant, Charles A. Gargano, a legendary D'Amato fund-raiser who is now in charge of economic development for the Pataki administration. The Senator had made a Wall Street speech to a gathering of 200 world bankers, finding them too drowzy and suddenly asking how fast a 10 percent growth rate would double the national cost of Medicare.
"C'mon, this is a test, wake up!" he gibed at the silent sea of gray suits. "This is a tough one, huh? I'm boring you?" he added. "To hell with it; I'll sit down," he needled with his best beyond-the-boroughs nasality from Nassau County. He let them off the hook by announcing the answer and instructing the bankers to buy 10 copies each of his new book.
His bemused confidence was understandable. In truth, the intersection of big money and power politics is a signature nexus for Mr. D'Amato, one that has produced steady controversy but undeniable power across 25 years.
His earliest days as an avowedly innocent underling in the Nassau County Republicans' fabled "tithing" patronage machine have been followed by tens of millions in senatorial fund-raising success, some of it criticized as lobby fleecing.
The Democratic sneering that such a man, called "the vacuum cleaner" for his prowess as a fund-raiser, should be looking for ethical violations in Whitewater is rooted in his own various vettings by news organizations, prosecutorial investigators and the Senate itself.
"Even though the ethics charges were thrown out with a small admonition -- the guts of them were thrown out -- the thing lives with you," said the Senator, speaking of the investigation by the Senate Select Committee on Ethics in 1991 of influence-peddling charges in the office of Senator D'Amato. He was eventually criticized by the committee for being "negligent" in letting his office and his letterheads be used by his brother, Armand, a military contract consultant, in an "improper and inappropriate manner."
This was followed by the ultimate exoneration for him, re-election. From that experience, the Senator maintains, comes a sense of empathy, not revenge, as he guides the Whitewater hearing and faces nervous, sometimes tearful witnesses with their reputations on the line.
"I have a much more acute awareness, given what I came through," he said. "Destroying someone by feeding unfair allegations -- I don't need that on my conscience. Let me tell you, families grieve."
"Become a pariah and see what happens," he advised a visitor with an owlish glare dark with memories. "A person can be turned into an outcast."
Even so, the Democrats are hardly counting on the tender mercies of Mr. D'Amato as the hearings' conclusions come out sometime during the 1996 election campaign. Concerned that the Republican majority would engineer a partisan setup, the minority, led by Senator Paul S. Sarbanes of Maryland, focused early on the critical pre-hearing procedures that produce the public testimony.
"Senator D'Amato did a good job setting it up -- he handled it fairly," conceded Senator Christopher J. Dodd of Connecticut, general chairman of the Democratic National Committee, who has been warning other Democrats against making hyperbolic complaints about the hearings thus far.
"He's not put on his partisan hat," added Mr. Dodd, a Whitewater committee member who is mindful of Mr. D'Amato's talent for suddenly seizing most political forums with a jagged-edged sound bite.
In "Power, Pasta and Politics," published by Hyperion, Mr. D'Amato notes: "History books are filled with great men who never had the nerve to act fast enough or decisively enough." He translated this dictum into his 1992 re-election drive when he leaped on his opponent, Robert Abrams, the state's Attorney General, with an expensive and overwhelming television blitz of bare-knuckled attack ads the day after the Democrat barely survived a blistering primary fight, broke and battered.
"I had him and had to stay on top of him, just had to stay there," Mr. D'Amato said with a simple nod at the elementary nature of it all. "Politics? I don't know about politics -- this is about life," he said, preparing for another day of Whitewater.

HEADLINE: U.N. Watches Romania Violate Sanctions

BYLINE: By JANE PERLEZ 

DATELINE: TIMISOARA, Romania, July 25

 BODY:
Three times daily, a blue-and-white Yugoslav Airlines 727 passenger jet, its seats usually empty and carrying only a few crew members, pulls up at the out-of-the-way airport terminal here.
For the brief flight back to Belgrade, each 727 is pumped with much more fuel than it needs -- enough to fill three MIG fighter planes or three turbo-attack helicopters, according to State Department estimates. 
 United Nations monitors watch and record the tens of thousands of liters of fuel being loaded from a Romanian state oil truck. The monitors say a friendly hello to the Yugoslav pilot, and after he takes off, they hand official reprimands to the Romanian fuel truck driver and the local customs inspectors.
These actions have been going on for months and represent one of the most flagrant violations of international sanctions against the Belgrade Government, American and European sanctions-monitoring officials said. Last week, the officials said, the Romanian authorities allowed the number of daily flights to increase from two to three.
"Timisoara is a very blatant violation of sanctions," said Antonio Napolitano, the sanctions coordinator in Brussels for the European Union and the Organization of Security and Cooperation for Europe. "With the increase of flights, it becomes quite an export of oil."
Each 727 leaves here with about 22,000 liters of fuel for a 25-minute flight to Belgrade that requires about 5,000 liters, records of the United Nations monitors show.
Under international law, it is up to the country where the sanctions are being broken -- in this case Romania -- to shut down the violations. But despite repeated appeals to the Romanian Foreign Ministry to stop the operation, it has increased, said Casimir J. Krul, an American customs official who heads the sanctions-assistance mission in Romania.
The Romanian official at the Foreign Ministry responsible for sanctions enforcement, Dumitru Tanasa, said he did not believe the accounts of the United Nations monitors at Timisoara. Mr. Tanasa said in a telephone interview that he had informed the Romanian Department of Transportation to allocate the Yugoslav planes only enough fuel for the flight to Belgrade.
"Every day we are in contact with the representative of the Department of Transportation, and they are informing us the instructions are observed," Mr. Tanasa said.
In May, the United Nations Security Council passed a resolution to close a loophole that the Belgrade Government had exploited after an easing of sanctions last fall. In exchange for agreeing to seal its border with Bosnia, Belgrade was allowed to resume commercial flights of Yugoslav Airlines.
After the easing, the airline immediately began to fly virtually empty 727's to Timisoara and fill them up. The May resolution said the Yugoslav flights could carry only sufficient fuel for the return flight and a little extra for an emergency situation. But after the Security Council action, the flights increased to two a day and then, beginning last week, started coming in three times a day.
A sanctions-monitoring team of 19 people, including 5 American customs officers, has issued 210 violation notices at the Timisoara airport, Mr. Krul said. Sanctions officers boarded the jets, took the names of the pilots and recently noted that the maintenance crewmember was wearing military identification.
American officials said that unlike many other sanctions violations in the Balkans region, where private individuals and companies are engaging in the smuggling while governments look the other way, the violations taking place here are being directed by senior Government officials. Three Romanian state authorities, the American officials said, are involved in the fueling: the state oil company, known as Romarc, the customs administration and the aviation agency.
The stepped-up operation at the airport here comes as the Romanians show that they are unable, despite some efforts at law enforcement, to stamp out the smuggling of oil from the Romanian side of the Danube River 120 miles south of here to the Serbian side. Fuel, which is coming from scores of new gas stations recently built to serve the smugglers, is also crossing by land.
A United Nations monitoring team estimated that 2,500 tons of fuel in tankers crossed the checkpoint at Naidas on Monday. On a stretch of road running 15 miles on either side of the fishing village of Pescari, where the fumes of gasoline are overpowering, United Nations monitors estimate that there are more gas stations than in all of Bucharest.
The fuel from the gas stations is pumped into jerrycans and then taken across the river in boats by hundreds of smugglers who are paid 50 German marks (about $35) each by the Serbs, the monitors said. Despite a raid by the Romanian customs authorities last month, the river smuggling seemed to be back to normal and had even increased, monitors said.

HEADLINE: CLINTON AND DOLE BIDDING TO BREAK WELFARE IMPASSE

BYLINE: By ROBERT PEAR 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
President Clinton and Bob Dole, the Senate Republican leader, will offer competing proposals on Monday to overcome a political impasse that has stalled welfare legislation for two months.
The two men are scheduled to outline their proposals in speeches to the National Governors' Association, meeting in Burlington, Vt. Gov. Tommy G. Thompson of Wisconsin, a Republican who is the group's incoming chairman, said he would work to line up support for Mr. Dole's plan. 
 Administration officials said today that Mr. Clinton, in an effort to recapture the initiative on welfare, would announce steps to encourage states to move more of their welfare recipients into jobs. At present, fewer than 10 percent of the five million adults on welfare are employed.
Mr. Clinton will offer states incentives to meet an ambitious new goal of putting large numbers of welfare recipients into jobs or programs that prepare them for work. States could choose a variety of strategies, including "workfare" or subsidies to private employers to hire welfare recipients.
A senior Administration official said Mr. Clinton would also give states more flexibility in running their welfare programs, a priority for governors from both parties as well as for Congressional Republicans.
Senator Dole, the front-running candidate for the Republican Presidential nomination, has for several weeks been struggling to broker a compromise on welfare legislation. Bills approved by the House of Representatives and by the Senate Finance Committee are, in general, much tougher than Mr. Clinton's proposals and would set more stringent work requirements.
Mr. Dole's proposal builds on the measure approved in May by the Senate Finance Committee. A bloc of 24 conservative Republicans led by Senator Phil Gramm, the Texan who is one of Mr. Dole's chief rivals for the party's Presidential nomination, rebelled against that measure, forcing Mr. Dole to move to the right in his search for a Republican consensus. Aides to Mr. Dole said his plan would place him in the middle, between a conservative bill drafted by Senator Gramm and a liberal alternative to be offered by Senate Democrats.
Mr. Dole's proposal, like the bill passed by the House in March, would give each state a lump sum of Federal money, known as a block grant, to assist poor people. State officials would have far more discretion than they now have in deciding how to use the money. But poor people would no longer have a legal right, or entitlement, to assistance, and the Federal Government would put a cap on total spending, regardless of economic need.
As they gathered in Vermont, governors of both parties said they favored welfare legislation that would leave states with the most money available and the fewest restrictions on how to use it.
Mr. Thompson said he continued to be concerned that some Republicans would load up any welfare bill with "conservative prescriptions," like work requirements or denials of cash to unwed mothers. Each of those mandates he said, just like liberal mandates of the past, "limits our flexibility, our ability to get the job done."
Senate Republicans have made progress in resolving one issue that was blocking action on welfare legislation: a dispute over how to divide up the Federal money that would be turned over to states in the form of block grants. Senators from Sun Belt states had objected to the formula used in the House bill and in the Finance Committee bill, saying it made no allowance for population growth.
Senator Kay Bailey Hutchison, Republican of Texas, said the Republicans had agreed on a formula to compensate high-growth states with a pool of money totaling $860 million over five years.
"I believe it will be acceptable to everyone concerned," she said in an interview. And Governor Thompson said today that Senator Dole had assured him that under the new fiscal formula no state would get less Federal money than it now received and that there would be increases for high-growth states like Florida and Texas.
Gov. George E. Pataki of New York, a Republican, said Mr. Dole's plan was "clearly a major, major improvement" over other proposals because it would not reduce the amount of money going to states like New York. The population of New York State, around 18 million, has not increased significantly since 1970.
President Clinton, a former Governor of Arkansas, has often expressed support for governors seeking more flexibility to devise and run social welfare programs. In the President's speech on Monday, Administration officials said, he will propose expanding the authority of the Secretary of Health and Human Services to approve state welfare experiments.
Administration officials said they hoped Mr. Clinton's proposals would enable him to regain control of an issue that he made a campaign priority in 1992, only to see Republicans claim it as their own after last year's elections.
An Administration official said Mr. Clinton's speech to the governors would convey this theme: "I want welfare reform. I thought we were making progress, but now things have slowed down in the Senate. In the meantime, while Congress is snarled, I will take action." The official said Mr. Clinton's action would move states in the direction of "work-based welfare reforms."
On welfare, more perhaps than on any other issue, Mr. Dole's instincts as a deal maker in the back rooms of Congress conflict with the ideological purity demanded by the conservative wing of the Republican Party.
"This may be the first philosophical battleground in the race for the Republican Presidential nomination," said Gov. Stephen Merrill of New Hampshire, which is tentatively scheduled to hold the first 1996 primary on Feb. 20.
Governor Merrill, a Republican who has not endorsed any candidate for President, said Mr. Dole was riding high in the polls in New Hampshire because he had effectively "articulated Republican policies in contrast to those of Bill Clinton." But Mr. Merrill said the welfare issue might work to the advantage of Senator Gramm.
"This gives Gramm a specific issue on which he can contrast himself with Bob Dole," the Governor said.
The view of many conservative Republicans is expressed by Robert Rector, a senior policy analyst at the Heritage Foundation, who helped draft the welfare bill passed by the House in March. "Dole's lack of leadership on welfare reform, particularly on the issue of illegitimacy, has been very disappointing," he said.
Conservative groups like the Christian Coalition, the Family Research Council and Empower America have endorsed Mr. Gramm's bill.
Mr. Dole says his efforts to negotiate a compromise show his strengths as a pragmatic lawmaker. "It's not easy to put together a welfare reform package," he told the Republican National Committee two weeks ago. "It's easy to put together if you don't care how many votes that you get. But to get one that will pass is not easy."
The bill passed in March by the House would give states control of $15.4 billion a year in Federal welfare money. It would deny aid to unmarried mothers under the age of 18, most legal aliens and additional children born to families already receiving welfare. It would also authorize financial bonuses for states that reduce out-of-wedlock births.
Mr. Gramm supports these proposals; Mr. Packwood says the Federal Government should not impose its will, but should let states decide.
Senator Dole is considering several proposals to discourage out-of-wedlock births. One proposal, described in confidential documents prepared by the Senator's staff, would "require teen mothers to live at home and attend school" and would "expressly give states the option to deny benefits." Mr. Dole is also considering a proposal to "mandate that states deny benefits to teen mothers," but to let states "pass legislation to opt out" of this requirement.
William B. Lacy, deputy chairman of Mr. Dole's campaign, emphasized the differences between Mr. Dole and Mr. Clinton, rather than the disagreements among Republicans. "Clinton promised in the 1992 campaign to end welfare as we know it, but he has not made it happen," Mr. Lacy said. "He hasn't lifted a finger. His inaction shows a lack of leadership."
Democrats say Mr. Clinton deserves credit for putting welfare on the national political agenda. Rahm I. Emanuel, a White House aide who worked on Mr. Clinton's 1992 campaign, said: "Senate Republicans are in disarray because of three forces: geography, ideology and Presidential politics. Senator Dole is bobbing and weaving among those forces."
By contrast, he said, "the President is already taking action by encouraging and approving state experiments that move people from welfare to work."
Efforts to negotiate a compromise in the Senate are hampered by the fact that one of the main negotiators, Senator Bob Packwood, is preoccupied with his own political welfare. The Senate Select Committee on Ethics has found "substantial credible evidence" to support accusations that he made unwanted sexual advances to women. Mr. Packwood, an Oregon Republican who is chief sponsor of the Finance Committee bill, is fighting the charges.
Mr. Dole recently threatened to keep the Senate in session through August to pass a welfare bill, but it is unclear whether he intends to carry out such a threat.
If the Senate does not pass a welfare bill before the recess, Republicans said, they will try to include its main provisions in an omnibus bill to reduce the Federal budget deficit. Senate rules allow only 20 hours of debate on the budget bill, and that bill will be a vehicle for many other Republican initiatives, including tax cuts and efforts to slow the growth of Medicare and Medicaid.

HEADLINE: Mexican Connection Grows As Cocaine Supplier to U.S.

SERIES: The New Drug Lords: First of two articles.

BYLINE: By TIM GOLDEN 

DATELINE: CIUDAD JUAREZ, Mexico

 BODY:
The bodies of police informants still turn up around this sprawling border city, their mouths sometimes stuffed with one of the fingers they might have pointed at drug traffickers.
Mostly, though, the stronghold of the man thought to be Mexico's most powerful cocaine smuggler, Amado Carillo Fuentes, is almost peaceful compared with other drug capitals. Gun battles are rare, car bombings unheard of. The police chief cannot remember the last time armed thugs took over one of the discotheques for a party. 
 That quiet, drug enforcement officials say, is the sound of business booming.
As Mexico's political and economic ties to the United States have strengthened in recent years, American demand for illegal drugs has helped a new generation of Mexican traffickers to consolidate their power, carving out an ever larger share of the world's drug trade and posing a growing threat on both sides of the border.
That the violence among major traffickers has begun to fade may only portend greater efficiency, Mexican and American law enforcement officials say. With once-rival gangs working together more, Mexican traffickers have expanded their markets, diversified their products and cut better deals with their powerful Colombian suppliers.
Mexicans who once earned cash for transporting the Colombians' cocaine north now frequently take their payment in kind, officials say. They earn as much as half of any large load, distributing it across a network that has expanded to include most of the western United States, parts of the South, much of Chicago and pockets of New York.
The Mexican traffickers' share of the profits has risen sharply, and officials say their sophistication, contacts and corrupting influence have grown as well.
"If we don't do something, both in the southern United States and in Mexico, Mexico will take over from Colombia in a few years as the traffickers' headquarters of choice," the United States Ambassador to Mexico, James R. Jones, said. "It will undermine democracy. It will undermine commercial development. It will undermine free trade."
American officials who once trumpeted Mexican cooperation in fighting drugs now worry that the Government has lost control of most of its police. When the authorities located a leading cocaine trafficker last month after his rented Learjet crashed as he flew to a wedding in Guadalajara, they needed army troops to capture him. The city's federal police commander and most of his deputies were on the trafficker's payroll, officials said.
President Ernesto Zedillo, like his predecessor, Carlos Salinas de Gortari, has made a point of calling the drug trade "the most serious threat to national security" that Mexico faces.
He has promised to crack down as hard as the Government can, but many Mexican and United States officials question whether the Government is prepared to confront all the obstacles.
While American officials lavishly praised Mexico's cooperation in fighting drugs under Mr. Salinas, growing evidence indicates that protection for the traffickers reached high into his Administration. Those directly implicated in taking bribes include former federal police commanders and two of the administration's three drug enforcement directors.
American officials say huge amounts of drug money have flowed into Mexico's tourism, transportation and construction industries, helping to fuel the speculative rise of the economy until last year. Without offering details, a senior F.B.I. official, James Moody, asserted recently that many of the state-owned companies privatized under Mr. Salinas had been bought by traffickers.
Some supporters of the Zedillo Government say the central question it faces is whether, in the midst of economic and political turmoil, it can afford the instability that a serious challenge to the drug mobs might entail. Another question is whether Mexico has grown addicted to the money it earns from drugs, now estimated by the United States Drug Enforcement Administration at more than $7 billion a year.
"They could hit the traffickers any time they wanted to," said one former D.E.A. official with long experience in Mexico. "But who is going to knock off the goose that lays the golden eggs?"
 
The Violence
Killing Each Other, Not the Politicians
 The bursts of violence that have attended the traffickers' rise have led many Mexicans to fear that their country is sliding toward the sort of terror that the Medellin cocaine cartel unleashed on Colombia during the late 1980's and early 1990's.
During the last three years, the victims of drug-related shootings have included the Roman Catholic Cardinal of Guadalajara, a crusading police chief of Tijuana, two former state prosecutors and more than a dozen active and retired federal police officials.
Anti-drug officials say the infighting among lower-level gangs is likely to remain endemic, especially along the border. Some officials are also braced for new violence in the United States as Mexican cocaine wholesalers strengthen their presence in distribution territory once dominated by Colombians.
At the same time, many analysts believe that the threat of more politically significant violence by the traffickers in Mexico is mostly latent.
Senior Mexican officials say they have never found hard evidence to support rumors that drug gangs were involved in the assassination last year of the governing party's presidential candidate, Luis Donaldo Colosio.
And since jailing Mr. Salinas's elder brother, Raul, on charges of ordering the murder last fall of the party's deputy leader, Jose Francisco Ruiz Massieu, the authorities have backed away from the idea that drugs might have played a role in that slaying.
Mexican drug gangs have generally avoided the bombings, murders and other terrorism with which the Medellin cartel, in particular, sought to intimidate the Colombian Government. Given the wide availability of police protection, Mexican traffickers have generally opted to pay.
The cost of public prominence has tended to be higher. The notoriety and ruthlessness of Juan Garcia Abrego, the leading trafficker along the northeastern border and the Gulf coast, has lost him millions of dollars in seized assets, and a half-dozen of his lieutenants have been arrested. Since March he has been on the F.B.I.'s most-wanted list.
Intelligence officials say the more recent Colombian influences on Mexican traffickers have not been the flashy, homicidal men from Medellin, but the cooler and more businesslike, if only marginally less violent, leaders of the Cali cartel. That has been especially apparent, they add, in the ascent of the powerful trafficker from Ciudad Juarez, Amado Carrillo Fuentes.
 
 
The Kingpin
A Career Built On Discretion
 Like Mr. Garcia Abrego, Mr. Carrillo, 39, is a nephew of one of the older traffickers who led Mexican marijuana and heroin smugglers into the cocaine age as the United States cracked down on smuggling routes through the Caribbean. Their uncle, Ernesto Fonseca Carrillo, was imprisoned in 1985 for the murder of an American drug agent, Enrique Camarena Salazar.
One American intelligence summary describes Mr. Carrillo as a stocky, brown-haired six-footer, reared in the sierras of the western state of Sinaloa and apprenticed in the early 1980's to Pablo Acosta, a cocaine trafficker based in Chihuahua. Mr. Carrillo took over the organization after Mr. Acosta was killed by the police in 1987.
Two sets of Mr. Carrillo's partners were arrested in 1991, and Mr. Carrillo himself was captured by the police in 1989, only to be freed for "insufficient evidence." His chief rival in Juarez, a flamboyant former state security commander, was gunned down in April 1993. That November, Mr. Carrillo narrowly escaped from gunmen apparently sent by Mr. Garcia Abrego to kill him at a seafood restaurant in Mexico City.
"He has been very lucky," said an American law enforcement official. "It has helped that a lot of people in Colombia trust him, that he has kept a low profile and a cool head and that he has a lot of contacts in all aspects of the drug trade."
American officials say Mr. Carrillo established strong ties to one of the most powerful leaders of the Cali cartel, Miguel Rodriguez Orejuela.
Like those in Cali, Mr. Carrillo's trafficking operation is said to be a model of criminal security, well managed and highly compartmentalized. Drug enforcement officials say he talks over the same secure telephones used by his Colombian partners; his underlings avoid surveillance by pirating the frequencies of cellular telephones across the border and by changing beepers as often as once a day.
Perhaps more impressive has been Mr. Carrillo's ability to broker deals among groups that are often called cartels but have acted more like competitors. By syndicating large drug shipments and assigning different smuggling tasks to different partners, intelligence analysts say, he has begun to achieve economies of scale that earlier traffickers mostly dreamed about.
The wide belief among American officials in Mr. Carrillo's pre-eminence is based not only on the amount of drugs he ships but also on the contacts and Government protection that allow him to operate throughout Mexico and abroad. Intelligence analysts rank him as one of five or six patrones, or bosses, of the Mexican trade.
American officials said Mr. Carrillo had been linked to most of the seven huge loads of cocaine that were flown into Mexico from May 1994 to March 1995 on converted Boeing 727 and Caravelle jets.
Like the Tijuana-based trafficking organization run by the brothers Benjamin and Ramon Arellano Felix, he is also thought to be responsible for container ships that have brought tons of cocaine into Mexican ports.
 
The Operation
A Retailing Secret: Learn to Diversify
 Following the lead of Mr. Garcia Abrego, Mr. Carrillo and other Mexican traffickers have also begun to demand more of their payment in kind, a move that could shift the balance of power within the global cocaine trade.
Though the Colombians appear to have freely allowed the Mexicans a wider distribution role as a means of reducing their own risks and maximizing profits, the compact remains something of a riddle to drug intelligence analysts because it seems to cede so much potential power to the Mexicans.
The officials say Mr. Garcia Abrego, for example, usually earned no more than $2,000 a kilo for the cocaine picked up from Colombian pilots in Mexico and delivered to Colombian distributors in Queens or the Bronx. But where a one-ton load might then have earned him $2 million in transportation fees, a half share of the same load would be worth about $10 million on the wholesale market in New York.
In some cases, the Mexicans are simply buying entire loads of cocaine from the Colombians and selling it themselves.
Compared with the Colombians, officials at the Drug Enforcement Administration and Mexican prosecutors say, the Mexican distributors are unsophisticated, even sloppy. Their wholesale networks are looser and more independent, built on those they used to market heroin and marijuana.
But by most accounts, the Mexicans are learning quickly.
"If history is any predictor," the D.E.A. administrator, Thomas A. Constantine, said in an interview, "I think the Mexican groups will eventually force the Colombians out of narcotics trafficking and control it themselves."
While continuing to transship about 70 percent of the cocaine coming into the United States, Mexican trafficking groups have also expanded product lines as the American cocaine market has begun to decline.
In addition to the marijuana and black-tar heroin that Mexico has produced for decades, smugglers have moved into higher quality white heroin from Colombia and Southeast Asia, officials say. In the last few months, agents in Mexico have seized Laotian opium, Nigerian heroin and 13 tons of hashish from Pakistan, by far the largest seizure of the drug ever in Latin America.
In barely two years, Mr. Constantine said, Mexican traffickers have also virtually taken over the growing and highly profitable American market for methamphetamine, or speed, displacing motorcycle gangs like the Hell's Angels. The wholesale price of speed has dropped from $10,000 a pound to $2,000, cutting into the crack cocaine market in parts of the southwest and fueling new spurts of gang activity and violence.
"A pound used to be a big seizure," said the special agent in charge of the D.E.A. office in Phoenix, Richard Canas. "Now we are seeing 400 pounds."
 
 
At the Border
Trade Pact Helps All Entrepreneurs
 Mexican traffickers also seem to have embraced a vision of North American integration not unlike that with which Nafta, the North American Free Trade Agreement, was sold to skeptics in Washington.
Law enforcement officials say more and more drug cargoes are moving through Mexico into the United States as part of the widening flow of legal commerce between the two countries. Tons of marijuana and cocaine have been seized on ships and trucks coming into Mexico, and tons more on trains, tank trucks and tractor-trailers passing through Mexico as traffickers buy into trucking firms, fishing fleets and other transportation companies.
And though Mexican officials deny that traffickers have taken advantage of the country's privatization program, the State Department recently cited reports that some have been buying bank stocks and seeking election to bank boards to ease the laundering of their profits. Some traffickers have even sought trade consultants to determine what merchandise moves most quickly across the border under Nafta regulations.
"They have very specific issues," said Craig Chretien, the special agent in charge of the D.E.A.'s San Diego office. "Does a perishable get through quicker than a load of steel? What kinds of cargoes go through faster than others?"
Clinton Administration officials insist that the 19-month-old trade agreement has not quickened the flow of drugs through Mexico. But United States Customs Service officials acknowledge that the smugglers are moving more of their drugs into the United States through legal ports of entry, taking advantage of rising truck traffic and a falling rate of inspections.
The traffickers have also apparently realized that American agents are not immune to the temptations to which some of their Mexican counterparts have long succumbed.
In May, two customs inspectors in El Paso were arrested on charges of conspiring with smugglers to allow more than a ton of cocaine across the border in return for a $1 million payoff. That same month, the F.B.I. began an investigation into charges by a customs supervisor in San Diego that as many as eight customs officials may have aided Mexican smugglers there.
A senior American anti-drug official described the two cases as "the tip of an iceberg."
 
 
The Corruption
Pervasive, Yes, But Not Dominant
 Reports of high-level drug corruption in the Salinas Government have helped popularize the notion that Mexico is becoming what critics call a "narcodemocracy" -- a polity in which traffickers have a disproportionate share of the votes.
But without playing down the growing danger that the traffickers pose, some analysts say the threat to political institutions remains more potential than present.
Though the earnings of Mexican traffickers are already thought to approach those of the Colombian cartels, the Mexicans' influence is diffused in a more complex political universe and an economy roughly six times as large.
Nor is there much sign that Mexican kingpins have developed the broad political agendas that have led their Colombian counterparts to form parties, finance presidential campaigns and even run for office.
It may be that Mexican traffickers have simply not yet had much to worry about: the considerable opening of Mexican politics in the last decade has seemed hardly to perturb them, and the corruption cases that have come to light suggest that the traffickers' bribes are well-aimed and rarely turned down.
"All you have to do," noted the Ciudad Juarez chief of police, Jose Luis Holguin, "is not look."
NEXT: Why Washington treated Mexico as a special case.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Mexican drug traffickers, who have traditionally dealt in marijuana and heroin, have expanded their markets, making deals to distribute Colombian cocaine. Many American and Mexican officials have questioned whether the Government's efforts to crack down on traffickers have been adequate. Soldiers and police uprooted and burned marijuana plants in Michoacan. (Phillippe Diederich/Contact Press Images, for The New York Times) (pg. 6)
 
Map of Mexico showing location of Ciudad Juarez. (pg. 6)
 
Map/Diagram: "The Flow of Drugs Through Mexico" shows routes of drug distribution through Mexico.
 
Chart: "A CLOSER LOOK: The Bosses"
 Analysts at the Drug Enforcement Administration compared the loose structure of Mexican drug trafficking to a federation rather than a series of cartels. In this view, the stature of the patrones or bosses, comes not from controlling territory so much as from the international scope of their contacts and their ability to operate across Mexico with Government protection. About a dozen lower-ranking "division heads," many of them up-and-coming young traffickers, may join different bosses for different deals. Division heads, in turn, contract with smuggling groups at different points along the border to move their shipments into the United States.
 
Amado Carrillo Fuentes: At 39, considered Mexico's most powerful trafficker, organizing huge shipments by air, land and sea, and operating throughout Mexico from such bases as Ciudad Juarez, Hermosillo, Mexico City, Cuernavaca and Cancun. Indicted in the United States on heroin and marijuana charges in 1988 and on cocaine charges in 1993, he faces only a minor gun charge in Mexico.
 
Juan Garcia Abrego: A 50-year-old native of La Paloma, Tex., he dominates drug trafficking in northeastern Mexico and along the Gulf-coast corridor. The main smuggler of Colombian cocaine going to Houston, Dallas and New York, he pioneered deals in which Mexican traffickers take payment in kind, raising their profits sharply. Put on the F.B.I.'s most-wanted list in March, there is a $2 million reward for his capture.
 
Miguel Angel Felix Gallardo: A 48-year-old former Sinaloa state policeman, he rose to become the "godfather" of Mexican cocaine trafficking until his arrest in April 1989 in connection with the murder of an American drug agent four years earlier. His nephews, Benjamin and Ramon Arellano Felix, moved part of his organization to Tijuana, establishing themselves as the strongest traffickers on the Pacific coast. Since being jailed, Felix Gallardo has continued to trade on his singular contacts, brokering deals around the country.
 
Juan Jose Esparragosa Moreno: A 46-year-old former associate of F'elix Gallardo's nicknamed El Azul, or "Blue," he is the least known of the country's major traffickers but has risen quickly back to prominence since completing a six-year prison sentence in 1991. Once bases mainly in Guadalajara, he is said to have moved north, working with Carrillo Fuentes and Hector Luis Palma.
 
Joaquin Guzman: A former Felix Gallardo lieutenant, Guzman, 40, broke away after the boss's arrest, allying himself with Palma. Known for his intelligence and 5-foot-6-inch -- he is called Chapo, or "Shortie" -- he became one of Mexico's more innovative smugglers, building tunnels beneath the border at Douglas, Ariz., and San Ysidro, Calif. Officials say Guzman was the target of an attack by the Arellano Felix brothers in which the Catholic Cardinal of Guadalajara was killed in May 1993, and he was captured a month later.

HEADLINE: After Three Months on the Job, France's President Exudes Elan

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: PARIS, July 29

 BODY:
In barely three months as President of France, Jacques Chirac has cultivated a macho image that is the envy of far younger leaders, like President Clinton.
After the French leader made a dramatic call for American and British support for stronger military action against Bosnian Serb attacks on United Nations "safe areas," one Administration aide in Washington said, "Some people in the White House are absolutely convinced that Chirac is grandstanding to make Clinton look bad." 
 Officially, Mr. Clinton, who will soon be 49, and Mr. Chirac, who is 62, are said to be on the best of terms -- "Jacques" and "Bill."
At the staff level, though, there is irritation in Washington along with grudging admiration for the legerdemain that has given Mr. Chirac a reputation as the most dynamic leader in the Western world before he has really had much chance to prove that he deserves it.
His moves have been decisive but brusque, like the man himself. In June, he outraged leaders in the Pacific, from Australia to Japan, by announcing a resumption of underground French nuclear testing in the Pacific this September. And he annoyed Germany, Belgium, Luxembourg and Spain by refusing to dismantle French border controls with them until he was satisfied that they would keep drug smugglers, illegal immigrants and terrorists from crossing into France.
Police checks of foreigners' papers were stepped up nationwide after a bomb exploded in a Paris commuter train last Tuesday and killed seven people.
Nevertheless, Mr. Chirac, elected with 52.6 percent of the vote after a campaign focused almost exclusively on promises to get the country's unemployment rate below 12 percent, has watched his poll ratings sink while unemployment has stayed stubbornly high.
In a nationwide telephone poll conducted by IFOP, a polling institute, in mid-July, only 44 percent of those polled said they were satisfied by Mr. Chirac's performance in office, compared with 59 percent in May.
Six feet tall and athletically trim, he favors double-breasted suits but takes to more informal American-style loafers and sports jackets when working political crowds, reportedly on the advice of his daughter and communications adviser, Claude Chirac.
She also has apparently told him to go slow on granting one-on-one television appearances or press interviews, for he has granted no such requests since May.
He has held two news conferences since his election, both of them following the White House format of the President standing behind a lectern and calling on questioners. That is a sharp change from the tradition begun by Charles de Gaulle of the President regally pontificating from behind an ornate desk.
Despite his tough policy statements, he has also brought an easygoing American style to the working habits of the Elysee Palace, the Paris equivalent of the White House, dropping in informally on aides to see how they are doing or picking up the phone rather than working from the formal memos favored by his predecessor, Francois Mitterrand. "Enjoy your siesta," he jokes to aides who work 14-hour days.
Visitors find him far more accessible than previous French Presidents. When Richard C. Holbrooke, the American Assistant Secretary of State for European Affairs, came to Paris to discuss Bosnia policy early this month, Mr. Chirac agreed on short notice to come over to an aide's office to talk with him. The President just came in and pulled up a chair, according to another American with Mr. Holbrooke.
Mr. Chirac apparently acquired this style during a summer in Cambridge, Mass., in 1953, when he and two classmates from the Institut des Sciences Politiques attended summer courses at Harvard College. He also got a job as a dishwasher at the local Howard Johnson's restaurant, but was promoted to be a waiter upstairs after three days.
Though French regional delicacies, and not Howard Johnson's fare, were served on the lawn of the Elysee at his first July 14 national day reception this year, Mr. Chirac invited 4,000 young people in blue jeans and scouts' uniforms and turned it from a garden party into something more like an American picnic.
Many of the young people had worked on his campaign, but the usual guests from the Paris elite of politicians, business leaders and well-connected journalists found themselves unexpectedly without invitations.
Mr. Chirac has tried to send other signals that he means to change things. Immediately after the election, he said that all members of the Government would give up one of the most cherished perquisites of political power in Paris -- the right to race through the streets to and from work in limousines with sirens, flashing lights, and police motorcycle escorts.
From now on, he ordered, Government drivers must stop at red lights and obey speed limits. Officials would travel on commercial aircraft; a VIP fleet would be dismantled and its planes returned to the French Air Force.
The President retained access only to two small Falcon 900 executive jets, and he used one of those for a tour of French-speaking Africa this month.
Mr. Chirac's many critics here believe, though, that it is far too early in his seven-year term to judge Mr. Chirac as a statesman. Some believe that his activity on foreign affairs is mostly a diversion from the intractable problems of unemployment, which he himself has said is the Government's first priority. He has taken steps to encourage employers to hire the long-term unemployed and entry-level workers.
In Bosnia, he has repeatedly signaled that he will not be satisfied with the status quo, threatening to withdraw the French peacekeepers unless the United Nations is given the means to be more effective.
When hundreds of peacekeepers in Bosnia were taken hostage by the Bosnian Serbs at the end of May in retaliation for NATO bombing raids on military installations in the Bosnian Serb stronghold of Pale, Mr. Chirac was reported to be furious -- at the peacekeepers, for submitting too meekly.
Aides say that he upbraided Adm. Jacques Lanxade, the chief of the French military general staff, as if he had been personally responsible.
Admiral Lanxade offered to resign, and last Tuesday French officials announced that he would be replaced by Gen. Christian Quesnot, an advocate of firmer military action in Bosnia.
Mr. Chirac persuaded Britain to join France in sending 10,000 additional troops to back up the peacekeepers, and he also pressed for American logistical support to move them around in helicopters.
What he finally got instead, after the Bosnian Serbs began overrunning Muslim enclaves in eastern Bosnia, was the promise of intensified American and NATO air strikes to deter further attacks.
But the French diplomacy in NATO and the United Nations was acknowledged to have been shrewd. As one American military officer pointed out, it is a French general in Zagreb, Croatia -- Gen. Bernard Janvier, the commander of the United Nations Protection Force for the former Yugoslavia -- who now has the ultimate authority to begin the bombing.
Mr. Chirac met with Prime Minister John Major of Britain today and got an agreement to try to restart the stalled peace talks for Bosnia.
Mr. Chirac's reputation for decisive derring-do is now so firmly established that when loud detonations were heard near Pale on July 23, people around the world were prepared to believe, despite Mr. Chirac's fervent denials, that he had ordered a bold French bombing raid.
At first, aides say, he was amused, but after the Paris commuter train bombing, he concluded that the Pale rumor might be seen as a provocation for terrorist actions. On Thursday, the French General Staff issued a formal denial that there had been a French bombing raid on Pale.

HEADLINE: Communism in Laos: Poverty and a Thriving Elite

BYLINE: By HENRY KAMM 

DATELINE: VIENTIANE, Laos, July 25

 BODY:
As Laos, a vital but virtually forgotten battleground of the Vietnam War, prepares to celebrate the 20th anniversary of its Communist "people's democracy," it remains a place of harsh inequalities.
It is a country of 4.5 million people where a small class of entrepreneurs and members of the Communist establishment has attained a level of prosperity reminiscent of the privileged life of those who benefited from French colonialism and the years of American dominance that ended with the Communist victory. 
 "The social injustices, the growing differentiation among the few and the many are enormous," said the Ambassador of a formerly Communist country.
After the Lao People's Democratic Republic was proclaimed in December 1975, the Government practiced an austere brand of Communism, with low economic activity. But in the late 1980's, following Vietnam's lead, it recognized a need to make up for faltering Soviet assistance by gradually opening up to foreign investment and appealing for broader international assistance.
The subsequent influx of businessmen, particularly from neighboring Thailand, led to many joint ventures, which found their Laotian partners among the existing elite, Communist and pre-Communist.
More of the former privileged classes survived in Laos than in Vietnam, where repression was more ruthless, and in Cambodia, where most of the old elite were killed during the Pol Pot regime. With the economic liberalization in this country, overseas Laotians, mostly in the United States and France, along with their relatives here, began playing an important role in business.
"Our hearts have always stayed here, and now it's also possible to do business," a frequent long-term visitor from France said.
But for the poor, there seems to be little such optimism in a country that is classified by the United Nations as a "least developed" nation.
"Only 20 percent of the national budget goes to the social sector, and the same is true for the aid of the donor Governments," said the head of a major United Nations agency, speaking on the condition of anonymity because of its presence here. "They have accepted this imbalance. The gap is widening. They have done nothing for the villages."
About 85 percent of the population is estimated to be peasant farmers, who enter the money economy only marginally. They live from what they grow, augmented by barter. Estimates of per capita annual income, ranging from $250 to $335 cited by international organizations, are based on Government statistics in which the organizations have little faith.
None of this appears to raise questions among the top leadership of this single-party state. The party forswore its ideology of creating a classless society when it began the "new economic mechanism" in 1986, but it continues to swear by the tenets of Marxism-Leninism.
When asked whether his party had changed its goals, President Nouhak Phoumsavan cited a need "to readjust" to "the reality of the situation."
"The aspirations and noble ideals of the Lao people of all ethnic groups are to lead toward development of a prosperous country and create a life of affluence, well-being and justice in society," the President said when asked whether Communism remained the eventual aim.
Mr. Nouhak Phoumsavan is a revolutionary hard-liner believed to be in his 80's and one of the few surviving leaders of the generation of disciples of Ho Chi Minh, founder of Communism in Vietnam, Cambodia and Laos. While reading prepared answers to questions submitted in advance, the President gave no indication how his Government intended to realize its goals.
There was however, some apparent criticism of the Government's policies from Sisana Sisane, who at 73 is the most outspoken of the early Communist leaders.
"We were a little dogmatic in the past and made a mistaken analysis," he said. "Laos is not yet ready for Communism or capitalism. First we must reach the point where we can opt for one or the other. We must be a little realistic. The subsistence economy is the obstacle."
Despite an annual economic growth rate of 6 to 7 percent since 1990, most of it concentrated in the capital, this progress is in part negated by a population growth rate of 3 percent and a gross domestic product of about $1 billion. Foreign assistance financed 44 percent of the 1994 budget, 40 percent of which was devoted to the public payroll.
From 1990 until the end of 1994 the Government succeeded in cutting rampant inflation, limiting it to single-digit rates for the last three years.
The country's principal exports are logs, hydroelectric power and garments and motorcycles made here from imported materials, because of the cheap labor in Laos.
Romeo A. Reyes, senior economist of the United Nations Development Program, prescribed "more fiscal discipline," more tax collection, less use of the money-printing press and cuts in the public payroll. But in the United Nations Human Development Index, Laos remains near the bottom. It ranks 133d out of 173 countries. Life expectancy is steady at 50 years. Of 1,000 children born this year, 182 are expected to die before the age of 5; the infant mortality rate is at least 125 out of 1,000. The United Nations Children's Fund reports that only one pregnant woman of 20 sees a health worker, and most births are unattended.
"There isn't really any functioning health-care system," said the head of the United Nations agency. "There are hospitals, but there isn't really any reason to go to them."
Visits to the hospitals of Xiengkhouang and Luang Namtha Provinces showed why. They are rundown, unsanitary places virtually empty of functioning equipment and medicines and receive electricity two or three hours an evening.
"Yes, from time to time people die here because we don't have what they need," said Dr. Sivay Vong Thongchit, deputy hospital director in Xiengkhouang.
Gov. Kuan Keomany of Luang Namtha, a mountainous province on the Chinese border, said he was satisfied with the achievements of socialism. He said modern diagnostic equipment in the military hospital in Vientiane had discovered he was suffering from a lung ailment, and he was cured.
He is a member of the ruling elite of the Lao People's Revolutionary Party, with access to the best. He acknowledged that at his provincial hospital not even the simple X-ray machine was working. Few in his remote province have ever been far from their villages.
A survey by the United Nations Food and Agricultural Organization last year concluded that 47.3 percent of children under 5 were "stunted" from chronic malnutrition and 10.5 percent "wasted" from acute malnutrition. The results showed no change from a 1983 survey. Fewer than half the rural population have access to safe drinking water.
"But I think poverty is even more serious," said Jan Mattsson, resident coordinator of the large United Nations community. "The statistics are very poor."
Underdevelopment is equally unchanging in education, according to international officials. Estimates of the illiteracy rate range from 50 to 80 percent.
The United Nations reports 70 percent enrollment in primary grades in 1993-94, with a dropout rate of 60 percent. Less than three years of schooling is the mean, and most teachers have had less than five years of education. A result of this is what some Laotians and most international officials, aid workers and foreign businessmen call "the human-resource problem" and consider the principal constraint on hopes for development.
The refugee exodus after the Communist takeover deprived Laos of about one-tenth of its population, including a majority of its small educated elite. The vacuum has not been filled and puts heavy strain on the few officials with whom the large number of foreign donors and businessmen must deal.
Deputy Foreign Minister Souban Srithirath said in an interview, for instance, that Laos did not aspire to join the Association of Southeast Asian Nations, because it did not have enough qualified officials to take part in its work.
But despite the evident abandonment of Communist objectives, the governing party does not provide any indication of willingness to dilute its monopoly on power, which is anchored in the Constitution.
"When most people don't know how to read and write, how can you have democracy?" the French-educated Mr. Souban Srithirath said.

HEADLINE: Asian Nations Putting Pressure on France Over Nuclear Tests

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON 

DATELINE: BANGKOK, Thailand, July 29

 BODY:
With France only weeks away from resuming nuclear tests in the Pacific, governments across Asia and the South Pacific are demanding that the French reconsider, and there are warnings of an economic boycott that could damage the French economy.
The most potent threat may come from Japan, where the Government has bitterly criticized the decision by President Jacques Chirac to resume nuclear testing in French Polynesia this fall after a three-year moratorium. Mr. Chirac says his decision is irrevocable. 
 Last week 47 Japanese lawmakers, many of them prominent members of parties in the coalition Government, called for a boycott of French luxury goods, a threat that carries weight given the affection of millions of Japanese consumers for brand-name French fashion, perfumes and liquor.
The Japanese market accounts for as much as half of the profits for some French makers of luxury goods, and shares of several of those companies have been tumbling in the French stock markets as a result of the protests in Japan.
"Nations that possess nuclear weapons must show their wisdom and set an example to countries that do not have nuclear weapons," the Japanese Science and Technology Minister, Makiko Tanaka, said in a letter to Mr. Chirac. Prime Minister Tomiichi Murayama has accused France of "betraying" nonnuclear countries with the resumption of nuclear tests.
Mr. Chirac announced in June, shortly after his election, that France would carry out eight underground explosions in two tiny Polynesian atolls -- Mururoa and Fangatauta -- from September through May. After that, he has promised, France will sign the Comprehensive Nuclear Test Ban Treaty and end nuclear testing forever.
The French Government has said it needs to carry out the tests to check the reliability and safety of its existing nuclear arsenal. But that has not satisfied foreign leaders and environmental campaigners who say computer simulations would offer much the same information.
There is debate among scientists about the environmental impact of the tests, with French geologists insisting that none of the radiation from the test sites can leak from the hard basalt bedrock of the atolls. Scientists elsewhere are not so sure, concerned that radiation could reach the ocean through a porous layer of limestone.
The decision to resume the tests has been criticized by the United States, Britain and Russia -- nuclear powers that have all halted testing.
Last week, the lower house of the Russian Parliament condemned the French tests, describing as "dangerous such testing in the fragile systems of coral reefs." Only China, which has continued to conduct underground nuclear experiments at Lop Nor in the western province of Xianjiang, has continued to test.
Although they can threaten nothing like the economic wallop of a Japanese boycott, the Governments of Australia and New Zealand have offered far stronger words against the French.
"An arrogant action of a European colonial power," Prime Minister Jim Bolger of New Zealand has said of the French tests. The Australian Prime Minister, Paul Keating, described the tests as "deplorable."
"We are determined to maintain the pressure on France to modify its program to desist from testing weapons and also to encourage further international focus on France," Mr. Keating said last week in Melbourne after meeting with Mr. Bolger.
