HEADLINE: Gowanus: Rougher Road Ahead;
Highway Work Will Make It Worse Before It's Better

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

 BODY:
It would be hard to blame people in Sunset Park and Red Hook for wanting to turn back the clock, to reclaim western Brooklyn as it was before the soaring, noisy eyesore known as the Gowanus Expressway was rammed through their neighborhoods.
But the river of steel and concrete forced on another generation by Robert Moses in 1941 is about to become even more of an intrusion. 
 The state plans to overhaul the 3.8-mile elevated part of the Gowanus, which is falling apart in places, at an estimated cost of $500 million to $600 million.
State officials say the highway is too important to be shut down, even briefly, so they plan to do the work piecemeal. That will take six years, far longer than it would take to build a new highway.
While the work is under way, the neighborhoods along Third Avenue, cast into shadow and economic decline 54 years ago by the creation of the Gowanus, will be filled again with noise and heavy equipment. The expressway traffic that roars or creeps past the third-floor windows of row houses will be worse, the traffic that spills onto Third Avenue will be worse and the traffic on alternative routes, like Fourth Avenue and the Fort Hamilton Parkway, will be worse, perhaps much worse.
State officials say this is the price of keeping alive the Gowanus, a commercial and commuting artery on which the city is utterly dependent, used daily by tens of thousands of commuters and trucks.
The Gowanus has always exemplified the hardships created by building a highway through an urban area. And its reconstruction will probably reinforce those lessons.
"Construction on this scale is devastating to a community," said Robert E. Paaswell, director of the University Transportation Research Center at City College. "The project may be absolutely necessary, and it may be wonderful when it's done, but the pain while it's being done is very real."
In the next few months, the State Transportation Department will release its environmental assessment of several proposals for the Gowanus and designate one as the best choice. The department will then hold public hearings and choose one option; it clearly favors keeping the expressway open while the repairs are going on, in work that could begin as early as 1997.
On the side streets where the green-painted Gowanus looms, where windows are shut even on beautiful days to keep out fumes and soot, many express a deep resignation about the discomfort ahead.
"I guess they've got to fix it, but I'm dreading it," said Alexandra Mendez, 49, who runs a day care center in her home in Sunset Park, a mixture of middle-class and working-class neighborhoods with large Hispanic and Chinese populations. Shaking her head, she glanced up at the expressway and said: "It's going to be awful. Six years . . . ."
Another Sunset Park resident, Sonia Solero, a 43-year-old homemaker, said: "I've been hearing about this for years, so I know it's coming, and I guess there's nothing we can do about it.
"It's already so bad, with the cars and the smog and the noise," she said. "A lot of times when there are accidents up on the highway, they come down here, on the streets. And it's impossible to move, and there are more accidents on the streets and it's not safe for the kids to cross the street."
She worries that having the Third Avenue median full of construction equipment and temporary supports will invite prostitution and drug dealing. "It's no good when you give people places to hide," she said.
Some residents say that the project is unnecessary and that its cost could approach $1 billion. "It's going to create a poverty zone," said Tony Giordano, executive director of Sunset Park Restoration, a community group that has fought the project. "Any time you have all that construction going on for years and years, anyone who can afford to move out of that location will move away."
 
Ahead: Traffic Jams, Noise and Traffic
 Brian T. Ketcham, a private transportation engineer who has worked with the project's opponents, acknowledged the difficulty of putting a price on noise, traffic and pollution, but he estimated that the project would cost Sunset Park $215 million, in both tangible and intangible ways.
But the Transportation Department and five independent experts interviewed say the Gowanus must remain. "We know there will be impacts," said Sandra E. Williams, the regional director of the department. "But it has to be done. We're going to go farther out of our way to mitigate the effects of this project than we've ever gone before."
The department will close three of the Gowanus's six lanes at a time. To keep traffic moving, Ms. Williams said, tow trucks will wait just off the highway to remove disabled cars quickly. The state plans to repave the streets parallel to the highway, like Fourth Avenue, before work begins on the Gowanus, so those roads can better manage the spillover. Officials hope that thousands of commuters will switch to public transportation.
Last year, state officials said the overhaul would take 10 to 12 years -- too long even by New York standards -- and that brought howls from residents and elected officials. In response, the state planned round-the-clock work schedules and decided to promise bonuses for contractors who finish work on time. The current estimate for the project is six years and four months.
On an average day, 175,000 vehicles use the Gowanus, which stretches from the Verrazano-Narrows Bridge in Bay Ridge to the Brooklyn-Queens Expressway and the Brooklyn-Battery Tunnel in Red Hook. Although the highway is important for commuters, its most important role, experts say, is in commerce.
Heavy trucks make up about 5 percent of the traffic, and that share may rise to 25 percent during off-peak hours. The Gowanus is the major route for freight moving between New Jersey and Long Island, between Manhattan and Staten Island, and between Brooklyn and much of the rest of the world.
"Brooklyn is stuck with trucks," said Jeffrey M. Zupan, a transportation consultant to the Regional Plan Association, a private group that studies development and transportation issues, "and that means it's stuck with the Gowanus. If it weren't a major truck route, I might say we could do without it, but the Gowanus serves a function for which there is no alternative."
 
6 Years of Trouble, Or 2 Years of Chaos?
 Opponents of the project argue that the state has not seriously considered alternatives. "They're talking about the most expensive highway, mile for mile, in United States history," Mr. Giordano said, "and what we are going to get for that is basically the same roadway that was built 50 years ago."
The opponents want to raze the elevated part of the expressway, north of 66th Street, and convert Third Avenue into a boulevard with local and express lanes, like the West Side Highway in Manhattan.
But a boulevard would be inadequate, say independent experts, including Mr. Ketcham, the engineer who has ties to the opposition groups. "Traffic would redistribute itself through the local streets, and I don't think that's what the community wants, either," he said.
He proposes an equally radical, and cheaper, answer: tear down the Gowanus and build a new elevated highway. "It could done in two years, to a standard that will last for 100 years," he said. His plan would trade six years or more of trouble for two years of utter chaos, a swap many would oppose.
"Close it and have all those trucks on my streets?" asked Hector Rodriguez, 46, an unemployed Red Hook resident. "No thank you."
State officials concede that Mr. Ketcham's way would be faster and less expensive, but they insist that going without the highway, even for a short time, is not an option, and many outside experts agree.
During the most disruptive part of the project, the replacement and widening of the concrete deck, three of the six lanes will be shut down at a time. Two lanes will always be open for the heaviest traffic, with one for traffic heading the other way.
The elevated section sits on steel supports, most of them anchored in the Third Avenue median. Some steel will be replaced, so heavy equipment will block parts of the avenue while much of that work is under way.
The rebuilding plan also calls for changing several features that contribute to the Gowanus's notorious traffic jams. The road has very little room outside the traffic lanes, so a single breakdown or accident always stops a lane. To create a partial shoulder, the roadbed, already more than 80 feet wide, will grow by about 3 feet. That would provide room for a car to move part of the way out of traffic.
Areas of the highway that are chronic bottlenecks or frequent accident sites will be widened or redesigned, including the transitions from the Gowanus to the B.Q.E., from the Prospect Expressway to the Gowanus, and from the Gowanus to the Shore Parkway, and the exits at 38th and 39th Streets.
 
Yet Another Blow For a Battered Street
 When the Gowanus was built in 1941, most of the immediate dire effects were felt in Sunset Park. Through the Depression, Third Avenue was lined with stores, restaurants and theaters. An elevated train line cast a venetian blind shadow on the avenue, but that line was closed in 1940.
Sunset Park residents wanted the Gowanus to run above Second Avenue, through an industrial area between Third Avenue and the waterfront. But Mr. Moses thought that using the steel pillars of the elevated tracks in the columns for his highway would make it cheaper.
The homes and businesses on the east side of avenue were demolished, and others were lost to on-and off-ramps. A hundred stores and 1,300 homes were ripped up, Robert A. Caro wrote in "The Power Broker" (Alfred A. Knopf, 1974), his Moses biography.
The avenue was pitched into darkness, and its constant flow of cars and trucks turned pedestrians away. Nearly all the businesses that were not torn down closed. Third Avenue became desolate.
"If Third Avenue was the heart of the neighborhood," Mr. Caro wrote, "Moses tore it out."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Plans to overhaul the Gowanus Expressway are being greeted apprehensively in western Brooklyn. Map/Diagram: "TRAFFIC ALERT: Rebuilding the Gowanus"
 The Gowanus Expressway, which slices through Sunset Park and Red Hook in western Brooklyn, is deteriorating. The state plans to overhaul the 3.8-mile elevatged part of the highway, adding a partial shoulder and widening or redesigning major interchanges that are the sites of chronic bottlenecks and frequent accidents.
 
SHORE PARKWAY INTERCHANGE
 The outbound Shore Parkway Interchange is the Gowanus viaduct's deadliest stretch, largely because the parkway exit is where drivers don't expect it: on the left. The state plans to reverse the ramps, and to close the Third Avenue ramp to the inbound Shore Parkway.
 
BUS/HOV LANE
 Between 92d Street and the Brooklyn-Battery Tunnel a bus and High Occupancy Vehicle lane could be created.
 
38TH AND 39TH STREET INTERCHANGE
 The interchange at j38th and 39th Streets is another accident-prone area. The overhaul would widen the exit ramps' sharp curves and lengthen their approach lanes.

HEADLINE: Sexual Assault Trial Puts a Chicago Congressman's Career on the Brink

BYLINE: By The New York Times 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, July 29

 BODY:
Five days into the trial of a Chicago Congressman charged with sexual assault, obstructing justice and soliciting child pornography, a recording of a sexually explicit telephone conversation has cast a long shadow over the short career of a rising political star, while the woman who once claimed to be his victim sits in jail, refusing to testify.
Representative Mel Reynolds, a Democrat elected in 1992 from the Second Congressional District, is accused of having sex with Beverly Heard when she was a 16-year-old campaign volunteer, and of trying to get lurid photographs of a 15-year-old girl. 
 He is also accused of persuading Ms. Heard to leave the state to avoid talking to prosecutors and to sign a sworn affidavit recanting her testimony against him before a grand jury.
If convicted, he faces a mandatory minimum sentence of four years on the sexual charges and up to seven years for obstruction of justice.
Prosecutors say that Ms. Heard, now 19, went to the Cook County State's Attorney's office last June and told them she had had sexual relations with Mr. Reynolds beginning in June 1992, after he approached her outside her high school and offered her a job. Their sexual relationship continued, she said, until September 1993, when Ms. Heard joined the Air Force.
On the basis of Ms. Heard's statements and a police-monitored telephone conversation between Ms. Heard and Mr. Reynolds, prosecutors obtained a court order to record phone conversations between the two.
Those tape recordings now represent the bulk of the prosecution's case, because Ms. Heard issued a 55-page statement last December saying that although she and Mr. Reynolds had gone to hotel rooms when she was 16 and had played with sex toys, they had never had intercourse. The statement contradicted her testimony before the grand jury last summer.
On Wednesday, the third day of the trial, Ms. Heard appeared in court for the first time since issuing her statement. She appeared nervous and downcast under questioning by the First Assistant State's Attorney, Andrea Zopp.
She first refused to answer any questions, then responded to queries about her military service. But when asked by Ms. Zopp to describe her first meeting with Mr. Reynolds, Ms. Heard invoked the Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination.
Judge Fred G. Suria of Cook County Circuit Court told Ms. Heard that she could not refuse to answer questions if no criminal charges could result from her replies, and he ordered her to answer the question. When she refused, he declared her in contempt of court.
Ms. Zopp asked another dozen questions about how and when Ms. Heard had met Mr. Reynolds, all of which Ms. Heard refused to answer. For each refusal, Judge Suria imposed a new contempt-of-court charge. Finally, he ordered her to spend the night in Cook County Jail.
On both Thursday and Friday, the process repeated itself. Today, Ms. Heard remains in jail. Unless she relents and agrees to testify, she will remain there at least until the end of the trial unless she agrees to testify.
The legality of the judge's action, however, is not clear, because the District Attorney has refused to grant Ms. Heard immunity from prosecution for perjury. And since she has sworn to two opposite versions of events, whichever version she testifies to on the witness stand would make her vulnerable to perjury charges on the other version.
Late Friday, prosecutors softened their stance on immunity from perjury charges. "If we get to the point where the judge feels she truly should not answer a question because her Fifth Amendment rights against self-incrimination would be in jeopardy, we would consider immunity," Andy Knott, a spokesman for the State's Attorney's office, said in a telephone interview.
For his part, Mr. Reynolds told reporters on Wednesday that he considered the treatment of Ms. Heard by the prosecutors and judge "a travesty."
But he had no comment about the salacious nature of the tape recordings that have now assumed center stage in his trial or about any other aspect of the case.
Jurors seemed to alternate between shock and amusement as they read a transcript of two conversations recorded last June. On the transcripts, Mr. Reynolds asks Ms. Heard what color underwear she was wearing. He then reminded her how much fun they had had "that summer" when they had sexual intercourse "out south in that Riverdale apartment." He next asks her to describe to him in detail her most recent sexual encounter with her lesbian lover.
At another point in the transcript Ms. Heard offers to introduce Mr. Reynolds to a 15-year-old friend, "Theresa," who, Ms. Heard tells him, is interested in "threesomes." Theresa was also, Ms. Heard said, a Catholic schoolgirl.
Mr. Reynolds exclaims, "Did I win the Lotto?"
Later he asks Ms. Heard to take Polaroid photographs of Theresa's breasts and genitals for him. It is that request that led to the charge of solicitation of child pornography.
In fact, Theresa never existed. She was created by Ms. Heard and the State's Attorney's office. Ms. Heard's lawyer, Eric Dunham, recently told reporters that the recordings had merely captured a sexual fantasy game that she and Mr. Reynolds played over the telephone.
That is also the stance of Mr. Reynolds's defense team. "He had no physical contact with Beverly Heard," said Sam Adam, one of the Congressman's lawyers. "He had a series of phone calls with her. Explicit, graphic phone calls. So? He's an adult. She's an adult. What's illegal? You can still talk dirty if you want to, to the best of my knowledge."
Whatever the eventual verdict, there can be little doubt that the charges will have a profound impact on Mr. Reynolds's political future, and on that of his overwhelmingly minority and lower-income district.
Three years ago, Mr. Reynolds, a Rhodes Scholar and lawyer, defeated Gus Savage, the incendiary, race-baiting incumbent, in the Democratic primary.
"He was the opposite of Gus Savage, who ran on a platform of racial polarization," said David Axelrod, a Chicago Democratic political consultant.
In Washington, Mr. Reynolds won a coveted seat on the House Ways and Means Committee. From that foundation, he seemed poised to become one of the more visible young black voices in Congress.
But when the Democrats lost their House majority in the 1994 elections, Mr. Reynolds lost his Ways and Means seat. Today, he sits on the much less powerful Economic and Educational Opportunities panel.
He is also facing a separate Federal investigation of his campaign finances and of whether he falsified information to receive a bank loan. "There is likely to be a large group of voters in the Second District, especially churchgoers and conservative suburban and middle-class voters, who will have a hard time supporting him in 1996," says Alan Gitleson, a professor of political science at Loyola University of Chicago. "But then there is another group that will likely support him more vocally exactly because of the charges he's faced. This group will use the claims that he was victimized by the white establishment and by the media and the unfair justice system to increase sympathy for him."
Mr. Axelrod, the political consultant, adds: "If Mel runs in 1996, it will probably be as a mirror image of Gus Savage. He will embrace everything he once rejected. I think we'll see him playing the race card for all it's worth."
For now, Mr. Reynolds refuses to say whether he will run in 1996 if acquitted. But even if he should win re-election in 1996, it seems unlikely that he would ever achieve the prominence for which he once seemed destined. "The national Democratic Party will probably stay as far away from him as possible," Mr. Gitelson says. "There won't be any of those plum committee assignments ever again."
Even so, Mr. Reynolds is not without support. On the sidewalk outside the courtroom on Thursday afternoon, the Congressman was approached by three young black men who embraced him and shook his hand.
"Give 'em hell, Mel," said one.
"Yeah," said another, "we're with you, man. We'll always be with you."

HEADLINE: ON BASEBALL;
The Market Is Strong for Hired Guns

BYLINE: By Claire Smith 

DATELINE: MINNEAPOLIS

 BODY:
Have Gun, Will Travel. Wire . . . David Cone.
Peace in the Wild West, peace in the Transvaal may have made the gun-toting mercenary passe. But the ball-throwing variety is gaining in stature as never before. 
 It used to be that the term hired gun applied to precious few strong-armed baseball players. Through the 1980's, Doyle Alexander had the market practically to himself, riding from town to town, providing innings and, late in his career, more than a few victories and a division championship or two.
Now, though, the woods are full of Doyle Alexanders, as economics and the assertiveness of big-market contenders has caused antsy also-rans and cash-poor teams to dump expensive talent in exchange for unproven, cheap diamonds in the rough.
So it is that Jim Abbott and Ken Hill changed teams this week. Then there's Cone, the former Royals prospect turned Mets ace, turned Blue Jays pennant insurance, turned reborn Royal-for-life turned Blue Jay gamble, turned Yankees savior.
On Friday, Cone was shipped by Toronto to the Yankees, a pitching-poor team seeking pennant-race insurance. And, as the Yankees had hoped, Cone paid immediate dividends by stopping the Minnesota Twins on six hits over eight innings to win, 4-2, last night.
Cone's 10th victory of the season was his first in a long while in a game that had importance as he helped the resurgent Yankees keep up the pressure on the first-place Boston Red Sox.
Like Abbott and Hill, Cone is leaving a club that acquired him with high hopes in the spring only to march full speed into a rebuilding process once the season fizzled.
"Maybe it's the state of the game," Cone said. "Kirby Puckett said when the Twins traded Rick Aguilera, there's no more loyalty. And you're probably going to see a lot more of this in the future as far as rosters getting turned over quicker and players getting moved."
When Cone donned a Yankee uniform yesterday, it made official the sixth stop in his relatively short career, a phenomenon that would have been unheard of for such a talent in past eras.
So, too, would have been the movement of an incumbent Cy Young Award winner. Cone is now two teams removed from the season in which he won the pitching honors as a Kansas City Royal.
As Cone said, he knows that the moving of players like so many chess pieces is now the reality. It is also ironic that he is so intertwined with others, like Bobby Bonilla, his former Met teammate turned Oriole. Then there's Bret Saberhagen, Cone's teammate in Kansas City and in New York. The Mets right-hander threw eight shutout innings last night in what could have been a nifty audition for courting contenders like the Red Sox.
"He's a good friend," Cone said of Saberhagen. "We played together in Kansas City. I bought his house when he went to the Mets and I went back to Kansas City. I always watch him and pull for him. It would be extremely ironic if he ended up with the Red Sox."
Ironic, but not surprising, not since what was once an identifiable core of untouchables on each team became obsolete. Add the talents and track record of a pitcher like Cone and it was a no-brainer that he would not only be sought after, but moved to a contender in the days leading to the July 31 trading deadline.
"It's not puzzling at all," said Buck Showalter, the 1995 winner of the David Cone sweepstakes. "He's coveted."
From Kansas City to New York and back, again, Cone has shown that he is mentally and physically equipped to handle not only life's little bounces, but the major potholes as well. And as New Yorkers know, potholes can be killers in the Big Apple. Cone traversed such potholes.
It would be an upset if the witty and media-savvy Cone was unable to handle the Bronx Zoo. Cone is perfectly suited for the role now handed him, just as Alexander was, but for entirely different reasons.
Alexander, hardened by an unsettling trade from the Dodgers to the Orioles early in his career, embittered by that truly forgettable tenure in the Bronx Zoo, settled into a steely, but detached, resolve. It showed when he pitched the division-clinching victory for Toronto over the Yankees in 1985.
Alexander went on to author one of the more incredible performances following a late-season trade in 1987. After being sent to the Tigers by the Atlanta Braves for a youngster named John Smoltz, Alexander started 11 games down the stretch. He won nine times. The Tigers won all his starts.
But while Alexander could win games for teams, he wouldn't bleed for them. He rented his skills, not his heart and soul.
Cone does both. He, too, can be rented and help win, as he showed in 1992. That year it was the disappointing Mets who did the selling and the surging Blue Jays who did the buying. Cone made seven starts down the stretch. Though he won four and lost three, he limited the opposition to four earned runs over the course of 44 1/3 innings in his last five starts.
Cone also oozes with emotion. But it is not a bitter emotion.
"After a while I guess it would wear you down, but right now I really don't feel it," Cone said after last night's game, adding: "I don't mind being called a hired gun; it doesn't bother me. I consider it a great challenge. If I can come through and pitch well for this team, who knows what the future will hold?"
Cone was one of the more ardent and articulate union representatives during the strike that halted the 1994 season and the start of the 1995 campaign. He speaks with great emotion.
Cone is a gypsy asked to harness such emotion, because teams know he can.
Perhaps Cone will settle in New York for a good while this time. That will happen only if the Yankees win with Cone instead of losing in spite of him. For to do the latter would inevitably make Cone what he was Friday: a quality commodity more likely to be sold than savored.

HEADLINE: Speak Up for Harry Wu

 BODY:
China now claims that the American human rights activist Harry Wu has confessed to falsifying information in two television documentaries on the Chinese prison system. The claims, made in a report by China's official news agency, Xinhua, and a videotape sold to news agencies by a state-affiliated firm, cannot be independently assessed at this point.
 The tape shows a haggard Mr. Wu telling four interrogators that a number of scenes in the British Broadcasting Corporation programs were falsely or misleadingly identified. But nowhere does he say that he was personally involved in any misrepresentations in the broadcasts. Nor does he admit to the more serious charge of espionage on which he is now being held. The BBC defends the accuracy of its two programs and plans a detailed rebuttal. 
 Mr. Wu, of course, is not free to tell his side of the story. He is a prisoner of his Chinese interrogators, threatened with execution. He has not been allowed to talk to American consular officials since an initial interview three weeks ago.
 But if Mr. Wu is constrained in what he can say, Secretary of State Warren Christopher, who meets China's Foreign Minister, Qian Qichen, on Tuesday in Brunei, is not. While the Clinton Administration rightly wants to halt the dangerous deterioration in relations between Washington and Beijing, China now needs to hear a firm message -- about Harry Wu and about human rights in general.
 The Administration also needs to draw a firmer connection between Beijing's conduct and the hospitality it will offer China's top leaders. President Clinton is about to invite China's President and party leader, Jiang Zemin, to meet with him when Mr. Jiang visits the United Nations this fall.
 This would be an inappropriate moment to bestow such an invitation, which Mr. Jiang has long sought as a way to boost his personal prestige as he struggles to succeed Deng Xiaoping. Not only is Mr. Wu's case ominously unresolved. China has spent most of the last two months accusing the United States of all manner of imagined offenses while locking up high-profile dissidents, menacing Taiwan with missile tests and, according to U.S. intelligence officials, exporting prohibited missile parts to Iran and Pakistan.
 America has a lot to lose if the continued downward drift of relations between the United States and China cannot be checked. But China has an even greater stake in halting the slide.
 China exports four times as much to the United States as it imports, gaining in exchange vital hard currency resources it could not obtain anywhere else. Further unraveling of relations would severely constrain the pace of China's economic development, which its leaders see as the key to political stability.
 China's strategy of diverting attention from its own offenses against international norms by loudly and falsely accusing others should not be rewarded. Mr. Christopher should use his meeting with Mr. Qian to patiently detail America's legitimate grievances with China and search for constructive solutions. Mr. Clinton should put off meeting with Mr. Jiang until some of these grievances have been addressed and Mr. Wu is safely back on American soil.

HEADLINE: For the Record/The Pork Barrel;
Watch Out With That Budget Ax. My District NEEDS Than Dam.

BYLINE: Michael Wines 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
For four decades, the Democrats who ruled Congress larded Federal spending bills with pork. Locks, dams, people-movers, Federal research centers, passenger rail lines, electric buses, airport control towers, highway "demonstration projects" and Lord knows what else rained from Washington like inflation-adjusted pennies from heaven.
 Everybody on Capitol Hill got wet. But Republicans got damp, while Democrats got soaked. And the Democrats who dispensed the goodies -- those who sat on Congress's appropriations and authorizing committees -- were so flooded that they needed dams, which, fortunately, they were in a position to finance. 
 Whether this was representative government at work or thinly veiled corruption was a matter of pitched debate. Democrats asserted that the projects weren't pork, but vital additions to the national infrastructure. Republicans accused Democrats of trying to buy votes with highways and frittering away the national wealth. And they took power last November in no small part on a pledge to change it all.
 So how are they doing? Buy way of illustration, here is a selection of Republicans on the House Appropriations Committee and the House Transportation and Infrastructure Committee, along with those identifiable projects earmarked for their Congressional districts in the transportation spending bill that the House passed last week. (The list is taken from those projects that the committee specifically ordered financed even when Government agencies had not asked for them). For comparison, Democrats whose districts can likewise be identified with specific projects are also listed.
 Other Democrats and Republicans in the House also were awarded projects for their districts in the legislation. But the preponderance of the grants, largely for transit programs, went to committee members.
 Sooner or later, Democrats will surely accuse Republicans of spending these precious tax dollars to buy notes. Republicans already have a reply, expressed eloquently by Representative Jim Bunning, a Kentucky Republican, on the House floor just last week. "This project is not pork," he said, defending his own allotment for a freeway. "This project is a vital infrastructure necessity."

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (94%);

HEADLINE: NEW YORK 1945;
The War Was Ending. Times Square Exploded. Change Was Coming.

BYLINE: By SAM ROBERTS

 BODY:
FOR millions of New Yorkers waiting anxiously in Times Square, World War II ended at 7:03 P.M. Aug. 14, 1945. At that moment, the 15,000 light bulbs on the Times Tower's zipper sign spelled out five words that touched off a tumultuous celebration: "Official -- Truman announces Japanese surrender."
After surviving years of scarcity and sacrifice, with London bombed, Paris humbled and Rome, Berlin and Tokyo occupied by foreign troops, New York had suddenly been elevated from the nation's major metropolis to the unrivaled world capital. 
 "All City 'Lets Go,' " a New York Times front-age headline exulted the next day, merely hinting at the outpouring to come later with V-J Day and the formal signing of the surrender. If the day's jubilant headlines reported the present, some of the advertisements suggested the future.
One, aimed at executives in the nation's largest manufacturing city, exhorted: "Bring Your Factory to Texas." The ad pointedly cited Texas's tax advantages, a large, semi-skilled labor force idled by peace and the potential markets developing in the Southwest. Another ad, from the Academy of Languages, promised readers with an eye on Latin America or East Harlem that by taking advantage of "the famous Ibarra method" they could learn Spanish in record time.
And in the classifieds (in which apartment seekers and others still insistently identified themselves as "Christian"), the greatest demand for houses was in the suburbs. Typical was an ad placed by an executive with two school-age children who was looking for a place with three or four bedrooms within one hour's commuting from New York City.
Today, the youngest World War II recruit would be close to 70 years old. Of all the people living in New York City today, fewer than one in four were even born in 1945, much less remember firsthand what the city was like then.
In the summer of 1945, New York was a city riding a wave of triumph, even as the undertow of the future began to tug at its feet. It was a time of unbridled self-confidence. The city had contributed 850,000 servicemen to the war effort. The war had transformed New York into the capital of the world. By 1946, seven airlines offered regular service between Queens and Europe, and the United Nations would later settled on the East Side of Manhattan, where the Modernist headquarters of an organization devoted to peace displaced a stand of odorous slaughterhouses.
As Jan Morris recalled in her book "Manhattan '45" (Oxford University Press, 1987), a promotional brochure from Bankers Trust proclaimed that New Yorkers were a people "to whom nothing is impossible."
"There were no strangers in New York yesterday," Alexander Feinberg wrote in The Times, reporting on the celebration of Japan's surrender.
But a Depression, a world war, migrations and government policies had already laid the foundation for profound political, social and economic change.
During the war, shortages of food-ration stamps for grocery purchases led more people to eat out. (Restaurants were serving eight million meals a day during the war compared with three million before.) Women moved in droves into the work force, replacing soldiers. The dearth of housing construction, coupled with an influx of defense workers, produced another shortage whose mixed legacy endures today: rent controls. When the war ended, 75,000 New Yorkers were still waiting for telephone service because so few new instruments were being manufactured. (By choice, not necessity, Mayor Fiorello H. La Guardia did not have a telephone on his desk at City Hall). A few months after V-J Day, 25,000 women mobbed Macy's after a sales clerk posted a sign advertising nylons.
Still, New York had not let a war a continent away (but within U-boat sight of New York harbor) disrupt its routine any more than necessary. The national curfew was midnight, but Mayor La Guardia insisted that New Yorkers be allowed to drink, legally, until 1 A.M. On West 56th Street, Miyako's, a traditional Japanese restaurant, took its orders from loyal customers -- not from Tokyo -- throughout the war. Broadway was awash with blockbusters, including "Carousel," "Harvey," "Oklahoma!" "On the Town" and a play, "The Glass Menagerie," by a popular new writer, Tennessee Williams. The city's baseball teams were doing less well on Aug. 14, 1945: The Yankees and Giants each in fourth place, the Dodgers in third.
 
THE OTHER SIDE OF THE GOOD OLD DAYS
During the war years, Brooklyn still had its own daily newspaper (Queens had two, the Bronx one) and its own baseball team (run by Branch Rickey, who, a year later, broke the sport's color barrier by recruiting Jackie Robinson). Harlem was still a mecca for aristocrats of black culture. Downtown, Manhattan was still home to Augustus Van Horne, the last descendant of Peter Stuyvesant.
In the summer and fall of 1945, millions of them turned out for separate ticker tape parades to welcome Eisenhower, de Gaulle, Gen. Jonathan Mayhew Wainwright, Adm. Chester W. Nimitz and Claire Chennault. Harry S. Truman visited that fall, in what was described as the first time a sitting President paid his respects to a New York City mayor at City Hall. La Guardia read the funnies on radio during a newspaper strike that year (providing, perhaps, besides the airport that bears his name, the most durable memory of his mayoralty).
But if, apart from the anxiety about loved ones overseas, the war years seem in retrospect like the good old days, they were not that good (though, in some respects, statistically, at least, better than today). About 165,000 families were living doubled up. Welfare consumed $70 million or nearly 10 cents of every dollar of the city's budget (compared with nearly 25 cents for the Human Resources Administration today). The poor were growing so out of hand that the Mayor tightened a ban on begging in the subway. New mothers were leaving about 1,000 babies a year at the New York Foundling Hospital.
New York, as it has always been, was a changing city in the 1940's. And, as always, the instruments of that change were considered suspect -- even when they were fellow Americans lured, like newcomers before and since, by the promise of better life in the form of defense industry jobs.
Puerto Ricans, outgrowing the island's agricultural economy, began migrating to the city during the decade in record numbers -- tripling their population to nearly 190,000 by 1950. As a result of an influx from the American South, the city's black population rose by more than 60 percent.
But, much more so than today, the city was racially segregated. It was still acceptable then not only to admit it but to justify it. Sacrificing civil rights for an opportunity to lure private investment to vitally needed housing, in 1943 La Guardia reluctantly agreed to municipal tax breaks that would enable the Metropolitan Life Insurance Company to build Stuyvesant Town in Manhattan -- apartments for which blacks need not apply. As the company's president explained at the time, "Negroes and whites don't mix."
The City Council adopted a resolution prohibiting city participation in any similar projects unless the agreements with private developers included an anti-discrimination clause. La Guardia, seeking to reconcile seemingly intractable goals, wrote the insurance company that if the tenant selection process at Stuyvesant Town were ever challenged in court, the city's position would be that racial discrimination should not be tolerated. Charles V. Hamilton, researching his biography of Adam Clayton Powell Jr., later discovered a copy of the letter in the Mayor's files with a handwritten notation: "Not sent."
The following year, Metropolitan Life announced plans for another housing project, the Riverton in Harlem. That was the same year that Harlem proudly elected Powell as New York's first black Congressman, projecting his advocacy involving issues like Stuyvesant Town onto the national stage and onto the House floor, where using the word "nigger" was still tolerated.
In Brooklyn's Little Harlem, as Bedford-Stuyvesant was known, a grand jury investigation concluded that "it has become dangerous and unsafe to traverse the streets of this area before and particularly after dark." The grand jurors took pains, though, to blame the situation not on race but on lapses in law enforcement and on social conditions, including too many people illegally collecting home relief and living tripled up or worse in former one-family homes, many of which had been carved into rooming houses for Brooklyn Navy Yard workers. (As if to further confirm that crime was an equal-opportunity employer, a sign posted in the changing rooms at S. Klein warned in Italian and Yiddish, as well as English: "Do not disgrace your family. The punishment for stealing is jail.")
The Brooklyn grand jury reported that "many churches have closed completely because their parishioners do not dare attend the evening services," that "groups of young boys armed with penknives of all sizes and other weapons roam the streets at will and threaten and assault passers-by," and that "many law-abiding children and adults residing in this area are therefore forced to carry similar knives and weapons for protection."
Foreshadowing an era plagued by similar problems but later bedtimes, the grand jury suggested that at 9 every night radio stations broadcast the question: "Parents, where are your children right now?"
Wartime manpower demands had shrunk the police force, which was further challenged after V-E Day by a growing arsenal of illegal guns brought home by returning servicemen. After V-J Day, there was nearly one murder a day committed in the city, or about double the previous year's rate. The toll for all 1945 was 292.
 
LA GUARDIA STEPS ASIDE; TAMMANY HALL IN DECLINE
Early that year, after three tumultuous terms, La Guardia decided not to seek re-election. The mayor, Thomas Kessner wrote in his biography, "left no party legacy, no structure, no institutional fusion or urban reform movement." After being shut out of the mayoralty for 12 years, the Democrats were revived by the election of William J. O'Dwyer, the former Brooklyn District Attorney (whose grand jury had produced the Bedford-Stuyvesant report). The selection of O'Dwyer, who had arrived in New York City from Ireland at the age of 19, marked the 11th consecutive time (since 1909) that the Democrats had chosen a mayoral nominee of Irish descent.
But the election of New York City's 100th mayor was also a turning point, even if it took another two decades for the decline of the Democratic Party machine to play itself out. Tammany, unable to meet the mortgage payments, had to sell its Union Square headquarters. Its demise was further signaled by two developments: New Deal programs had largely supplanted the party as a provider for the poor and powerless (by 1946, more than 100,000 people were receiving relief); and, in the year of the ethnically-balanced ticket, a Hollywood producer created campaign "newsreels" for the Democratic ticket, which foreshadowed the radio and television commercials that candidates would soon regularly broadcast to appeal directly to the voters.
Moreover, Edward N. Costikyan, a former Tammany leader, remembers: "World War II broke the geographic solidity of the Democratic Party's control over the electorate. After the war, people were more mobile and their long-term relationships with their district captains and district leaders disappeared."
Winning wasn't everything. What O'Dwyer inherited, he later recalled, was a city in which "not a single hospital had seen a paintbrush in the 16 years prior to my swearing-in ceremonies.
"In the meantime," he said, "the city's population had increased by a million residents, and each department was woefully undermanned. The smoke was choking New Yorkers, and the raw sewage had long ago discouraged fish in the bay or in the North River and had rendered our beaches dangerous for bathing . . . Veterans were returning home with no place to live . . . the rapid transit system, once our pride and joy, was falling apart and badly in need of equipment and repair."
The morning after the election, he also inherited a plan. A minion of Robert Moses, whose 1939 World's Fair had made the public -- and himself -- absolutely giddy about the future, delivered the master builder's master plan for reconstructing the city. His ambitious blueprint with a $1.5 billion price tag called for completing the Brooklyn-Battery Tunnel and Idlewild Airport and for new housing, as he prepared to bulldoze existing homes to complete a network of highways, including three elevated expressways that would slice across Manhattan.
Soon after, Cleveland Rodgers, a City Planning Commissioner, and Rebecca B. Rankin, librarian of the Municipal Reference Library, echoed O'Dwyer's lament. "New York has never kept up with the demands for vitally needed facilities and services," they wrote in 1947. "It has not solved the basic problems of existence. Nowhere are there more glaring contrasts between rich and poor. Life is hard for millions and living and working conditions are deplorable in many respects."
Life in the city was also increasingly congested. (Unlike many other American cities, New York had lots of undeveloped land then, but Manhattan still had more residents than Queens.) Gas rationing during the war had slowed the migration to the suburbs. But between 1940 and 1950, for the first time, more people moved out of the city than in (over all, though, the population rose from about 7.4 million to more than 7.8 million because of higher birth rates at the beginning of the Baby Boom). While nearly 100,000 more foreigners, 230,000 nonwhites and 130,000 Puerto Ricans settled in the city than moved away, at least 340,000 more non-Hispanic whites left than arrived -- a middle-class exodus that accelerated in succeeding decades.
 
THE RISE OF THE CAR, THE SPREAD OF HIGHWAYS
Meanwhile, more and more of those who had left were returning to work in the city by car. By 1950 (the year that alternate-side parking was instituted and one year after O'Dwyer finally doubled the nickel subway fare), nearly 120,000 people were commuting to the city by car -- more than three times the number only two decades earlier.
Even more fundamental changes would soon be wrought by the deliberate and the unintended consequences of the Government's postwar policies on housing, transportation and veterans' benefits as the city's political influence waned in Washington. At the beginning of 1945 -- and not since then -- the President of the United States, Roosevelt, was a native New Yorker. More congressmen came from New York City that year than represented the entire state of California.
That an expected recession, like the one that occurred after World War 1, did not materialize reflected, in part, the Government's decision not to limit veterans' pensions to the elderly and disabled but to subsidize the education of returning soldiers and their ability to buy a home. But the criteria for Federally insured mortgages generally favored suburban developments, not urban housing -- just as Robert Moses's network of parkways had opened Long Island, Westchester and Connecticut to an influx of New York City expatriates.
Plenty of people were left, though, and more were on their way. By the end of the decade, E. B. White would write a paean to New York, with a caveat. Never, he wrote then, had the city been "so uncomfortable, so crowded, so tense."
 
 
 
The Summer of '45: A Diary
 The summer is dominated by news of the war. Many foods are in short supply. Produce store owners close their doors to protest the price ceilings set by the Office of Price Administration. Horse meat is shipped from the Midwest for people who miss beef. Advertisers ask readers to support them because they are supporting "our boys overseas."

AUG. 7 It is reported that if an atomic bomb were dropped in Brooklyn, it could entirely wipe out Flatbush and parts of Bay Ridge and East New York. Editorials proclaim the bomb to be a triumph of science but warn of its powers.
 
AUG. 8 Alfred P. Sloan, chairman of General Motors, says the Sloan Foundation will donate $4 million toward a new cancer research institute. It will be run by Charles F. Kettering.
 
AUG. 9 Atomic bomb is dropped on Nagasaki.

AUG. 12 Mayor La Guardia announces a study of baseball's color line.
 
AUG. 13 A celebration in Times Square is stopped when it is discovered that Japan has not surrendered yet, as was thought.
 
AUG. 14 Restaurant and hotel owners say they can't wait until the soldiers return so they won't have to hire unqualified people.
 
AUG. 15 Japan surrenders, and two million people celebrate in the Times Square area.
 
AUG. 27 A boy who is beaten by his father is asked by a wealthy banker to spend a week at his country home.

HEADLINE: NEIGHBORHOOD REPORT: RED HOOK;
Two Cranes and Deep Water Help Put Hum Back in a Troubles Port

BYLINE: By MICHAEL COOPER

 BODY:
The Brooklyn waterfront once hummed with activity as hundreds of longshoremen hauled freight each day from dozens of ships onto trains and trucks. But in the last 30 years, most of that business fled to newer, more spacious piers in New Jersey.
In the last year the city's Economic Development Corporation moved two refurbished, 120-foot cranes from the South Brooklyn Marine Terminal in Sunset Park to the struggling Red Hook Marine Terminal, the only port in the city where cargo containers are unloaded from ships. The agency also met with shipping companies to help Red Hook snare new contracts. 
 The efforts seem to be paying off. Sal F. Catucci, the chief executive of American Stevedoring, which began managing the Red Hook terminal in 1994, said it was handling almost twice as much freight as last year.
This year Red Hook has unloaded about 20,000 cargo containers from ships and 11,000 from barges, Mr. Catucci said. It expects to unload a total of 35,000 containers from 288 ships and 20,000 from barges. In 1994 it unloaded 18,000 containers from 228 ships and nearly 7,000 from barges. Mr. Catucci said he employed 125 to 300 longshoremen a day, up 10 percent from last year.
Development agency officials said that working in Brooklyn's favor is that Buttermilk Channel, which leads to the Red Hook piers, goes down 65 feet before it hits rock. The channel is about 40 feet deep now, but its sandy bottom could be dredged to accommodate larger ships. By comparison, Kill Van Kull, which leads into Newark Bay, hits rock at 40 feet.
"Ships are getting bigger and deeper," said Representative Jerrold L. Nadler, a Manhattan Democrat who leads a private group that is lobbying the Empire State Development Corporation to study how to develop the Brooklyn port. "When you drive along the Shore Parkway and see ships idling, they are waiting for high tide so they can go." The group has also asked Mayor Giuliani to halt waterfront development until a study can be completed.
For Mr. Catucci, such a study cannot come too quickly. "Brooklyn has the deep water," he said. "This is where the port should be."
 MICHAEL COOPER

HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.;
Tales From the Sky

BYLINE: By CHASTITY PRATT

 BODY:
FROM where Frank Kind works, sidewalks look like gray chalk lines, billboards are the size of postcards and Central Park is a tidy mosaic of bonsai trees and bushes.
When he is perched on a scaffold 45 floors above the cement sidewalk, Mr. Kind, 58, doesn't think about his brother-in-law Harry Heller, who fell from a scaffold and ended up with one leg shorter than the other. And he never looks down. "I just pay attention," he said, "to what I'm doing." 
 Mr. Kind has been a window washer for 43 years, since he was 15. When he started in the trade, he used a wooden chair suspended by rope from building rooftops or a cotton belt hooked around his waist and onto the outside of the building where he worked. In those days, scaffolds were little more than wooden planks powered by rope and muscle. Sometimes a belt would fray, a hook would pull free from masonry, a plank would tip. Window washers know the horror stories: four washers who fell from a scaffold at the Equitable Building in the 60's, another killed when his cotton belt tore in 1970, two others who died in 1990 when their scaffold plunged from the 12th floor of the Holiday Inn Crowne Plaza in Times Square.
But today, nylon has replaced cotton, and hooks are bolted into window frames. New skyscrapers have customized wire scaffolds, powered by electricity and equipped with walkie-talkies and fire extinguishers.
Everyone agrees that the window washer's job is safer. Still, working conditions are not for the weak of will or stomach. On Thursday, a fierce wind smashed a scaffold into a window of 919 Third Avenue, near 56th Street. A facade repairman crawled through the broken window onto the 22d floor, but another worker dangled from a safety rope 20 floors above the sidewalk until a lawyer in the building saved him.
Other enemies of the window washer are wild birds that attack when their building-ledge nests are disturbed and windows that slam shut, leaving a washer dangling -- sometimes for hours -- with no way to climb back inside. And there is the occasional thrill seeker, be it nudist or parachutist.
"I was on the 110th floor of the Twin Towers when the guy jumped out with a parachute," Mr. Kind recalled as he and his partner, William Bubak, 61, took a break from the hundreds of windows they wash regularly at 888 Seventh Avenue, a 45-story office tower at 57th Street. "He looked at me, I looked at him and he jumped out and pulled the cord. The Port Authority called me and asked why I didn't stop him. I said, 'I didn't have a parachute.' "
Cesar Gerena still remembers the time he was washing windows at an apartment building on 58th Street. "The guys inside say, 'Finally, we can see; it's that time again,' " Mr. Gerena said. "A lady walked past naked in the window right across from the ones I just washed. She did it every day at 8 A.M. That's New York."
Window washers in the city belong to Window Cleaners Local 2. Managers of large office and apartment buildings usually contract with maintenance companies, which get their workers from the union. The local president, Arnold Geller, said "business was good" until about 1985; then the recession brought commercial construction to a near-halt and forced companies to slash maintenance. Union membership plummeted. Today, Mr. Geller said, it is rebounding. "Window cleaning," he explained, "follows construction and the economy."
Most cleaners earn about $40,000 a year. Among the job's perks: bumping into someone famous, like a movie director who needs a window washer in his next film. "I was working at 22 Broadway when Oliver Stone saw me in the lobby and says, 'Hey, how long you been doing this?' " said Patrick DeFillippo, 45, of Glendale, Queens. "I was with them all day taping 'Wall Street.' In that scene where Michael Douglas is arguing with Charlie Sheen, he goes to the window and waves at me. I got paid good for it, too."
The typical window cleaner's day begins between 5 and 6 A.M., before the sun starts its ascent, and ends before it scorches their skin and drenches their drab blue uniforms in perspiration. From their perches, they watch the city yawn its way into the working day. Their tales are told matter-of-factly; the daily monotony strengthens the gut.
"In 1969 was I working on the 19th floor, and a hook came out of the window," Mr. Bubak recalled. "I swung off the window, hit my head against the building and was out cold. I gave it up for seven years." But, he said, the pay brought him back. "Sometimes I still get butterflies if I haven't been on the scaffold for a while, but it goes away."
Mr. Kind started washing windows with his father, Jacob, in 1952. His sons, Jason and Steve, have followed him into the union. A 14-karat squeegee hanging from a gold rope chain around his neck has three diamonds -- one for each generation of window cleaners in his family.
"It's quiet up here," said Jason Kind, 21, looking toward New Jersey from the roof of 888 Seventh Avenue. "All you hear is the wind and the air-conditioner motors. You get a rush; it's like bungee jumping."
His father and other veteran washers also speak of the rush. Some say modern safety measures have taken the thrill out of the job. "Years ago, it used to be that there was no limit on how high you could go on rope," Frank Kind said. "You climbed over the roof into the chair. Twenty-five, 40 floors. The good thing with rope is you knew you were going down and you knew you were coming back up."
Bill Bubak agrees, especially about the mandatory tether from the roof to a washer in a chair or on a scaffold. "You got this lifeline connected to you that smacks you in the face," he said. "It's a pain. Besides, once you get so high, it doesn't matter anymore. It's all the same if you fall."
 
ON THE JOB
Squeezing Squeegees in the Sky
Some facts about window-washer in the city:
Numbers 800, down from peak of 3,000 in 1916.
Salary $16.91/hour, plus $11/day for scaffold work. Average annual salary: $40,000-$42,000.
Training Six months on the job, supervised by veteran cleaner. Each cleaner must be certified by U.S. Occupational Safety and Health Administration for every type of scaffold used.
Equipment Extension ladder for jobs up to 30 feet; Rope-suspended chair for up to 75 feet; Electric scaffolds and nylon belts have no height restrictions.
(Source: Window Cleaners Local 2)

HEADLINE: WAR WITHOUT END

BYLINE: By SEBASTIAO SALGADO

 BODY:
When President Clinton announced earlier this month that he was normalizing relations with Vietnam, he spoke with care about the 2,202 United States service personnel officially listed as missing in Southeast Asia. He said next to nothing, however, about the million or more Vietnamese who became P.O.W's and M.I.A.'s of a different kind after Saigon fell to North Vietnamese troops 20 years ago. The Communist victors violently repressed freedom of expression, of commerce, of movement, of religion, of association. Hundreds of thousands of Vietnamese were sent to "re-education" camps -- there to undergo a regimen of forced labor, starvation rations and torture. Nearly a million others fled, packing themselves into small boats that drifted over the South China Sea. Many died on the waters. Nearly 800,000 survived, and most of them were eventually settled in America.
 Normalization "will advance the cause of freedom," the President declared, and he promised that his Administration would discuss human rights issues with Hanoi. But he was careful not to mention the 40,000 Vietnamese boat people who remain in detention camps -- half of them in Hong Kong; the rest in Indonesia, Malaysia, the Philippines and Thailand. Almost all these detainees have been declared economic migrants, not political refugees, by the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees, whose representatives screen the boat peole -- hostily and unjustly, the detainees and their human rights advocates maintain. As a result, these boat people cannot receive asylum under international law. They have been presented with a United Nations plan, endorsed by the Administration, that seeks to close the camps by encouraging those remaining in them to return to Vietnam voluntarily -- and, should this fail, by deporting them to Vietnam forcibly. 
 The President spoke of how Vietnam's economy is liberalizing. The detainees I've spoken with in camps in Hong Kong and the Philippines are all for economic reform, but insist it is meaningless without political reform. They know that the Communist Party in Vietnam remains all powerful, that Buddhist monks and Government critics are often jailed and that free trade and a booming business sector do not mean freedom and democracy.
 As Sebastiao Salgado shows in his photographs, days in the camps are achingly dull and depressing. There is nothing to do but worry. Men, women and children lie shelved on bare-board bunks, fenced off from the world. Pam Baker, an asylum lawyer I met in Hong Kong, observed: "The effects of prolonged detention, especially on kids, is just devastating. You see them when they come out, this line of snot-nosed kids staring at a dog. They've never seen one before. Cockroaches and rats are the oly animals they know."
 Still, detainees refuse to go back. In June, detainees in Hong Kong and Malaysia who were faced with forced repatriation began to riot. Others who had agreed to go back changed their minds. Later, a number of them said they had been encouraged by reports of legislation, sponsored by House Republicans, that could result in offers of asylum to as many as half the detainees. The bill faces a Presidential veto; opponents of this asylum plan argue that there is not once documented case of a returnee being politically persecuted. Human rights monitors I've spoken with do not deny this, but say there are many reasons to fear persecution. And so another battle looms in the war that will not end.
 Salgado's images are more quietly unsettling than these we associate with the Vietnam War. Yet they tell of people still reeling from decades of violence and dread. Peasant flock to the big cities in search of more money and liberty. City dwellers wend their way through a system that might -- just might -- get them legally to the United States. Detainees at the Galang Island Camp in Indonesia depart for Vietnam as part of a repatriation. Look at the expressions -- stony or grief-stricken -- of the family members Salgado saw and photographed that day at Galang. Look how he found them, abandoning the hopes that carried them across the sea. These are people turning to face 20 years of defeat.
 Studying these picutres, I found myself recalling a conversation I had last winter with one detainee who was about to be repatriated. "These years in the camp have been the most significant time of my life because I can sit here and talk to you like this about anything I want," he said. "When I go back, I have to put myself into the society and help myself survive. If I see you in Vietnam, I will avoid you." PHILIP GOUREVITCH
 Sebastiao Salgado is a contributing photographer for the Magazine. His last project, a photo essay of Russians immigrating to the Unitd States, appeared in November 1994. Philip Gourevitch, a contributing editor of The Forward, wrote recently about the boat people for Granta.

GRAPHIC: Photos: HONG KONG -- Half of the 40,000 boat people who were not resettled in the United States have been detained at camps in this British colony. At Whitehead, on the preceding page, some 4,400 of the 11,000 Vietnamese held are children, many of whom have never seen a live animal. At High Island, right, daily life for the 5,000 Vietnamese who are stored there -- one family per cubicle -- is akin to existence in an overflowing prison. Today, there are 5,000 boat people left on Galang. And like other Vietnamese detainees, they are being encouraged by the United Nations and the Clinton Administration to return to Vietnam voluntarily, a repatriation they have greeted with dread. When a family does agree to return, it often dispatches younger members first, delegating to them the responsibility of reconnoitering the homeland before they decide whether to follow. Such separations are fraught with torment; the leave-takings are anguished. This statue, carved by a detainee and located at the camp's entrance, makes clear where they would rather go. (pg. 28); HO CHI MIHN CITY -- The journey back to Vietnam is a fearful one for the boat people who decide to make it. Leaving a wretched existence behind, they are heading toward the promise of not much more. At least in the detention camps, there are family and friends. The country they are about to enter is not the one they fled; if anything, things may have got worse. At the end of the war in 1975, the population of Saigon was about three million. Today, as Ho Chi Minh City, it may be as much as double that, according to some recent visitors. In the absence of any mass-transit system, thousands of motorbikes clog the city's arteries, choking it with pollution. The Vietnamese Government may want the boat people back, but where will it put them? How will it sustain them? HO CHI MIHN CITY -- In 1979, the Vietnamese Government established the ongoing Orderly Departure Program to counter the exodus of the boat people. Since 1980, more than half a million Vietnamese have been allowed to leave, immigrating mostly to the United States (and to a lesser extent, Australia, Canada and France). For those whose departures have been approved, ID photographs and medical exams are, of course, mandatory. PRECEDING PAGE: Even with the increasing urbanization of Vietnam, some things don't change. HEADLINE: INSIDE QUEENS;
The High-Wire Act of the 109th

BYLINE: By JANE H. LII

 BODY:
Driving past crates full of cuttlefish, water spinach and stringy gourds in downtown Flushing last December of the 109th Precinct, Capt. John McManus barely recognized the bustling area from his childhood memory of rundown streets and abandoned warehouses.
 At age 42, after a brief stint as second-in-command at the 88th Precinct in Brooklyn, he was now in charge of one of the largest precincts in the city, and certainly its most culturally diverse -- a district sweeping over northeast Queens populated on the one hand by wealthy, predominantly white residents who demand prompt response, and on the other by new immigrants, many suspicious of the police. 
 "I didn't know what to expect," Captain McManus said. "But I knew I had to work with them and gain their trust."
 He put the word out -- he was accessible. One by one, the groups came calling with a list of demands:
 Indian immigrants wanted a police escort for the annual pilgrimage of their Hindu holy man, Mahantswami, and his entourage of 75.
 The Pakistanis wanted the captain's direct phone number. They also wanted the police to enforce order when more than 20,000 descend upon parks to celebrate Eid al-Fitr, the Muslim holy day at the end of Ramadan. But they could not give the captain an exact date because the festival is determined by the moon's position the night before.
 The Chinese merchants wanted peddlers off the main thoroughfares. The Koreans demanded more Korean-speaking police officers. And the Chinese and the Koreans also wanted the police to patrol on bicycles.
 It was reminiscent, in many ways, of Captain McManus's days as a schoolteacher, when he was faced with a class full of elementary pupils who each wanted individual attention, but by necessity had to be dealt with as a group.
 "As a schoolteacher, I developed patience," he said. "That and my communications skills have assisted me in my effort to reach out to the community. The most important thing is that I listen. Through listening, I learn about things."
 For example, he learned about the highly delicate process of greeting Asian businessmen. A seemingly simple task like exchanging business cards was a ritual. Never put the card in your back pocket, Captain McManus learned -- accept it gracefully, with two hands, and bow.
 But such lessons in cultural differences were easy compared with the task of garnering trust in the Asian community, which includes Koreans, Chinese, Indians, Pakistanis and Filipinos and makes 40 percent of the population within the precinct's boundaries. Many Asians have traditionally shunned the police out of fear of retaliation or because they do not want to lose face by reporting crime.
 Captain McManus has overcome some barriers by taking such considerations into account, community leader say. "He is a good captain," said Mark Lii, president of the Flushing Chinese Business Association. "He understands what we need."
 But Captain McManus has also found that by satisfying the needs of one group, he runs the risk of offending others. When he closed Geranium Avenue off Kissena Boulevard for several days during the construction of the Muslim Center of New York non-Muslim residents besieged the station house with angry phone calls complaining about the inconvenience.
 Wide economic disparity within the precinct's boundaries also plays a role in the politics of law enforcement. The neighborhoods within the 109th Precinct -- Queensboro Hill, Flushing, College Point, Malba, Whitestone, Beechhurst and Bay Terrace -- each have problems that seldom extend beyond their own borders, necessitating a varied law-enforcement approach.
 In downtown Flushing, concerns include traffic, the proliferation of gypsy cabs and overcrowding. College Point and Malba, a more affluent neighbor, are feuding over a plan to reverse the flow of traffic on 11th Avenue, a residential street that locals say has become a traffic nightmare since motorists began using it as a shortcut to nearby highways.
 Beechhurst has been plagued with illegal fireworks. Cedar Grove has formed its own anti-crime posse.
 In Whitestone, Captian McManus led a July 4 detail to sweep away illegal fireworks being set off under the Throgs Neck Bridge. His action in attacking what had long been a traditional Independence Day activity risked tearing down some of the goodwill he had built up.
 "I knew I was doing the right thing," he said, "but I wasn't sure how the community would receive it."
 Overall, his techniques have drawn praise.
 "The captain is a man of his word," said Regina Colletta, the district manager of Community Board 7 in Flushing. "He follows through with the promises he makes. And he gives equal attention to all the different groups."
 But sometimes, even when all a community's demands have been met, some people are still not satisfied. During a meeting with the Flushing Chinese Business Association, board members complained that the shorts worn by officers on bicycles were not distinctive enough and that people often mistook them for mailmen.
 
BIOGRAPHY
JOHN B. McManus
 
Born Jan. 18, 1953, in Sunnyside, Queens.
Marital Status Single
Education Graduated Holy Family High School, Union City, N.J., 1971; Bachelor of Science degree in education, Manhattan College, 1975; Master of Science degree in communication arts, New York Institute of Technology, 1992.
Career Teacher, grades K-8, St. Joseph's School, Manhattan, 1975-79; Joined New York Police Department, 1979; promoted to sergeant, 1984; to lieutenant, 1989; to captain, 1993. Current assignment: commander, 109th Precinct, Flushing.
