HEADLINE: Where the Mississippi Begins

BYLINE: By CHRISTINE S. COZZENS; CHRISTINE S. COZZENS, who teaches writing at Agnes Scott College in Decatur, Ga., spent her summers in the North Woods as a child.

 BODY:
THE mighty river that changed so many lives and landscapes in the summer of 1993 begins un assumingly enough as a bright little stream rushing out of a tranquil lake fringed with wild rice in central Minnesota. For 150 years the river's humble beginnings -- and the fact that it flows north and east for miles before curving southward -- fooled the region's European and American explorers, who claimed several other lakes as the headwaters before confirming what the Sioux and Ojibway had known for centuries: that the Y-shaped lake later named Itasca (from the Latin words veritas and caput, for truth and head) fed the river that had come to be known as the Great River, Father of Waters -- the Mississippi.
Now enclosed in 32,000 acres of protected land, Itasca State Park remains open to visitors all year, though the food, lodging, and boat and bicycle rental facilities close for the winter. The park draws more than half a million visitors a year to view the headwaters site and touch the waters that flow 2,552 miles to the Gulf of Mexico. In a clearing at the northern tip of Lake Itasca, a simple wooden marker identifies the beginning of this historic waterway: a tiny outlet where the lakes and creeks of the Itasca basin merge in what can -- in compliance with historical and geographical precedent -- be deemed a river. 
 A convenient rocky ford, well-placed boulders for sitting and climbing, and clear, bubbly water racing over a tidy river bed -- all look just as one might expect the source of a great river to look. And with good reason. Originally a "swampy, muddy and dirty sight," in the words of a park superintendent of the early 1930's, the headwaters area was reimagined, in keeping with the park philosophy of the 1930's, by a series of Civilian Conservation Corps projects, which included the digging of a new channel, elimination of swamp areas, mass plantings, and the building of a dam, thus ensuring the continuing popularity of the spot.
Since that time, every day from May to October visitors have hiked to the clearing, determined to ford the Mississippi at this narrowest point or wade in its usually chilly water. For all but the youngest, an air of reverence pervades the experience. On the August morning that I made the journey with my husband and children, we joined an elderly man with a cane, a crowd of Japanese teen-agers, and several mothers with babies strapped on approach the bank and solemnly dip our hands and feet in the waters. "When I touch this water I'm touching the Gulf of Mexico," my 14-year-old son called to me from the opposite bank.
Lying just north of Minnesota's geographical center, between Park Rapids and Bem idji, Itasca State Park stretches across highlands that rise 1,600 feet above sea level in a glacial moraine 20,000 years old. As the glacier receded, it carved out the complex of lakes and stream beds that mystified the explorers seeking an obvious source for the Mississippi.
 EVIDENCE of human habitation in the Itasca region dates to about 6,000 B.C.. We visited a camp and a bison kill site from this period that were excavated in the 1960's. Burial mounds from 500 to 900 years ago near the headwaters site suggest the presence of woodland peoples, who gathered wild rice from canoes using techniques that have changed little over time.
 True wild rice still grows along the edges of Lake Itasca, showing pale yellow-green blossoms in early summer and sheltering a unique ecological community of ducks, turtles, fish and plant life. Though most wild rice is now cultivated and harvested by combines from drained patties, the Ojibway still gather it by hand.
 It was an Ojibway chief, Ozawindib, who guided Henry Schoolcraft, an Indian agent, mineralogist and folklorist, when he set out to find the source of the Mississippi in 1832. Ozawindib provided the Schoolcraft party with maps and led them to the lake, which they named Itasca, and to the northward-flowing stream that forms the outlet. Over the next half century, several adventurers challenged Schoolcraft's claim to have found the source, but in 1889 Jacob Brower, later Itasca State Park's first commissioner, surveyed the area and finally settled the dispute: Schoolcraft's discovery held, and the explorer retained credit for the knowledge his Ojibway guides had shared with him.
Following the footsteps of the Ojibway, Schoolcraft, and Itasca's other discoverers, a web of 24 well-marked biking trails, bike paths, and roads make almost every corner of the park accessible to visitors, who can choose the route best suited to their interests and stamina. After several scouting trips by car, my family and I chose to explore the park on bicycles, which we rented at the Itasca Sports Rental shop on the Main Drive near the Park Headquarters. During the season, boats, canoes, fishing licenses and live bait are also available here, and visitors can try for sunfish, crappies, bass, northern pike, walleye and other northern species.
With stops for sightseeing, a picnic, wildlife watching, and to catch our breath, it took us about four hours to circumnavigate the park, a 15-mile trip. A paved bike path dips into the dense pine forest and breaks out again at overlooks for views of the sparkling lake, following Itasca's eastern and northern arms and the Bear Paw and Pine Ridge Campgrounds, a swimming beach, and the headwaters site. Along the way we stopped to admire the elegant stand of fire-scarred red pines at Preacher's Grove, named for a group of Methodist ministers who camped there in 1916. Apparently the message of peace and love did not always take: the Pioneer Cemetery further along the path provides a final resting place for the victims of a famous feud in the 1890's that began with a dispute over hunting rights and an accidental shooting.
Just to the east of the headwaters site, we stopped to visit one of the park's most interesting log structures, the Itasca Natural History Museum, built in 1922 and now home to an exhibit of indigenous wildlife. From Memorial Day to Labor Day at the adjacent amphitheater and other locations throughout the park, the Park Service plays host to musical performances, storytelling, historical lectures, craft demonstrations, seasonal activities and guided nature walks.
Beyond the headwaters site, on the lake's western side, the bicycle path -- now a road -- reaches around the 2,000-acre Wilderness Sanctuary, wandering far from the shoreline but passing within sight of several smaller lakes. We stopped to explore the forestry demonstration area, view the state's tallest white and red pines (both over 110 feet) and the bison kill site, and climb the observation tower before heading on to the South Itasca Center and cool drinks on the porch at Douglas Lodge.
 Douglas Lodge was built in 1905 from local timber in the rustic style of the early national park era that emphasized natural materials, informal design, and handcrafted details. With its spacious lobby, restaurant, coffee shop, five guest rooms, and the 18-room Nicollet Court Motel next door, the lodge is a busy spot on a summer afternoon. All meals are served on the lovely Douglas Lodge china -- white stoneware with the Great Seal of Minnesota and the showy lady-slipper (the state flower) painted on it in pink and red -- designed in the 1930's and reissued to commemorate the parks' centennial in 1991. The Itasca Nature Store in the Forest Inn Visitor's Center, another historic log building across the way, sells the china and a wealth of items representing the heritage of the North Woods.
By the mid-19th century, Schoolcraft's discovery had stirred interest in the Itasca region. Conservationists bent on preserving the historic headwaters site and wilderness areas flocked to it, as did logging companies that had heard of its legendary trees. Today the immense size of the original forest can be imagined at certain groves of 300-year-old trees in the Wilderness Sanctuary, or at the Old Timer's Cabin, built in 1934, a short walk from Douglas Lodge. The cabin's massive walls of virgin timber are only four logs high.
If Itasca's history could be summarized in one word, it would have to be timber. The establishment of the park by the state legislature in 1891 created a vast domain of forests, lakes and streams, but it was a park on paper, since much of the land still belonged to the logging companies, which resisted the park concept. Thanks to the Minnesota legislature, by 1919 the big logging operations had ended, and the park on paper was a park in fact. Today's park management treats the logging era as an important part of Minnesota's economic and social history; in summer, tours of a working sawmill and demonstrations of logging techniques are among the park's daily programs.
 THOUGH I knew that no physical evidence of the period of exploration remained, I was happy to discover that summer visitors can follow Schoolcraft and Ozawindib's voyage across the lake to the headwaters aboard the Chester Charles, a sightseeing boat that docks near Douglas Lodge, where the explorers saw the lake for the first time.
We sat on the upper deck for the 90-minute trip. As the boat glided along the glassy surface, the guide explained the lake's natural and human history, often cutting the engine to let the boat drift silently closer to a deer that had paused in a clearing, a beaver lodge, a bald eagle perched on a dead branch high above the water's edge, or a family of loons.
As we approached the island where the Schoolcraft party made camp before following Ozawindib's directions to the headwaters, I remembered the words of the explorer, moved by the experience of "reaching the actual source of a stream so celebrated as the Mississippi." He wrote of the beauty of the "transparent and bright" lake, "lying among hills . . . surmounted with pines, which fringe the distant horizon and form an agreeable contrast with the greener foliage of its immediate shores." Happily, those words still describe Lake Itasca and the park surrounding its shores.
 
Discovering the headwaters
 
Getting There
 Itasca State Park is about a five-hour drive from Minneapolis-St. Paul via Interstate 94 West; at St. Cloud take Highway 10 to the north and east until Highway 71 North, which goes direct to the park.
 
Information
 The park information number is (218) 266-3654.
 A Minnesota annual vehicle permit (sold at all state parks) for $18 ($12 for seniors) allows unlimited vehicle entry to all of the state's 60 parks. Daily permits are $4.
The park distributes maps, guides, and free information about programs. The Itasca Guide Book, $5.95, contains a great deal of information about the park, including articles about its history and natural resources, detailed trail and park maps, and is updated annually. It is available at both gift shops (near Douglas Lodge and at the head waters), park headquarters, and Douglas Lodge. A cassette tape driving tour of the entire park and one for Wilderness Drive can also be bought at the gift shops for $8.75 each.
 
Lodging and Eating
For accommodations in the park -- at Douglas Lodge, Bear Paw Cabins, the Clubhouse, the Nicollet Court Motel, or at one of several well-appointed campsites (the site nearest the lake, Bear Paw Campsite, is especially comfortable): (800) 246-2267. Some campsites are available on a first-come, first-served basis. Group sites are available at Elk Lake and Squaw Lake. The Mississippi Headwaters A.Y.H. Hostel is in a restored log building from the 1920's. The price is $12 a person per night for members; group rates are cheaper. For hostel information call (218) 266-3415 or write to A.Y.H. Hostel, Itasca State Park, HC 05 Box 5A, Lake Itasca, Minn. 56460. Reservations are necessary at all park accommodations.
Douglas Lodge's pleasant dining room looking out into the woods is open from the end of May to the beginning of October and serves meals and snacks from 8 A.M. to 8 P.M. Picnic lunches are also available. A house specialty, Wild Rice Hot Dish, costs about $6 at lunch. Evening meals are in the $10 range.
A variety of accommodations and restaurants may be found in or near the towns of Bemidji (call the Visitors and Convention Bureau at 800-292-2223), Park Rapids (call the Chamber of Commerce at 800-247-0054), or Cass Lake (800-356-8615).
 
Activities
Between Memorial Day and Labor Day, the park offers activities for all ages and interests. Each summer it publishes a newsletter, listing times and places for its regular and special events.
From early May to early October, bicycles, boats and canoes can be rented seven days a week at Itasca Sports Rental on the Main Park Drive (218-266-3654). Prices range from $2.50 to $7 (for a tandem) an hour and from $15 to $40 a day. Canoes and rowboats cost $3.50 for the first hour, $1.50 for each additional hour, or $12 a day. A boat with a motor and gas costs $9 for two hours plus $3 for each additional hour, or $30 a day. Life jackets are provided.
During the summer, the Chester Charles operates 90-minute guided tours of the lake daily at 11 A.M., 1 P.M. and 3 P.M. and an evening tour Mondays, Wednesdays and Thursdays at 7 (minimum 10 passengers). Tickets are $6.50 a person and can be purchased on board the boat about five minutes before departure.
Though the food and hotel facilities are closed during the winter, the park remains open for cross-country skiing, snow or rustic camping, and hiking. Snowmobiles are allowed in certain areas.

HEADLINE: Honors Studies Shine at Community College

BYLINE: By SHARON W. LINSKER 

 BODY:
GINO SCALABRINI, 21, planned to major in accounting when he entered Westchester Community College in 1992, but he became fascinated with political science after taking courses in the honors program. He has just completed his junior year at Dartmouth College in Hanover, N.H., where he is majoring in government.
Each year about 100 students take part in the honors program at Westchester Community College in Valhalla. Many of them credit that program with introducing them to new subjects and stimulating their interest in college-level work. 
 Being in the program also helps students get into four-year schools and earn scholarships. Another drawing point is the tuition, the lowest in the county.
"Students are choosing Westchester Community for both academic and financial reasons," said Abby J. Hirsch, director of guidance for the Somers school system. "There has been a tremendous change in attitude toward W.C.C. over the last couple of years, especially because some families have been hard hit economically."
In 1994, 20 percent of Somers High School's 160 graduates went to Westchester Community, and several could have qualified for four-year schools.
Two typical honors program courses are the Art of the Novel and Life: Origins and Evolution. Enrollment in each class is limited to 18. Students take part in symposiums that explore topics in greater depth than in regular courses, hear guest speakers and develop close relationships with professors who offer academic guidance and share similar interests with the students.
The honors program is headed by Carol Klein, associate professor in the chemistry department, and Dr. Linda Ching Sledge, assistant professor in the English department. Both women hold the Joseph and Sophia Abeles chair in the honors program, sponsored since 1994 by the Abeles family of Mount Pleasant.
Until now, speakers have addressed individual honors program classes. For example, the Westchester residents Benjamin Cheever, a novelist, and Aranka Siegel, a young author who lectures on the Holocaust, visited during the last academic year. But financing from the Abeles chair will enable the directors to run seminars next year that will be open to the public. The first such meeting will take place in the spring, and the topic will be "Can Justice Be Done in the United States?"
Honors participants include the same mix of traditional and nontraditional students found throughout the college. There are teen-agers just out of high school, women with children, students from foreign countries and adults with diverse work experience. During the last academic year, honors program students included a former dancer, an auto mechanic and a police officer.
Among those attracted to Westchester Community College are young men and women who cannot afford the high cost of four-year colleges. Tuition at the community college this fall will be $2,150 for two full-time semesters for residents and $90 a credit part time. In contrast, Mr. Scalabrini will pay about $27,000 for room, board and tuition during his senior year at Dartmouth.
Westchester Community College scholarships also help ease the cost of going to college. In the honors program, Nancy Jo Abeles Honors Scholarships are awarded for outstanding academic performance.
April Landshof, 24, of Katonah, chose the community college over a four-year school because of the saving it offered. "I didn't want to waste money since I wasn't sure what I wanted to do," Ms. Landshof said. She is the oldest of three children and is putting herself through college by working while attending classes.
At the college, Ms. Landshof took all the drama courses available and psychobiology, mathematical excursions: problem solving, and other honors classes. In the fall, she will transfer to New York University to study drama education.
Honors program students frequently talk about the involvement and innovative approach of their teachers and about the strong bond among classmates. James Holdridge, 43, a Mahopac resident who was a police officer for 21 years, said that while he had always read on his own he had not discussed literature with others until coming to Westchester Community College in 1993.
Now Mr. Holdridge is getting straight A's and was twice a finalist at the Beacon Conference for Student Scholars at Two Year Colleges, held this year at Dutchess Community College in Poughkeepsie. In 1995, 7 of the 44 finalists from 26 colleges were from Westchester Community College, and 6 of the community college's participants were honors program students.
When Mr. Scalabrini graduated from high school, his composite Scholastic Assessment Test scores totaled nearly 1,200, but his grade point average was low. Accepted at only a few expensive private colleges and one New York State four-year school, he decided to attend Westchester Community College.
There, he found the honors program a "place for serious students to work with other students," he said.
"I felt really well prepared when I began my junior year at Dartmouth," he added. And Dartmouth credited all his course work from Westchester Community College.
Mr. Scalabrini was also accepted by the University of Virginia, the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill, Yale University, Boston College and the State University of New York.
Each year, several institutions award scholarships to Westchester Community College students, and the University of Rochester gives a full scholarship to an honors program graduate. Westchester Community College has also signed articulation agreements with 22 four-year colleges, automatically granting two full years of credits to entering juniors.
Dr. Joseph N. Hankin, president of Westchester Community College, said: "Our students have gotten into very strong schools that they couldn't have gotten into initially because their grades weren't high enough. And what's more, they save two years of higher tuition."
At Westchester Community College, students earn two-year associate degrees, participate in one-year certificate programs in areas like practical nursing and electronics, or take noncredit courses. Enrollment continues through the end of August. For further information, call the Office of Admissions at 785-6735.

HEADLINE: A LA CARTE;
A Longshot Wins Diners' Top Zagat Rating

BYLINE: By RICHARD JAY SCHOLEM 

 BODY:
A LONGSHOT has run away with top food ratings in the first stand-alone Zagat survey of Long Island. Piccolo, a popular Northern Italian spot in Huntington, received the highest ranking in a survey of more than 2,000 diners who filled out questionnaires after they had visited a total of 530 restaurants. They rated Mirabelle of St. James second and the Mill River Inn of Oyster Bay third.
Piccolo, which received a rating of "24" for its food in the 1995 Zagat Tri-State survey, jumped to 27 out of a possible 30. The storefront restaurant in the Southdown shopping center was the front-runner in the Italian and Continental categories, as well as No. 1 among Suffolk restaurants. 
 When the owner, Dino Philippis, was told about the voting, he was surprised. That is understandable. Piccolo has always had a fiercely loyal, largely Huntington-based following. But it had never achieved ratings that put it in the category of Mirabelle, the Mill River Inn, Panama Hattie's, Starr Boggs and Navona, all of which have won consistent acclaim.
Piccolo's emergence shows that a well-established restaurant with a devoted clientele in an affluent area where people dine out frequently and let their preferences be known can beat out better-known competitors.
The publisher, Tim Zagat, indicated that although Piccolo had been mentioned in 74 questionnaires and that Mirabelle was on 341, the food at the Huntington restaurant had been very highly praised by almost all who rated it. The 140-page Survey of Long Island, which costs $9.95, will be available the middle of next month. The guide with a green cover is the first of four separate suburban books that replace the regional survey. Separating Long Island, New Jersey, Connecticut and lower New York State is a result of the growing importance and sophistication of the restaurants in the areas, a spokesman for the publisher said.
Zagat voters registered preferences on decor and service, as well as food. The Pine Island Grill in Bayville, East Hampton Point and the Ram's Head Inn on Shelter Island placed first, second and third in decor, and Mirabelle, the Mill River Inn and da Ugo of Rockville Centre were given the nods for service. Peter Luger of Great Neck, Mirabelle and the Mill River Inn proved to be the most popular choices.
Diners who seek outstanding value will go to the listings of best buys, which rank Maureen's Country Kitchen in Smithtown, the Tortilla Grills in Huntington and Merrick and Fish on the Run in Huntington as "Top Bangs for the Buck."
Panama Hattie's made a dramatic jump from being unrated to a 26, making it the most improved spot. The Grill Room in Hauppauge, also with a 26, was the No. 1 newcomer. Riverbay in Williston Park had the highest marks for a seafood restaurant.
The Long Island survey recorded more responses from diners than all others except the New York City, Los Angeles and Washington editions.
 
On the Move
 La Viola, popular Italian family-style restaurants in Cedarhurst and Syossett, are planning to open a branch in Manhattan. The restaurant in Cedarhurst will offer monthly opera dinners in the fall. It is also selling elaborate gift baskets of specialties and has already introduced a summer salad menu.
An agreement to acquire a site on West 44th Street between Eighth and Ninth Avenues is close to completion, the restaurant says.
The gift baskets, from $20 to $50 and available only in Cedarhurst, include La Viola's Bolognese, marinara, filetto di pomodoro, vodka and clam sauces, as well as De Cecco pastas. Herb-flavored oils and vinegars, pickled Portobello mushrooms, eggplant and string beans, dip and salad dressings, sun-dried tomatoes and breads from the wood-burning oven are also among the items.
The $40 opera dinners, on the first Thursday of each month in Cedarhurst, will begin on Sept. 7 with selections from "La Traviata," "Aida," "Il Travatore" and "Adriana Le Couvreur." Diners who pay for five nights of opera in a year will receive a sixth opera dinner free. The meal that accompanies the music consists of salad, pasta, a veal, chicken or fish entree, dessert and coffee.
Among the summer salads are avocado, endive and radicchio, red beets and new potatoes, tuna and borlotti beans and arugula and sauteed shitake mushrooms with herbs.
La Viola is at 499 Chestnut Street in Cedarhurst (569-6020) and 41 Jackson Avenue (364-8383) in Syosset.
 
New Menus and Meals
 Pomodorino, Pomodoro's lower-priced kid brother, at 33-65 Hillside Avenue in New Hyde Park (739-1318), has a new menu that offers any of 11 pastas along with salad and a cannoli for $7.95. The pastas include chicken raviolini in a porcini mushroom sauce, penne alla vodka, meat lasagna, rigatoni with sausage and mushrooms in a light tomato sauce and spaghetti with chicken, broccoli, olive oil and garlic.
The more-economical menu lists seven individual pizzas for $5.50 each. An antipasto table of grilled vegetables, cured meats and cheeses, salads and hors d'oeuvres is $6.95 for one and $11.95 for two.
Georgia's German and American Restaurant at 722 South Wellwood Avenue in Lindenhurst (957-8484) is featuring German dinner specials for $7.95 on Mondays, Tuesdays and Wednesdays. Monday is fresh ham and roast beef, the Tuesday special is sauerbraten, and Wednesday is schnitzel night.
Palmer Vineyards in Aquebogue (329-3300), in cooperation with the Mediterranean Manor, is holding two evenings of music, dancing and dining in a tent. Next Saturday, local foods will be featured on a bountiful Long Island grill, and on Aug. 26 a Caribbean buffet and reggae music are scheduled. The dinner, dancing and a glass of wine costs $48.
The Honest Diner on Montauk Highway in Amagansett (267-3535) is serving an early bird special from 5 to 6:30 P.M. every day. The $9.95 dinner consists of the chef's selection entree and a soup or salad. New on the regular dinner menu is a combination of a lobster roll, french fries and cole slaw for $10.95.
 
Chefs' Corner
 Frank Falcinelli is the new chef at the Pine Island Grill at the Crescent Beach Club in Bayville (628-3000). Mr. Falcinelli, a graduate of the Culinary Institute of America, also trained in Auch and Toulouse, France, and David Burke and Dan Budd at the River Cafe in Brooklyn. After a stint with Charles Palmer as a chef saucier at Aureole in Manhattan, he opened his own restaurant, Culinary Renaissance, a highly rated spot in Metuchen, N.J.
Ella Rocca, executive chef at Navonna in Great Neck, was among the 120 North Americans recently initiated into Chefs 2000, an international organization based in San Francisco. Ms. Rocca, one of three women honored, was cited for "providing culinary leadership into the 21st century." The members of Chefs 2000 will meet in Washington in the year 2000 to discuss culinary trends in the 21st century. Ms. Rocca also appeared at the Jacob K. Javits Convention Center in Manhattan recently for a specialty food show. The Berreta Rice Company invited her to cook dishes that she offers at Navonna.
Sam Gottlieb, the son of the owner of the Grand Cafe, Steve Gottlieb, is now a chef at the restaurant at 66 Newtown Lane in East Hampton (324-9207). Mr. Gottlieb most recently worked for the executive chef at Della Femina's, Pat Trama.
 
Closings
 Dar Tiffany, 44 Glen Cove Road, Greenvale, a longtime traditional American steakhouse.

PUB-DATE: July 30, 1995

SECTION: Section 2; Page 5; Column 1; Arts and Leisure Desk 

LENGTH: 2264 words

HEADLINE: THEATER;
Curtain Going Up on a Rescued Summer Theater

BYLINE: By PATRICIA VOLK 

 BODY:
ADARK THEATER IN A SMALL town is like a corpse in the living room. It's dead and it's there. For many of its 40 years, the Sharon Playhouse in the northwest corner of Connecticut limped along, cherished but in debt, a primer for How Not to Run a Summer Theater. Five years ago, gasping for air, the big barn finally went belly up.
"It was your typical summer theater experience," says the actress Jane Curtin, who lives in Sharon. "You know, holes in the roof. They would do 'Godspell.' When it closed, you'd drive by and it was very sad." 
 Weeds grew. Paint sloughed. Somebody toyed with rezoning the property for a shopping village cum restaurant-theater. An auctioneer and an auto body shop nosed around.
Then suddenly, last September, a group of theater people, many involved in the Broadway musical "The Phantom of the Opera," closed on the property, west of Main Street on Route 343, a mile from the New York border. Scaffolding appeared. By May, the red barn with the lopped off peak was getting two enormous air-conditioning towers on either side. By June, it looked like Chartres with a buzz cut. The Sharon Stage was born.
Sharon, Conn., is a time warp. Ninety miles from Broadway, the police blotter reads: "Following too close. Mailbox struck. Bricks stolen." A hair dye goes for $25 at Dor-Ray Coiffures and a rose-breasted grosbeak sighting is front-page news. Houses so lovely you have to pull over and recover date from 1739 and start at $80,000. (Jasper Johns recently bought one for $3.9 million.) The latest census lists "2,901 whites, 12 blacks, 5 American Indians, 9 Asians, 20 Hispanics and 1 other," though she may have moved.
For a long time, Sharon supported the playhouse. Literally. In 1972, to keep it going, 80 shares were sold for $1,000 each. Two local residents, Marilyn Reagan and Nelson Slater, co-signed personal loans. It had such a loyal following that when a fire broke out during a matinee, the audience refused to leave. Once, a storm doused the lights during "Gypsy" and someone drove a Camaro into the lobby, jacked it up and flipped on the brights.
People still throng to nearby Tanglewood, the Berkshire Theater Festival and Jacob's Pillow. So why did they stop coming to Sharon?
"There's a lot of ill will there," says Gail Mirabile, a town librarian. "It was badly managed. They were always losing money. There were always people who thought they were artists. Talk to Bill Landis."
Mr. Landis says he and his wife, Liz, became producers by default. "It was never a money-making proposition," says the former president of the Sharon Creative Arts Foundation. "Some very good people came here. Rene Auberjonois. Olympia Dukakis. Jane Alexander. The only year I think the theater made any money was 1979. We netted a $10,000 profit."
Mr. Landis and his wife tried opening and closing with big musicals to finance the dramas in between. They tried using local performers to generate community support and to save on Equity salaries. But in 1981, the trustees wanted more. "They had visions of grandeur: rubbing elbows with stars," says Mr. Landis, who once played Polonius to Dirk Bogarde's Hamlet. "They forgot it was a little theater. They were bankers and brokers."
Nelson and Brenda Slater sit in the living room of their woodland aerie in nearby Taconic, Conn., with their springer spaniels Tiffany and Chester. Stage right of a wall-size stone fireplace is a portrait of Samuel Slater, Nelson Slater's great-great-grandfather, who operated the first successful cotton mill in America.
"After Marilyn Reagan and I co-signed some notes, the bank called the loan," says Mr. Slater. "But to some extent we recouped our losses when the playhouse was recently sold. The people who bought it agreed to pay back part of what we paid at so much a month. We have an agreement with them that if they don't, we get the whole thing back. Not that we're anxious to do that.
"The kicker," Mr. Slater adds, "is that there are no good restaurants in Sharon. And let's face it. Part of summer theater is having a restaurant nearby that people can go to and have dinner before the play."
The selling price was $200,000 for 3.5 acres, the big stage, an art gallery with a small stage, a costume barn, a dorm for interns and an office building. Eleven principals chipped in $5,000 to $38,000 each.
Plays started pouring in. "We looked at many possibilities for our opening season, but there were only 15 real contenders," says Tim McHenry, the board president of the new Sharon Stage. Three productions were planned, from June 20 through Sept. 3, for three weeks each.
"Originally," says Michael Gill, the managing director at Sharon Stage and the company manager for "Phantom," "we were going to open with the world premiere of 'The Woman Who Laughed' by Joyce Carol Oates, go to 'The Belle of Amherst' with a major name, then do 'The Robber Bridegroom.' Then an independent producer called and said, 'I just wanted to let you know that Estelle Parsons and Carol Kane have already committed to doing 'Twice Removed.' And I thought, well, if they believe in it and they're committed, we better read this."
The inaugural season evolved into "Twice Removed," a new comedy by Mark Rosin and Barry Glasser; a revival of "The Robber Bridegroom," a musical based on the Eudora Welty novella (which runs through next Sunday); and the premiere of Ms. Oates's tragi-comedy about a New York teacher in a Midwest school. It opens on Aug. 15 and closes on Aug. 27 -- a week early. ("It's the economics of putting on new plays," says Mr. McHenry.)
 
Sunday, May 28: Mildew and Rogaine
 Sweat equity is when you use your back instead of your wallet. It's three and a half weeks before opening night and Jack Gaughan, secretary-treasurer of Sharon Stage and the orchestra conductor of "Phantom," is bolting seats to the concrete floor. Mr. McHenry appears to be hoeing. Lee Austin, a Sharon Stage board member and former "Phantom" wardrobe supervisor, has sifted through 40 years of junk in the costume room. What wasn't mildewed is stored in garbage bags labeled Men's Suit Coats, Indian Headdresses, Curtains Red . . . Another board member, Merle Frimark, a Broadway publicist who represents "Phantom," is checking on the grass she planted. It looks like a bald head one month post-Rogaine. She has cleaned the old prop room. "We've come a long way," Ms. Frimark says. "It doesn't smell anymore."
 
Sunday, June 4: Passing the (Straw) Hat
 It's six days before the benefit that opens the season. The parking lot is loaded with tractors, Dumpsters and air-conditioning trucks. Paint cans and rolls of carpet fill the lobby. A not-for-profit theater too young to get grants depends on donations.
The signs were a gift from King Display. Serino Coyne, the Broadway advertising agency, designed the logo. Francis Gill, Michael's father, has been working as a contractor for six weeks nonstop. The 380 deep-blue theater seats are a present from the Jujamcyn company's Virginia Theater on Broadway. They are about half in, but not alphabetically: M,H,C,K,P,N,B,L,A,H,L and F. For the first time, there is a pit, and the theater will be air-conditioned.
The construction has cost $120,000, but you can still see daylight through the barn walls.
 
Saturday, June 10: Kyra, Kevin and Sam
 A workman says one air-conditioner will definitely work for tonight's benefit, but he's worried about how loud it is. In the field behind the theater, a donkey brays. Ms. Frimark seems pleased with local support: "Kyra Sedgwick and Kevin Bacon want to do something. Sam Waterston said, 'I'll help you whenever I can.' " (The names of homeowners in Litchfield County often appear in bold: Streep, Blass, Kissinger.)
Mr. Gill is hauling trash.
"What do you have to do before 7 P.M.?" someone asks.
"Everything," he grins.
At 7:40 the parking lot is jammed. It's the big night, Sharon's first look at its stage. The chinks in the walls have been sealed from the outside. An intern is cutting limes. When the lights go up, six local actors are sitting on stools. Jill Clayburgh, who lives in Sharon, introduces them. Ethel Merman's voice singing "There's No Business Like Show Business" vibrates the rafters. Jane Curtin reads Dorothy Parker's review of an A. A. Milne play. The audience howls.
"Is there a doctor in the house?" Mr. McHenry asks, as the lights go up. Speaking of the Sharon Stage, he adds: "A new baby has just been delivered!"
 
Wednesday, June 14: The Cash Flow Isn't
 Six days before the official opening night, the theater's board of directors meets for the 49th time in the Broadway offices of Frimark & Thibodeau Associates.
Details are gone over for "Twice Removed." Lucie Arnaz has committed to "The Woman Who Laughed." The provisional liquor license still hasn't come through, but a mailing list with 13,000 names on it is back from computer hell. The playbill will cost $12,898. Only $8,000 in ads has been raised.
"Our cash flow right now is tight," says Mr. Gill, who has a budget of $600,000 per year. Ticket sales will bring in 60 percent (prices range from $12 to $25). For the rest, he's counting on fund-raisers, a general donation campaign, Friends of Sharon Stage and a major benefit to be held Sept. 10 at the Majestic Theater on Broadway (the home of "Phantom").
Three days before opening night, a frayed power line outside the theater sets fire to a tree. The electricity shorts out for five hours and the phones go dead.
Matthew Fahrner, formerly a chef at the West Street Grill in Litchfield, takes over the West Main Cafe. For the first time, Sharon will have nori-wrapped tuna and garlic smashed potatoes.
 
Tuesday, June 20: Fire Watches Ready
 Opening day. At 2 A.M., Ms. Frimark is painting the ladies' room. By 5 P.M. geraniums and petunias line the walk. A new flagstone path went in an hour before, laid by Bill Marshall, a local composer and astrologer. There's a rubber plant with a green bow in the lobby. The seats are now alphabetical. On stage, the actors are still rehearsing. No one is going to wait up for reviews. The closest thing the town has to Sardi's is the Nutmeg Pantry, and true to Sharon Stage's view of itself as a safe haven for works-in-progress, New York critics have not been invited. Besides, two of the key papers are weeklies.
By 6:40 P.M. the parking lot is beginning to fill up. Three fire watches are in place. A fire watch is a person who stands by with a fire extinguisher. During a performance, that is the only job the fire watch has. Stan MacMillan, Sharon's building inspector and fire marshal, will visit to make sure the watches are where they're supposed to be. It's 90 degrees outside. The air-conditioner sounds like a B-52.
At 7:15, with a full house, the curtain rises on the comedy "Twice Removed." There is a collective gasp, followed by a collective "Ooooo!" In one stroke, the Sharon Stage's intentions are clear: This is not summer stock. This is not Mickey Rooney grabbing Judy Garland's hand and shouting "Come on, kids! Let's put on a show!" This set is Broadway caliber, a rotting Victorian in Poughkeepsie with French doors, a solarium and a staircase, designed by Paul Wonsek. Ms. Parsons, Ms. Kane and Deborah Rush perform so smoothly, no one would guess that two hours before they were learning a new scene and dropping two old ones. But 10 minutes into the first act, the air-conditioner goes off. You can't hear with it on. People start fanning themselves with the playbills. Drops of sweat hang from the actors' chins.
Later, at the reception, playgoers wearing everything from shower thongs to cowboy hats study photos of the original opening night in 1955 when playgoers wore mink stoles and dinner jackets. A harpist plucks "Greensleeves." There is trout mousse and champagne.
 
Monday, June 26: The Critics Speak
 The air-conditioning problem has been fixed. The reviews are in. The Litchfield County Times: "giddy, fizzy, wacky." The Lakeville Journal: "loopy and funny." The Berkshire Courier: "funny and professional." The Poughkeepsie Journal: "a must-see." Only The Hartford Courant blasts the play, but four out of five beats Babe Ruth's batting average. The theater is a hit. Sharon is optimistic, New England-style:
Jane Curtin: "The hardest part they're going to have is getting people to come up. It's an unknown commodity. But once they get over that hurdle, it'll be fine."
Nelson Slater: "There isn't any Frank Rich who will review their plays and have everyone read his review. But if the word of mouth is good, everyone will come."
Bill Landis: "It's wonderful to see the theater in the hands of professionals. I think they'll be successful. But I assume they'll lose money. You can't make money in that theater."
Mr. Gill explains: "A profit would mean you're not spending enough on your mission." He envisions Sharon Stage as a cultural center, with readings, workshops and children's shows. "Our goal is to break even."
 
Sunday, July 2: Invitations to a Play
 Tim McHenry drops Lee Austin off at the Metro North station in Dover Plains, N.Y., for the ride back to his day job at the soap opera "Another World." Mr. McHenry returns to his Ford Explorer but doesn't open the door. Instead, he reaches in and extracts a pile of Sharon Stage fliers.
"See Estelle Parsons in 'Twice Removed' ! See Estelle Parsons in 'Twice Removed' ! " He hands the crisp blue and yellow leaflets to people in their cars. They take them, curious and smiling. That night, the parking lot is full.

SECTION: Section 7; Page 10; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 2096 words

HEADLINE: Did We Need to Drop It?

BYLINE: By Michael R. Beschloss; Michael R. Beschloss is the author, most recently, of "The Crisis Years: Kennedy and Khrushchev, 1960-1963."

 BODY:

THE DECISION TO USE
THE ATOMIC BOMB
And the Architecture of an American Myth.
By Gar Alperovitz.
With the assistance of Sanho Tree, Edward Rouse Winstead, Kathryn C. Morris, David J. Williams, Leo C. Maley 3d, Thad Williamson and Miranda Grieder.
847 pp. New York: Alfred A. Knopf. $32.50.
 
CODE-NAME DOWNFALL
The Secret Plan to Invade Japan --
And Why Truman Dropped the Bomb.
By Thomas B. Allen and Norman Polmar.
Illustrated. 352 pp. New York:
Simon & Schuster. $25. 
 FOR 20 years after Harry Truman ordered the atomic bomb dropped on Japan in August 1945, most American scholars and citizens subscribed to the original, official version of the story: the President had acted to avert a horrendous invasion of Japan that could have cost 200,000 to 500,000 American lives. Then a young political economist named Gar Alperovitz published a book of ferocious revisionism, "Atomic Diplomacy: Hiroshima and Potsdam" (1965). While acknowledging the paucity of evidence available at the time, he argued that dropping the atomic bomb "was not needed to end the war or to save lives" but was Truman's means of sending a chastening message to the Soviet Union.
Now, in "The Decision to Use the Atomic Bomb," Mr. Alperovitz, who is the president of the National Center for Economic Alternatives, writes that "oversimplified versions of my argument (together with some obvious graduate-student errors) were pounced upon by critics who could not abide criticism of the Hiroshima decision." Benefiting from documentary discoveries of the past 30 years and the less fractious post-cold war atmosphere, he has produced a more ambitious and far-reaching work. As the author notes, his earlier book focused on "how the bomb influenced diplomacy." With the advantage of greater hindsight and documentation, this volume seeks to deal more comprehensively with the decision to drop the bomb and to suggest why the public clings so tenaciously to the original explanation of why Truman gave the order. With piquant irony, he has chosen the same title that Truman's first Secretary of War, Henry L. Stimson, used in a famous 1947 essay in Harper's that did much to establish the original version of the story in the public mind. Mr. Alperovitz has lost none of his instinct for provocative judgments about one of the century's paramount historical controversies. He has written what will almost certainly serve as a bible for the next generation of revisionist scholars, a book that is elegantly documented (with the aid of seven research collaborators) and intensely argued.
Fifty years after Hiroshima and Nagasaki, Mr. Alperovitz declares that a final answer to why the atomic bomb was used is "neither essential nor possible." He continues with a more debatable premise: "What is important is whether, when the bomb was used, the President and his top advisers understood that it was not required to avoid a long and costly invasion, as they later claimed and as most Americans still believe." More orthodox historical critiques of the atomic bomb decision sometimes question the estimates of casualties to be expected from a full-scale invasion of Japan. The argument goes that if these appraisals were inflated, the President had less business justifying the horror of the bomb as a way of saving lives. This debate is less interesting to Mr. Alperovitz. He insists that without use of the bomb, Japan might still have been made to surrender before the first American landing on the island of Kyushu, planned for November 1945. He notes that many American military leaders then and later felt that using atomic weapons against Japan was unnecessary.
But, Mr. Alperovitz argues, Truman and his Secretary of State, James F. Byrnes, were struck by the notion that ending World War II without dropping the atomic bomb would not have brought added strength to American diplomacy against the Soviet Union in Europe. More than in the earlier book, Byrnes is the villain of this piece. Mr. Alperovitz insists that a decision not to drop the bomb could actually have bolstered American diplomatic objectives in Asia -- for example, by helping to create the atmosphere for a more harmonious postwar American-Soviet relationship. He criticizes Truman for failing to issue a more explicit warning to Japan about the bomb and for attacking Hiroshima rather than a nonurban target, as his Army Chief of Staff, Gen. George C. Marshall, had suggested.
As evidence of the link between the bomb decision and diplomacy toward Moscow, Mr. Alperovitz points to Truman's postponement of his Potsdam meeting with Stalin and Churchill until July 1945, when the new weapon would have been tested. At Potsdam, after hearing about the first successful detonation in New Mexico, Truman turned suddenly more truculent. According to Stimson, Churchill marveled that the President "was a changed man. He told the Russians just where they got on and off and generally bossed the whole meeting." Truman confided to his crony and reparations negotiator Edwin Pauley that the bomb "would keep the Russians straight." Mr. Alperovitz argues that "the U.S. feeling of cheerfulness rather than frustration" over differences with the Soviets at Potsdam "makes little sense unless one realizes that top policy makers were thinking ahead to the time when the force of the new weapon would be displayed."
But how might Truman, if he were disinclined to use the bomb, have ended the war without the large number of casualties required, by any estimate, for the invasion of Japan? Mr. Alperovitz says that the President could have shown himself a lot more eager to welcome the Soviets into the Asian conflict. Franklin D. Roosevelt, for example, had wanted Stalin to help pin the Japanese down on the Chinese mainland, making it harder for them to reinforce their home armies when the Americans invaded.
Mr. Alperovitz suggests that on the issue of Soviet participation in the Japanese war, Truman zigged and zagged after taking office in April 1945. In mid-June, American officials like General Marshall were arguing that a Soviet war declaration might "prove to be the decisive blow to force a Japanese surrender." But at Potsdam, Mr. Alperovitz writes, Truman sought to delay a Soviet war declaration: although it might have precluded the use of the bomb on Japan, it would have given Stalin a large foothold in east Asia. Mr. Alperovitz says that the timing of the Hiroshima bombing -- Aug. 6, 1945 -- was no accident. Two days later the Soviet Union declared war on the Japanese and then crossed the border into Manchuria.
 MR. ALPEROVITZ offers another alternative for ending the war without using the bomb: relaxing the unconditional surrender demand issued by Roosevelt in 1943 at Casablanca. He suggests that the President might have provided assurances that if Tokyo surrendered, the Japanese Emperor, Hirohito, would be permitted to retain his throne. This idea indeed found strong support among Truman's advisers. Stimson proposed that Truman allow the Japanese "a constitutional monarchy under the present dynasty if it be shown to the complete satisfaction of the world that such a government will never again conspire to aggression." Mr. Alperovitz notes that in mid-August, after the bombs had been dropped and the Russians had entered the conflict, Truman and Byrnes were willing to provide assurances about the Emperor. Doesn't the fact that these weren't provided earlier, when they might have helped end the war, indicate an eagerness to drop the bomb?
Mr. Alperovitz gives less weight than other scholars to the arguments against such an offer. As the Stanford historian Barton Bernstein has recently noted in the journal Diplomatic History, the Tokyo regime of mid-summer 1945 was badly split over what kind of American peace offer, if any, to accept. At that point, granting a concession on the Emperor's role could have drawn the United States into extended bargaining with the Japanese leaders. Haggling with a regime that Roosevelt and Truman had denounced as criminal, that had attacked Pearl Harbor and that had committed well-publicized atrocities was the kind of thing the unconditional surrender doctrine had been drafted to avoid. Not irrationally, Truman told Churchill that he did not think the Japanese had "any military honor after Pearl Harbor." (And there is also the possibility that ambiguity over Hirohito's role might have impeded America's ability to occupy the country and reform the political system from the ground up.)
Mr. Alperovitz devotes considerable space to showing how Stimson's article in Harper's, misleading official memoirs and the American Government's refusal over the years to release certain classified documents helped enshrine the original explanation of the atomic bomb decision. Yet, as energetically as he argues his case, he is unlikely to convert those who do not believe that finding an alternative to the atomic bomb should have been an overarching priority for Truman in the summer of 1945.
Moreover, Mr. Alperovitz's new volume lacks what the Harvard historian Charles S. Maier has called the "shock value" of his earlier one. One reason is that we are more skeptical about the motives of our leaders and the origins of the cold war than we were in 1965. But another is the degree to which Mr. Alperovitz's views have pushed other scholars to re-examine their assumptions about Hiroshima and Nagasaki.
 IN "Code-Name Downfall," Thomas B. Allen and Norman Polmar, the authors of "CNN: War in the Gulf" and "Rickover: Controversy and Genius," reveal new sidelights on the planning to invade Japan. Amid some purple prose (the book begins, "The United States was plunged into despair on Sunday, Dec. 7, 1941," and later says, "MacArthur's life and career were a parade of superlatives"), they show how the fall of Okinawa in July 1945 became the prelude to the planned landing by seven Army and three Marine divisions on Kyushu and the 17-division landing on the main Japanese island of Honshu, the latter action scheduled for March 1946. They describe the fictitious attacks and feints devised to deceive the foe, and the possible American use of poison gas, anthrax germs and atomic weapons during the invasion. Told of Hiroshima, one American planner said he wanted "six of these things" for the Kyushu landing. Ignorant of the danger of radiation to his own troops, General Marshall pondered using atomic bombs on Kyushu before the Americans came ashore.
Mr. Allen and Mr. Polmar explain that before the atomic bombs were dropped the Pentagon expected to be faced with Japanese resistance until November 1946. Grimly recalling a March 1945 bombing attack on the Japanese capital, General Marshall said, "We had 100,000 people killed in Tokyo in one night, and it had seemingly no effect whatsoever."
The authors also describe Truman's effort to assess the possible casualties that would result from a full-scale invasion. Whereas Mr. Alperovitz laments Truman's manipulation of casualty estimates after his retirement (in 1959 he argued that the bomb saved "millions of lives"), Mr. Allen and Mr. Polmar are more intrigued by the manipulation of casualty estimates before Truman made his decision. They note a "worst-case scenario" in June 1945 that estimated the number of battle casualties at 220,000, but caution that the military was not averse to reshaping casualty estimates in order to influence Truman's thinking on whether or not to invade Japan: "High estimates would make the invasion a far less attractive alternative to the bomb."
The authors display little ambivalence about the question raised by the second half of their subtitle, "And Why Truman Dropped the Bomb." They dispose of the immensely complex problem of whether or not the bomb should have been dropped in a few paragraphs, writing, "As for the use of the atomic bomb as an implied threat to the Soviet Union, geopolitics may have been on the minds of some of Truman's advisers, but the war and American lives were on his mind. Preparations for the massive amphibious assault on Japan were under way, and Truman went to Potsdam in July seeking assurance that Stalin would enter the war against Japan. Then Truman learned on July 16 that the atomic bomb would work, and he ordered it used. It was a weapon, and it might end the war without an invasion." Mr. Allen and Mr. Polmar conclude that Kyushu "would have been the bloodiest invasion in history" and "could have been surpassed by the assault of Honshu." The debate goes on.

HEADLINE: The Race to Armageddon

BYLINE: By Daniel J. Kevles; Daniel J. Kevles heads the Program in Science, Ethics and Public Policy at the California Institute of Technology. His books include "The Physicists: The History of a Scientific Community in Modern America."

 BODY:

DARK SUN
The Making of the Hydrogen Bomb.
By Richard Rhodes.
Illustrated. 731 pp. New York:
Simon & Schuster. $32.50. 
 THE discovery, in early September 1949, that the Soviet Union had recently exploded its first A-bomb forced American nuclear-weapons policy to a fraught point of decision. Although the United States possessed a formidable and steadily expanding nuclear arsenal, a number of scientists and policy makers demanded a crash program to build a thermonuclear weapon that they called "the Super"; fueled by hydrogen, it would explode with the power of millions of tons of TNT in a blast a thousand times greater than the one that destroyed Hiroshima. The H-bomb's most zealous advocate was Edward Teller, a theoretical physicist and refugee from Hungary, for whom the Super had become an obsession. He battled scientists like J. Robert Oppenheimer, the charismatic head of wartime Los Alamos whose public career he would later help destroy, politicking against their attempts to block the escalation of terror and warning that if the Soviets beat the United States to a thermonuclear weapon, "our situation will be hopeless."
In "Dark Sun," a book of formidable range, Richard Rhodes recounts in detail what led up to the Soviet explosion, how Mr. Teller and his allies obtained Presidential authorization for a crash hydrogen bomb program and how the weapon was ultimately invented. His book, the first in a new Sloan Technology Series sponsored by the Alfred P. Sloan Foundation, handsomely fulfills the series' aim, which is to deepen public understanding of modern technology in all its human and technical dimensions.
Mr. Rhodes draws on a vast array of sources, melding previous scholarship with an abundance of fresh material from memoirs, interviews, F.B.I. files and recently released Soviet documents. Illuminating issues through the play of personalities, he sometimes loses sight of how bureaucratic forces shaped policy disputes and gives little attention to the industrial underpinnings of the nuclear weapons enterprise. But no great matter. He writes with a sharp eye for anecdote, character and political context, and with the talent for lucid exposition of technical issues that he displayed in his award-winning "Making of the Atomic Bomb." The result is a brilliantly rich and vivid account of the interlocked early history of the cold war and the arms race that is as chilling as it is enthralling.
In a kind of extended prologue to his main story, Mr. Rhodes devotes much of the first third of "Dark Sun" to the espionage that speeded the Soviet A-bomb effort. Here he makes especially effective use of F.B.I. records and Soviet sources, laying out the activities of key atomic spies like Klaus Fuchs, David Greenglass, Julius Rosenberg and Harry Gold. He explores their motives, recounts their shady rendezvous at bars, apartments and street corners with one another and with the Soviet rezidents who ran the show in the United States. He concludes that the espionage was extensive -- as this month's disclosure of decrypted Soviet cables demonstrates -- and that by the end of the war it produced probably 10,000 pages, much of it from American classified documents. By correlating the information the spies passed on with contemporary Soviet technical writing, he finds that it significantly shaped the Soviet technical program, guiding Igor Kurchatov, the cheery and resilient physicist who headed the Soviet nuclear enterprise, to an advantageous reactor design, to the recognition that a bomb could be made of plutonium and to the implosion method of detonation. Mr. Rhodes, whose independent treatment of the Soviet story complements David Holloway's 1994 book, "Stalin and the Bomb," estimates, as do Mr. Holloway's best qualified sources, that espionage accelerated the Soviet bomb by as much as two years.
On one side of the debate that followed the discovery of Joe 1, as the Soviet bomb was known in American intelligence circles, was a distinguished complement of scientists and policy makers, including Oppenheimer, since Hiroshima a leading adviser on nuclear policy, along with other oppositionists, who urged that the United States not build an H-bomb in the hope that the Soviets would then not either. Others argued that the bomb should be pursued only if the Soviets would not agree to forgo developing one. Their reasons were partly moral -- an H-bomb would be a weapon of "genocide" and, as such, "necessarily an evil thing," as the Nobel laureates I. I. Rabi and Enrico Fermi put it -- and partly prudential: the national interest of the United States lay in avoiding a thermonuclear arms race.
The arguments were also technical. It was an open question whether the bomb design that Mr. Teller was promoting -- what Mr. Rhodes calls the "classical Super," as distinct from a more modestly energetic alternative called the "Alarm Clock" -- was inconsistent with the known laws of physics. The basic idea was to use the heat of a fission bomb to ignite a mixture of the hydrogen isotopes deuterium and tritium to a fusion burn, but under theoretical analysis, Mr. Teller's variations had repeatedly failed because of inadequate ignition or fizzling of the burn.
Lined up with Mr. Teller were distinguished physicists like Ernest O. Lawrence and powerful officials, including Lewis L. Strauss, of the Atomic Energy Commission, and the Joint Chiefs of Staff. Some could see no moral difference between slaughtering a city with several atomic bombs or with a single H-bomb. The Chiefs considered it "foolhardy altruism" voluntarily to renounce a superweapon, a view no doubt influenced by the big-bomb ambitions of Gen. Curtis LeMay, who in March 1949 had proposed an attack plan for his Strategic Air Command that, using just atomic bombs, would destroy 70 Soviet cities in 30 days. Secretary of State Dean Acheson was unconvinced -- justifiably, as Mr. Rhodes and Mr. Holloway both show -- that a paranoid adversary could be persuaded to disarm by example. At the end of January 1950, President Harry S. Truman announced the crash H-bomb program, at a time, Rabi bitterly remembered, "when we didn't even know how to make one."
The dramatic account of how we learned to make one is one of the most arresting sections of "Dark Sun." The breakthrough came in January 1951, on the periphery of the crash effort, in the inspiration of Stanislaw Ulam, the mathematical physicist who had pioneered the tedious calculations showing that the classical Super would fail. Ulam's idea was that a thermonuclear weapon might work if neutrons from the fission primary were used to compress and ignite the fusion fuel. Mr. Teller brilliantly improved on it, proposing the superior trick of using the fission primary's radiation and, in April, adding a second fission mechanism.
At a meeting of physicists in Princeton in mid-June, the new, unexpected approach was greeted as virtually miraculous. There was no talk of evil and genocide. Oppenheimer enlisted in the H-bomb army, later explaining that the breakthrough was too "technically sweet" to pass up. Mr. Rhodes rightly asks "why technical promise should decide questions of politics and morality," and answers that the frame of ethical perception had changed, because of the Korean War, the exposure of Fuchs, who knew the early ideas for the Super, and the expectation that if our physicists could think of a radiation-fired H-bomb, so could Soviet physicists. On Nov. 1, 1952, the principle of the new Super was proved in a spectacularly successful test in the Pacific, code-named "Mike," that yielded a 10-megaton blast and that Mr. Rhodes describes in riveting detail.
 YET for Edward Teller and assorted acolytes of a big-bomb defense, the advent of the H-bomb was not enough. They wanted to destroy Robert Oppenheimer as a credible participant in national-security policy. In singling him out for the Svengali-like influence they supposed he had over people who had sought to thwart them, they were driven by an obsessive anti-Communism, by distrust of Oppenheimer, who had mixed with Communists, and, in the cases of Mr. Teller and Lewis Strauss, by a deep hatred of the man. Mr. Rhodes compellingly summarizes the machinations that led to the official finding, in 1954, that Oppenheimer was a security risk. He believes that Oppenheimer contributed to his own downfall by lying at his security hearing about a 1943 wartime approach on behalf of the Soviet Union. But he emphasizes that the touchstone issue was his opposition to the hydrogen bomb.
A number of the key charges were drawn from what Mr. Teller had told the F.B.I. in 1952 -- that Oppenheimer had persistently hindered and effectively delayed the development of the weapon, and that but for him, it might have been ready by 1951 or even earlier. Mr. Rhodes shreds Mr. Teller's misrepresentations of the matter then and since, concluding convincingly that it was Edward Teller himself who delayed the development of the hydrogen bomb by forcing concentration of the American effort for so long on his idee fixe, the classical Super. Before too long, Mr. Rhodes observes, nuclear weapons became "obsolete" as instruments of destruction; the terrifying days of the Cuban missile crisis made tangible that using them would invite Armageddon. Yet they served many other purposes -- for example, helping to drain enormous resources from the civilian into the military economy, including General LeMay's swelling sector of it.
Robert Oppenheimer was in the end victimized because he eloquently advanced a view that threatened the megaton ambitions of Mr. Teller and his allies -- the view that greater security did not lie in ever more powerful nuclear arsenals and that the cost of pursuing them, as "Dark Sun" shows, could undermine the vitality of the democracy they were built to defend.

PERSON: KEVLES, DANIEL J; RHODES, RICHARD EDWARD TELLER (70%);

SECTION: Section B; Page 1; Column 2; Metropolitan Desk 

LENGTH: 1551 words

HEADLINE: Discord Rattles Co-op City Towers;
Feuds and Finances Threaten Stability of Middle-Class Refuge

BYLINE: By MATTHEW PURDY 

 BODY:
The very idea of Co-op City sounds idealistic, even friendly, like a gigantic concrete commune in the Bronx on the Hutchinson River. It is the largest cooperative housing development in the country, with 55,000 residents who own shares of the sprawling development. They are not called tenants or renters or even partners -- they are "cooperators."
Lately, however, "gladiators" would be more fitting. 
 Open warfare has broken out on the governing board of Co-op City, which seems to comprise some of the least cooperative of cooperators. They are engaged in the high-rise version of small-town politics, with players making their entrances and exits at the comical pace of a French farce.
But the bickering obscures the fact that this city within a city, as residents like to call their community, is at a difficult crossroads. Co-op City, which was the urban answer to the call from moderate-income residents for safe, affordable and spacious living when it opened in the late 1960's, is in a squeeze much like the middle class in the city as a whole.
Twenty-seven years after it opened, helping to speed the working-class exodus from the South Bronx, Co-op City'sunderground infrastructure of water and utility pipes is defective and needs more than $200 million in repairs. That is about $75 million more than either the State of New York or the corporation that owns the complex says it can afford. In addition, nearly 10 percent of Co-op City's 8,000 parking spaces are closed because the parking garages are plagued by crumbling concrete, which would take at least $10 million to repair.
Fixing the utility system is considered crucial to Co-op City's survival, but officials say paying for the repairs could require a 30 percent increase in residents' maintenance fees over the next six years. Apartment prices were slashed two years ago to attract new buyers, and officials are concerned about increasing fees just as a new wave of largely black and Hispanic middle-income earners from around New York is discovering Co-op City.
When it opened, Co-op City was a refuge for middle-income families eager to flee the inner city but unable to afford the suburbs. It was a self-contained neighborhood with decent schools, a low crime rate and its own stores and playgrounds, all built on a former swamp that had once been the site of the Freedomland amusement park.
Crime remains low, but the recent shooting of an off-duty police officer in one of the buildings has hastened a push for lobby attendants. New residents, like the generations that preceded them, laud Co-op City for its peaceful distance from the violence and decay of their old neighborhoods, but longtime residents decry the creeping graffiti and the occasional smell of urine in the elevators.
"We're fighting to keep it a stable community," said Gretchen B. Hazell, the newly elected president of the Riverbay Corporation, which owns Co-op City. She acknowledges that Co-op City has problems but said that the influx of new, young families interested "in protecting their investments" would give the development new energy.
"We're going to survive this," she said. "We're a city within a city, and all cities have problems."
Co-op City is engaged in a new marketing campaign that in 18 months has cut the number of vacancies among its 15,372 apartments to fewer than 600 from nearly 1,400. It now costs $1,750 per room to buy shares of Co-op City, and monthly fees amount to $140 per room.
The working-class promise of Co-op City is hardly lost. Ann Carty, 33, moved to Co-op City a year ago from the Castle Hill Houses, a public housing project, and said she considered the development "the last stop." She said other affordable neighborhoods were too dangerous and decayed. The hallways of her high-rise building at Co-op City are spotless, flowers grow in a garden outside the front entrance, and she says she feels relatively safe.
Ms. Carty, who has three children, works for a commercial cleaning company, and her husband works in building maintenance. Her concern, like others', is that Co-op City does not go the way of poorer neighborhoods. "I just don't want it to happen here," she said, while watching two of her children play on a nearby playground. "It you're working hard, you deserve better."
Questions about Co-op City's stability have been reinforced recently by its tumultuous politics.
Since April, the board has had three presidents. Board members have asked the Bronx District Attorney to investigate whether other board members have received special treatment, like free countertops or custom colors painted in their apartments, and Ms. Hazell unilaterally suspended the general manager of the $138 million-a-year corporation.
Two investigations are focusing on the Co-op City board. In a state investigation, the Attorney General's office is reviewing the awarding of contracts to the insurance brokers who purchase insurance for the development to determine if favoritism was involved, two Co-op City officials who have cooperated with the investigation said.
A spokesman for District Attorney Robert T. Johnson said that the office was looking into allegations that board members received free work on their apartments. The accused say they are not guilty.
"I have a receipt!" said Iris Baez, one of the three recent board presidents. "They claim I got a countertop. I did. But I bought it. They claim I had wallpaper. That's true, but my ceiling fell. I didn't get anything any other cooperator didn't get."
Mrs. Baez was removed as president by her fellow board members in April, after she made the original charges of corruption against the board members. Her successor, Clarence (Jake) Powell, a retired homicide detective, then began an investigation that reported "a disturbing pattern of special favors being provided to" board members -- including Mrs. Baez -- and turned that evidence over to the District Attorney.
Five weeks ago, Co-op City voters elected a new board majority that includes Mrs. Baez, who is now the first vice president and head of the Integrity Committee. She is an ally of the new president, Ms. Hazell.
Ms. Hazell has said she intends to bring order to Co-op City's management. Late last month, without taking a vote of the board, she suspended the general manager, Alan M. Brenner, blaming him for problems at the development. Although Ms. Hazell said Mr. Brenner had been operating under "a cloud" because some Co-op City workers had painted his Westchester County house, he said he had paid them privately.
Then Ms. Hazell moved on to other matters. She ordered the personnel director to draw up a dress code for employees and directed all employees to address each other with courtesy titles like Mrs. and Mr. "I come down the hall and people say, 'Hey, Gretchen,' " she said. "Like, I'm the president."
But Mr. Powell, speaking of the board's new majority, said, "They're really out of control."
A board member, Cheryl Abramson Richards, said of all the infighting, "If it weren't so dangerous, it would be hysterical."
Co-op City aficionados say that the development has long been afflicted with complicated, hardball politics and that the current financial problems have been long in coming. Co-op City was built as a state-assisted project, and its construction was overseen by the state.
"The basic problem with Co-op City was that it was built under lax supervision," said Congressman Eliot L. Engel, a Democrat who maintains an apartment in Co-op City, which he represents. "There were shoddy materials used, and there was wholesale thievery going on. Five or six years after the place was built, it was falling apart."
Since the 1970's, Co-op City residents and state officials have been arguing over who should pay for repairs. In the mid-1970's, residents went on a 15-month rent strike to protest increases in the monthly maintenance, or carrying, charges.
In 1992, the state agreed to provide $108 million for the necessary renovations, and the Riverbay Corporation put up $25 million. But the estimated cost of the repairs are $210 million, leaving a shortfall to be made up with increases in the monthly carrying charges.
Those fees are also expected to rise in the year 2000 because of an agreement that calls for Co-op City to begin paying the state 13 annual payments of $10 million to settle unpaid mortgage payments accumulated during past rent strikes.
Representative Engel said the state would have to come up with more money or risk pricing many residents out of their apartments. He and other Co-op City residents say the state should pay for the repairs because it was responsible for monitoring the initial construction.
The state has said it will not put up any more money, and even that has become an issue in Co-op City politics. The current board leaders accuse their predecessors of negotiating a bad deal with the state that straps residents with a future of increased monthly fees.
Then again, almost everything seems to become a matter of politics. "The politics here are absolutely cutthroat," said Shirley Saunders, a former board member who was once accused by political opponents of using two parking spaces but paying for one. Then she quickly added, "But the community is lovely."

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (95%);

HEADLINE: June Orders Down For Machine Tools

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 30

 BODY:
June orders for machine tools made in the United States fell less than 1 percent from May levels, holding essentially steady as higher shipments in the first half of 1995 kept orders afloat, a trade group said in a report to be released today.
Total orders fell seven-tenths of a percent in June to $375.9 million from a revised $378.6 million in May, the Association for Manufacturing Technology said. Earlier, the association had said that May orders reached $387.1 million. June orders were down 6.9 percent from $403.7 million a year earlier. 
 Despite the overall decline, Albert Moore, president of the association, said it was satisfying that export orders for the year were more than double the 1994 level.
"Healthy overseas markets will be a necessary part of the U.S. industry's continued growth," he added.
Export orders in June, however, fell 17.2 percent to $52.5 million from a revised $63.4 million in May, the association said. They rose 19.8 percent from $43.8 million in June 1994. May orders were revised down from $64.1 million.
Shipments, at $2.18 billion in the first six months of 1995, were 15.3 percent higher than the $1.89 billion for the period last yer.
Orders so far this year climbed 14.5 percent from the first six months of 1994. Export orders through June soared 158.6 percent from the 1994 period.
The association said the backlog of unfilled orders in June fell from May, to $2.37 billion. In June last year the backlog stood at $1.67 billion. June shipments rose to $438.6 million from a revised $371.7 million in May. A year ago, shipments were $371.3 million.
