HEADLINE: SENATE COMMITTEE STARTS RESHAPING HOUSE BUDGET BILL

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 30

 BODY:
Less than two weeks after a raucous House approved a $23.2 billion spending bill crammed with political trouble, the Senate has begun quietly but methodically stripping the measure of its most controversial provisions on abortion, AIDS education, the Mexican peso and the outright abolition of offices in the core of the Democratic White House.
The House's efforts drew headlines and loud debate on the floor of Congress. But much less notice was given six days later to the Senate Appropriations subcommittee that quietly picked up the House bill, excised each of those provisions, and cut spending by $175 million more. 
 The bill is one of 13 that set 1996 spending, totaling about $540 billion, for the three branches of Government and their agencies. About $1.2 trillion in spending on interest on the national debt and on benefit programs -- welfare, Medicare, Social Security and so on -- is covered in other measures that have yet to come to a vote in either house.
"Let's try to clean up some of these bills from the House," the Appropriations Committee's chairman, Senator Mark O. Hatfield of Oregon, said dryly as his panel approved the bill late last week.
As the Senate begins to receive the great slug of spending bills from the House, that appears to be just what is occurring, even though both bodies seem intent on making major cuts in spending.
The House, impatient to advance the conservative revolution, has used the bills not just to cut spending, but to insure that what is spent promotes a rightward ideology.
The Senate, either because it is less revolutionary or more realistic, is stripping out the ideology and keeping the spending cuts. It is as if the Visigoths had submitted the records of their latest conquests to a Big Eight accounting firm.
The House and the Senate must agree on every jot and tittle of all 13 measures, including the legislative riders on issues like AIDS education and abortion, before they can go to the President, who may reject them anyway.
"The Clinton Administration isn't going to like a lot of these bills," said Thomas Mann, the Brookings Institution scholar and authority on Congress, "but it's going to have a lot more to do with their funding levels than these legislative riders. A fair amount of them are going to fall out in the Senate."
The Administration already does not like a lot of the bills. It has threatened to veto 5 of the 11 that the House has approved so far unless the Senate vastly moderates them.
The Senate has its work cut out for it. When the House passes a $261 billion spending bill this week covering the Labor, Education and Health and Human Services departments, it will have virtually completed its work on that section of the 1996 budget. By contrast, the Senate has barely begun; its Appropriations Committee has plowed through about half the stack of bills and put off the prickliest ones until Congress returns from vacation in September.
The Senate has passed but two of the bills, neither controversial. It takes up a third, covering energy and water spending, this week.
Whatever differences the Senate has with the House measures do not extend to the amount they would spend. Both chambers agreed in June to a budget outline that sharply reduces the growth of spending in 1996 and the next six years. The size of next year's pot of money has already been decided.
The fiscal differences are niggling, but they mirror the two bodies' political leanings. The senators already are breaking ranks with the House over how and where to spend the money, and on whether the spending bills should be vehicles for other efforts to dismantle 40 years of more liberal and generally Democratic programs.
The Senate would allot about $1.4 billion less for the military than the more hawkish House, but set aside an extra $1.6 billion for labor, education and welfare programs. The House would spend a trifle more on highways, buses and airports; the Senate would spend more for veterans and public housing.
The House and Senate can reconcile such gaps when they meet to hammer out compromise spending bills. But the ideological differences within the bills, written into spending priorities and politically inspired amendments, will be tougher.
Consider the $23.2 billion bill, which finances the Treasury, Postal Service, White House and other general government agencies, that Mr. Hatfield's Senate panel tackled last week. It arrived from the House bearing an amendment that barred Federal employee health plans from paying for abortions except when the mother's life was in danger. Another prohibited Federal worker-training programs from seeking to change "values or life style," or providing more than medical and legal information about AIDS.
A third amendment barred the Treasury Department from using its Exchange Stabilization Fund to support a foreign currency -- in this instance, the Mexican peso.
A fourth amendment cut financing for Mr. Clinton's economic advisers, a heretofore uncontroversial group that apparently fell to the House's desire to show who holds the national purse strings.
The Appropriations subcommittee headed by Senator Richard Shelby, an Alabama Democrat turned Republican last year, removed all those clauses and largely followed the directives of Mr. Hatfield.
"Hatfield doesn't really interfere," a committee aide said last week. "Basically, he says not to put anything in your bill that's controversial -- if you have something, bring it to the floor, not the committee."
In fact, supporters of the House provisions will get a second shot, because the legislative rules provide that decisions to remove clauses from House bills must be approved by the full Senate. Most such changes are offered as a package that the Senate votes up or down.
The House is sending Mr. Hatfield some bombshells, especially in the labor and education spending bill being considered this week.
Some of the amendments attached to spending bills in the House reflect the demands of the Contract With America and repay Republicans' grass-roots supporters, especially conservative Christians.
As it now stands, the labor and education bill includes clauses that withhold Federal grants from nonprofit groups and companies that lobby or engage in political activities, limit medical research involving human embryos, and let the states withhold Medicaid money for abortions involving rape and incest -- which would limit Medicaid financing for abortions to only those needed to save the life of the mother.
The bill would restrict the National Labor Relations Board's ability to seek injunctions in labor disputes, and prevent the Occupational Safety and Health Administration from drafting or carrying out some safety rules, including one crafted to reduce repetitive motion injuries.
It would reduce money for public broadcasting and eliminate a billion-dollar program, called Liheap, that helps the poor pay their utility bills, and restrict the use of money for family-planning programs.
And that is one bill. Elsewhere, the House and Senate will probably disagree on how much to spend on the Energy Department, on money for Indian programs, on whether to abolish the office of the White House drug czar, and on how much to spend on exotic new weaponry.
When Congress returns to town after Labor Day, the Senate must settle those issues, approve virtually the entire budget, strike compromises on all 13 bills with the House, and then send them to the President, who seems determined to veto many of them anyway.

HEADLINE: Berkshire Eagle's Painful Decision to Sell

BYLINE: By SARA RIMER 

DATELINE: PITTSFIELD, Mass.

 BODY:
For 103 years, The Berkshire Eagle has been a Yankee institution, as important to the intellectual, social and political life of this corner of New England as the town meetings it has covered.
With a daily circulation of 32,000, The Eagle has been tough, thoughtful and thorough. Awarded the Pulitzer Prize for editorial writing in 1973, its reputation for excellence has extended far beyond Berkshire County. Journalists nationwide revere The Eagle as a guardian of their professional values. 
 One family, the Millers, has owned the paper -- based in this western Massachusetts town of 43,000 people -- and they have poured money into the newsroom. With a far larger staff than most other dailies of its size, The Eagle has chronicled it all: church suppers, school board elections, Boston Symphony Orchestra concerts at Tanglewood in nearby Lenox, labor problems at the local General Electric plant, politics at the Statehouse in Boston.
But now, the Millers' Eagle Publishing Company -- burdened by an estimated $19 million in debt, most of it from an ill-timed real estate deal -- is about to sell the paper, as well as its four other papers. An announcement of The Eagle sale is expected by mid-August; the paper is estimated to be worth $40 million to $50 million. The new owner is expected to be an outsider.
Michael Miller, publisher of The Eagle, has said that one prospective buyer is W. Dean Singleton, a Denver publishing entrepreneur whose companies own about 75 papers, including The Denver Post. Mr. Singleton is chief executive of Media News Group, which sold The Houston Post to its rival, The Houston Chronicle, in April -- resulting in the closing of The Post. He did not return telephone calls to his office.
Eagle reporters and editors also worry that under a new owner, The Eagle will lose its voice and its commitment to the community.
Over the years, The Eagle has become a training ground for journalists, including Helen W. Donovan, the executive editor of The Boston Globe; Stephen Rosenfeld, the deputy editorial page editor at The Washington Post; and the late Kirk Scharfenberg, who was the editorial page editor at The Globe.
The curator of the Nieman Foundation at Harvard University, Bill Kovach, began reading The Eagle in the early 1970's, while he was the Boston bureau chief for The New York Times. "The thing about The Eagle that really set it apart was its willingness to accept a responsibility to presume that it was the only paper its readers took in their circulation area," he said. "And it made the effort to provide the best possible coverage of the issues that affected their lives, including state government and the public-affairs issues confronting the whole state."
"That kind of willingness to use your resources to provide your readers with what they deserved, and what they needed, is the hallmark of great journalism," Mr. Kovach added. "It's really sad to see a paper like The Eagle on the block with an almost certain assurance that it will be bought by a corporate chain whose interests are corporate and the bottom line."
 Donald A. MacGillis, The Eagle's editorial page editor, has spent 21 years at the paper. "The Eagle makes money," he said. "We're afraid the new owners will say, 'I want it to make a whole lot more money.' American journalism proves you can put out an awful paper and still make money."
 Kelton B. Miller started running The Eagle in 1892, with one flatbed press and one Linotype typesetting machine. The family business eventually grew into the Eagle Publishing Company, which also owns two dailies and a weekly in Vermont and a daily in Connecticut.
When Kelton Miller died in 1941, his sons, Pete and Donald, inherited the paper. Pete was editor and Don publisher until Don died in 1972. Then Pete became publisher, too.
Pete Miller knew what readers wanted. "He had this little VW Beetle and he'd just drive everywhere," said Richard K. Weil, an assistant managing editor at The St. Louis Post Dispatch, who was a reporter at The Eagle. "Everything about the county interested him."
When Pete Miller died in 1991, his son Michael took over as publisher, and another son, Mark, became editor. "It's painful," Mark Miller said in a recent telephone interview. "It's a defeat. We'd rather have it be a success and carry on and try to put out a very good paper ourselves for another generation or so."
A third brother, Kelton, is the publisher of the Vermont papers. Their sister, Margo, is a reporter for The Boston Globe.
As the publisher of The Eagle and president of the company, Michael Miller played a key role in the real estate deal that left Eagle Publishing mired in debt. In 1987, the company bought an 11-acre mill complex in Pittsfield for $3.66 million, when the market was booming, and spent $23.7 million to renovate it for The Eagle, with space for other tenants. While the building has won awards and The Eagle has a new newsroom, the building is half empty.
The purchase was supposed to diversify the company's investments and make the company's future more secure. Grier Horner, the assistant managing editor, said: "The ironic thing is that the effort to insulate the paper from financial shocks ultimately became the death knell for the Miller ownership. It was a bold move that backfired."
Mr. Miller blames Eagle Publishing's problems partly on the recession, which worsened in Pittsfield in the early 1990's. General Electric slashed its work force from 8,000 to 600. House after house was put up for sale. England Brothers, the family-owned department store and a major Eagle advertiser, was closed.
 Mr. Miller said the stock market crash in 1987 and the soaring cost of newsprint also hurt the company.
 In 1991, Eagle Publishing acquired more debt, apparently, after it bought a paper in Connecticut. "I think buying The Middletown Press after buying that building just sealed it," Mr. Miller said. He declined to say how much had been spent to acquire The Press, but added that the paper had become profitable.
Seated at his grandfather's roll-top desk in his office during a recent interview, Michael Miller declined to talk about his feelings about the sale. He did offer, however, that "the new owners will probably make savings increases we haven't been able to."
With circulation steady and advertising solid, The Eagle remains profitable, Mr. Miller said.
But the newsroom is an anxious place these days. Under Pete Miller, not a single reporter or editor was laid off, even when circulation dipped. But since then, the newsroom staff has been cut from 62 to 40 employees. Twelve reporters have to cover territory once handled by 20.
The news editor, Clarence Fanto, said: "It's a community trust; it's the town crier; it's the bulletin board for the county. Who's going to buy it? What are they going to do to it? Will it still be The Berkshire Eagle?"
 And cost-cutting has meant the end of certain Eagle traditions, like free obituaries. Readers were outraged in May when the paper began charging $85 for an obituary.
But despite the possible change of ownership at The Eagle, "the newsroom hasn't quit," said Mr. Horner, who started as a reporter 30 years ago. "But it's harder. We're not as rich in subject matter and stories as we would be if we had a few more reporters. We're sort of like a runner with shinsplints. You're running, but it hurts, and you can't run as fast."

The Miller family, which is planning to sell the newspaper, has owned it for 103 years; The building that formerly housed the newspaper in Pittsfield. (Alan E. Solomon/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: 2 Killed as Speeding Car Crashes Into Police Cruiser

BYLINE: By CHUCK SUDETIC 

 BODY:
Alcohol, reckless driving and an apparently fake 911 call combined in deadly fashion before dawn yesterday in the Bronx, when a 31-year-old immigrant from the Dominican Republic drove a red Honda into the side of a police cruiser, killing himself and his girlfriend and injuring two police officers, a Police Department official said.
Witnesses told police investigators that the Honda, heading south on Crotona Avenue, had run at least one red light and had crossed a double yellow line before it went through a stop signal at East 180th Street in East Tremont at about 75 miles per hour, said Deputy Chief Frank Biehler, the Police Department's executive officer in the Bronx, at an afternoon press conference at the 48th Precinct. 
 The car hit the broadside of the police cruiser, which had entered the intersection from the east at about 20 to 30 m.p.h. Its red lights were flashing, but its siren was switched off, the police said.
The impact sent Officer Tracey Brown, 33, the passenger in the cruiser, through the windshield, the deputy chief said. He landed about 25 feet from the police vehicle.
The Honda crashed into the wall of a nearby building. "Four empty beer bottles were found in the Honda," Deputy Chief Biehler said.
Rescue crews required 45 minutes to extract the Honda's driver, Juan Antonio Inoa Debarez, also known as Jose Santos, and his girlfriend, Gloria Bajana, 37, the police said.
Ms. Bajana, the mother of three children, was pronounced dead at the scene, Deputy Chief Biehler said. Mr. Inoa, who had two children in Santo Domingo, was pronounced dead at St. Barnabas Hospital about an hour after the accident.
Officer Brown was in critical but stable condition at Jacobi Medical Center with a deep cut on his head, burns on his eyes and cuts on his legs, a hospital representative said. The driver of the patrol car, Officer Paul Montalvo, 31, was listed in stable condition.
Mr. Inoa and Ms. Bajana lived at 2345 Crotona Avenue in Belmont, Deputy Chief Biehler said. Ms. Bajana worked as a home attendant, and Mr. Inoa was unemployed, he said.
"Mr. Santos is known to us by a variety of names," Deputy Chief Biehler said. Mr. Santos, or Mr. Inoa, had a temporary driver's license issued in California, Deputy Chief Biehler said, and he was arrested in the Bronx on Dec. 4 for driving under the influence of alcohol and running eight red lights.
The Honda driven by Mr. Inoa is registered to a Manhattan resident, Deputy Chief Biehler said. The name of the car's owner was not released.
The collision occurred as Officers Brown and Montalvo were responding at about 5:04 A.M. to a 911 call about a robbery a few blocks from the accident scene, Deputy Chief Biehler said. Other police officers who answered the robbery call found no signs of foul play, he said.
"The witnesses are telling us the radio car was proceeding 20 to 30 miles per hour, and the green light was in their favor," the deputy chief said. As many as nine people saw the accident, he added, and only one said the police vehicle had beel traveling at high speed.
Deputy Chief Biehler said it was the usual police procedure for officers to use their roof lights but not their siren when responding to a street-robbery call.
Juan Arguetta, a son-in-law of Ms. Bajana, said the couple had been to a party in the area and had probably been going out to get something to eat.
"When I go to the scene," he said, "I see my mother-in-law on the floor almost already dead. I talked to somebody, and he see everything. He say they not going fast, but the police were going fast. They were going to an emergency."
Mr. Arguetta said Ms. Bajana had come to the United States from Honduras 10 years ago. "She came here for a better life for her kids," he said.
Officer Montalvo remembers nothing about the incident, his brother, Rinaldo, said. When Officer Montalvo became conscious, his brother said, he asked, "Where's Brown?"

HEADLINE: In Somalia, a Chameleon Thrives

BYLINE: By DONATELLA LORCH 

DATELINE: NAIROBI, Kenya, July 25

 BODY:
A millionaire businessman, reputed arms dealer and drug trafficker, self-styled diplomat and peacemaker, Osman Hassan Ali has his own private army in Somalia. But as he limps into the lobby in Nairobi's Intercontinental Hotel, wearing a jacket and striped shirt and clutching a crutch in one hand, he hardly raises an eyebrow.
Accompanied by only one bodyguard, Osman Ato, as he is widely known (Ato is a nickname that means "skinny"), has come for coffee and a chat with an American Embassy official. The get-together is part of a whirlwind of meetings on Somalia's future that occupy much of his time these days as he commutes in and out of Mogadishu. 
 Osman Ato, 50, is far removed from the character of Somali warlords, men greedy for power and wealth at any cost. In the five months since the United Nations withdrew its troops, three men have already claimed to have been elected President of Somalia and formed governments. But the country remains in turmoil and rudderless, divided into clan territories. Even the Mogadishu airport tarmac is divided and under the control of different clans. Insecurity in Mogadishu has kept all but a handful of relief agencies in the capital and no Western nation has diplomats in Somalia.
Straight-talking and focused but coy about his own ambitions, Osman Ato is the chameleon of Somali politicians, showing himself to be a master of survival and adapting to shifting alliances. He financed clan militias during Somalia's civil war; built deadly "technicals," the four-wheel-drive cars mounted with antiaircraft guns and other automatic weapons, and expanded his fortune with United Nations contracts.
Now he says he wants to bring peace to his country so business can thrive and Somalia can rebuild itself. Of all the Somali political players, Osman Ato is the most skillful and pragmatic, Western diplomats say.
Once the chief financier of General Mohammed Farah Aidid, Somalia's most powerful clan leader and recently one of its self-proclaimed presidents, Osman Ato is now his enemy. Like many of General Aidid's former supporters, he has openly split and says he disagrees with his former leader's international isolationism and his desire to fight other clans to maintain his grip on power.
In 1992, Osman Ato helped pave the way for the food airlift and later the American troop landing. He was the Americans' main contact and negotiator with General Aidid.
Within half a year as the tension escalated and the United Nations began an all-out assault against General Aidid, the American military bombed Osman Ato's garages, destroying hundreds of thousands of dollars of bulldozers and other equipment as well as equipment that gave him the ability to build technicals. American military officials described him as "General Aidid's evil genius."
As they searched for Mr. Aidid, the American military arrested and imprisoned Osman Ato. In prison on an island off the coast of Somalia for four months, he suffered from malnutrition and severe neglect, former Western diplomats in Somalia say.
A year and a half after his release, Osman Ato's garages are up and running and making technicals again, he says. He is also once again talking and dealing with the Americans, trying to negotiate for funds for reconciliation conferences.
"We had a dispute but that dispute can be settled at a later stage," Osman Ato said about his relationship with the American Government. "We have to work with the Americans. We cannot live without them. We need their help in assisting Somalia. Personal grievances can come at a later date."
His pragmatism has brought at least grudging respect.
"He's very shrewd," said Robert Oakley, a former United States Ambassador to Somalia and its special envoy during the United Nations mission there. "Obviously he knows how to make deals and how to work with the Americans. He understands what makes sense to us.
"Not that he's our man," he said. "Politically, he can't afford to be too close to the Americans. He's one of the people keenly aware of how much damage we did to Somalia."
Osman Ato has spent much of his life dealing with Americans in Somalia. From the age of 11, he worked for American oil companies, starting as a greaser and working his way up to general manager, developing close ties, in particular with Conoco. He also built businesses in heavy equipment and became one of Mogadishu's biggest real estate owners. He has 22 children, 3 of whom are in college in the United States.
"He makes more money than the country is making," Mr. Oakley said.
When the civil war broke out in Somalia in 1991, Osman Ato allied himself with Mr. Aidid and financed his militias. They are both members of the Hawiye clan and its Habr Gedir sub clan.
Mr. Oakley remembers a night in June 1993 when a truck piled high with weapons was smuggled out of Osman Ato's compound just days before his garage was bombed. When United Nations troops dug up the huge sand berms blocking roads near Osman Ato's garage, they found weapons caches. In a recent interview, Osman Ato said his garages were back in business making technicals.
Late last year, his car drove over a land mine in western Somalia and the explosion broke both his feet. He said he was only visiting the area; American diplomats said he was organizing a weapons transaction near the Ethiopian border.
Osman Ato said that when he was imprisoned by the American military, he was handcuffed and blindfolded for long periods of time. American diplomats deny it. But Mr. Oakley said Osman Ato's health had so deteriorated because of neglect and malnutrition that he had to be transferred to a hospital in Baidoa.
He knows how to play the chess game of Somali politics -- when to bluff, when to bargain, when to play innocent, when to ignore and when to fight. In 1993, the day before the Americans bombed his garage and with tension and anti-American hatred building in Mogadishu, he invited a reporter for tea at his house, just down the street from his garage.
The blinds were drawn against the scorching sun as he calmly sipped his cardamom-flavored tea and watched CNN. He did not talk about politics, but about the CNN program on cholesterol that he was watching.

HEADLINE: Baltimore Journal;
Japanese-Style Booths Put Police at the Center of the Action

BYLINE: By MICHAEL JANOFSKY 

DATELINE: BALTIMORE, July 28

 BODY:
The little booth sits just off a downtown intersection that once stood at the retail heart of the city, with large department stores, movie theaters and fashionable restaurants. In recent years, with one street a pedestrian mall and the other reserved for buses and commuter trains, the neighborhood has become a collection of specialty shops and stores for the budget-conscious.
But as the neighborhood struggles to stay vibrant without the tourist appeal of other downtown attractions like Oriole Park at Camden Yards and Harbor Place mall, few buildings seem to be as vital to the area's well-being as the small booth. It is known as a koban, a police substation modeled and named after similar buildings used in Japan as part of that country's law-enforcement policy of stationing officers among the people they protect. 
 Baltimore's Howard Street koban (pronounced: koh-bahn), an air-conditioned booth with bullet-proof glass large enough to accommodate two people, was opened in May at a cost of $150,000. Since then, officers assigned to the 10-by-10-foot koban say crimes that were common to the area, like purse snatchings, shoplifting, assaults and robberies, have sharply declined.
And that is in keeping with trends in other cities where the police are using kobans and other Japanese crime-control measures. A report to be published this fall by the Milton S. Eisenhower Foundation, a private organization that seeks solutions to urban problems, concludes that crime in those city neighborhoods dropped by as much as 35 percent.
"They're a novel way of bringing police officers closer to the community," said Alfred Dean, an executive with the Police Foundation, a group that studies policing techniques. "With police departments under the gun to control crime and being forced to do more with less, they're using more nontraditional methods. They're recognizing that the old methods are ineffective."
But as more cities turn to innovative ideas for policing, kobans are gaining in popularity not only as a crime deterrent. They are also proving valuable to groups leading drives for economic redevelopment and neighborhood revitalization.
"We're looking for the police to play a role in the total neighborhood redevelopment," said Lynn A. Curtis, president of the Eisenhower Foundation, which introduced city police chiefs to kobans on a trip to Japan in 1988. "We then try to mesh the kobans with the work of community development corporations."
Among the first cities to use a koban were Philadelphia and San Juan, P.R., after officials made the 1988 trip. In Japan, most city neighborhoods are served by kobans of various sizes rather than precinct stations, and the police work closely with residents, visiting each house at least once a year. Some Japanese officers assigned to larger kobans live in them with their families.
The structures contribute to the fact that Japan has one of the lowest crime rates in the world, some law-enforcement experts say.
The Eisenhower Foundation, a Washington-based group named for the brother of the former President, found that Japan's community policing techniques work in the United States. Its fall report concludes that the rate of reported crime in a residential neighborhood of San Juan, where officers worked with residents from a koban, fell by 35 percent from 1989 to 1993. Similar efforts in a retail section of Philadelphia north of downtown helped drive down the rate of reported crime by 24 percent from 1991 to 1993.
Successes were also reported in high-crime neighborhoods in Boston and Chicago, which used other Japanese methods, including having officers serve as mentors and counselors to troubled teen-agers. The police said reported crime in the neighborhoods declined by 27 percent in Boston and 22 percent in Chicago.
A more recent foundation-sponsored trip to Japan included officials from Baltimore, Little Rock, Ark., Los Angeles, Newark and Columbia, S.C.
Here in Baltimore, city officials have not yet linked koban officers and local community groups. But the mere presence of the structure seems to be deterring crime and easing tensions on the streets.
"It's so visible, and visibility is the key," said Gary Austin, manager of the Pick 'n' Pay shoe store just beyond the koban. "It has helped deter shoplifting and has cut down on some of the loitering."
The police department has not yet compiled crime statistics for the area. But Officer Dwight P. Thomas, whose workday includes sitting in the koban and walking the streets, said that only rarely had he come across criminal activity, from either watching through the window of the koban or patrolling.
In several months, the department plans to install surveillance cameras around the neighborhood to allow the koban officer to monitor a wider area. The city is also studying the idea of placing other kobans in highly populated neighborhoods.
"People down here love it," Officer Thomas said. "They say it's good to see you out here, and teen-agers don't congregate the way they used to, standing on street corners."
Many local merchants said that they believed the koban concept had made the area safer. But few people sounded more appreciative of the police presence than John Mathew Smith, a 36-year-old photographer.
Waiting for a bus near the koban recently, Mr. Smith was approached by a panhandler who asked for 50 cents. When Mr. Smith refused, the man punched him in the nose.
"I saw the whole thing," said Mr. Thomas, who was sitting in the koban at time. He dashed out and arrested the man on assault charges.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Officer Dwight Thomas, center, is not confined to the downtown Baltimore booth, or koban, where he is stationed. He stepped outside to talk with Chrys Cornish, who was giving his son Keyyon a ride on his shoulders, when a bicycle patrol officer and a youngster selling newspapers happened by. (Marty Katz for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: MICROSOFT'S MOBILIZATION: THE CUSTOMERS;
Computer Users Told To Go Slow in Change Of Operating Systems

BYLINE: By LAURIE FLYNN 

 BODY:
When it finally goes on sale on Aug. 24, Microsoft's Windows 95 will be better, faster and easier to use than any previous version of the software. So why is Brian Mott, a computer consultant to small and large companies, telling his clients not to buy?
Wait till next year, Mr. Mott advises. 
 "There aren't too many reasons to change right now," said Mr. Mott, who is based in Larkspur, Calif. "And there are too many things we don't know."
For Mr. Mott's clients, some of whom have hundreds of PC's, changing to the new operating system would be disruptive and expensive, and fraught with the minor technical glitches common in the first iteration of any software.
And he is not alone. While many businesses will begin the transition to Windows 95 immediately, many others will adopt Mr. Mott's wait-and-see stance, as they do when any significant new software program is introduced. Despite all the advance billing -- and the extensive field-testing by 400,000 volunteer users -- Windows 95 appears to be no exception.
"It would be a natural evolution of our existing system, but we're taking a cautious approach," said Annie Reutinger, a spokeswoman for the the McKesson Corporation. McKesson, in San Francisco, is the nation's largest distributor of pharmaceuticals and health-care products with 11,000 employees worldwide; it has 5,000 PC's that use Windows. "It'll take at least six months and a lot of research."
But even if corporate America takes its time, most analysts agree that just about every company running Windows today will make the transition to Windows 95 eventually. The question is how quickly and at what cost.
As software goes, Windows 95 is cheap at about $100. But for many people, the actual cost will be several times that amount, because they will need or want a faster microprocessor, more memory and a bigger hard disk to accommodate Windows 95's 11 million lines of programming code (Windows 3.1 has 3 million lines of code.) And to take full advantage of Windows 95's features, they will need new versions of word processors, spreadsheets and so on at a cost of anywhere from $50 to several hundred dollars apiece.
Kent D. Bentkowski, a Windows user who runs a graphics company called Perfik Graphix in Angola, N.Y., figures he will have invested $1,200 to $1,500 preparing his system for Windows 95.
In fact, Computer Intelligence Infocorp, a market researcher, says that fewer than 40 percent of the PC's currently in use in homes or businesses meet the technical requirements for Windows 95. The vast majority of existing computers have neither the memory nor the computing power. (A quarter of the people who rely on Microsoft software have yet to move to Windows at all and are still running MS-DOS, the company's original PC operating system.)
Microsoft's official position is that Windows 95 will run on machines with as little as 4 megabytes of memory, but people who have tested the software report that under those conditions performance is unacceptable for anything but the most rudimentary tasks.
Microsoft recommends that people have at least 8 megabytes on their machine, which is standard on most new computers; most early reviews in the trade press recommend 16 megabytes. That addition alone would cost about $350 retail.
The new operating system also hogs microprocessor power. Adequate performance requires at least a 486 chip made by the Intel Corporation and a couple of small competitors; optimal performance requires one of Intel's faster Pentium chips.
The computer's storage requirement has also climbed with Windows 95. Simply loading the software onto the machine requires about 35 megabytes of hard-disk space, depending on the features chosen, compared with the 10 megabytes needed by the version of Windows it is replacing, Windows 3.1. And the applications software takes up a lot more hard-disk space, too. The Windows 95 version of Microsoft Office, an assortment of business computing programs like the Word word-processing program and the Excel spreadsheet, takes up 89 megabytes -- 20 more than the Windows 3.1 version of Office.
In short, if millions of computer users want to start using Windows 95 and enjoying its benefits, they will have to buy additional hardware and software and "upgrade kits," creating a potential sales boom for makers of all those products. By the end of next year, Infocorp predicts, more than 60 percent of the PC's in use will have the horsepower to run Windows 95.
But before all those upgrades, the bulk of Windows 95 sales will be as the operating system software that will come installed on new computers. Many of those early sales are expected to be to home computer users, the market's fastest-growing segment.
"The person who will have the easiest time with this will be the home user who buys it pre-installed," said David Coursey, editor of PC Letter, an industry newsletter published in San Mateo, Calif.
For corporations, shifting to Windows 95 will be daunting. In addition to the hardware and software, they face the substantial cost of training people to use the new software and employee downtime as they learn.
"Here you have a system that is infinitely more complex," Mr. Coursey said. "There are just lots of things that add up to hours and hours." For starters, Windows 95 takes an entirely different approach to networking than version 3.1.
But even if corporate sales of Windows 95 start off slowly, analysts say, it is only a matter of time until managers come around rather than risk being left behind. The expectation is that in a year or two, the most sophisticated desktop business software will probably require Windows 95.
"I don't know any company that's not going to move to it," said William Cornfield, president of the Windows Support Group, a software consulting firm in New York whose clients include the National Football League, AT&T and General Electric. "It's just a question of timing."

HEADLINE: In America;
Out Of Work

BYLINE: By BOB HERBERT 

 BODY:
A very small group of people at a church in Greenwich Village is attempting to seriously engage the most devastating issue of our time -- the wholesale elimination of employment.
Jeremy Rifkin titled his book on this frightening phenomenon "The End of Work." 
 Peter Laarman, the senior minister at Judson Memorial Church, calls it "the triumph of profits over people."
Judson Memorial is on the southern edge of Washington Square Park. Its congregants are mostly middle class, and thus increasingly vulnerable to the threat of joblessness that has spread anxiety across the country. What Mr. Laarman learned, soon after coming to the church in early 1994, was that the men and women who were losing their jobs often were too upset, and too ashamed, to even talk about it.
Talented, successful and mostly middle-aged, they were not prepared to cope with the hardships of unemployment that have always bedeviled the working classes and the poor.
"Losing my job," said Susan Boyer, who agreed only reluctantly to speak on the record about her experience, "felt just like it felt the time I got mugged. The awful feeling in the gut was exactly the same. There I was in this nice high-tech job, and then I got downsized. I felt, 'This can't be happening to me.' "
Mr. Laarman decided it would help if the church could provide a "safe place" in which people could "speak about their frustration and their anxiety related to the workplace." That modest effort grew, and eventually the church became the headquarters for the Employment Project, an ambitious attempt, still in its earliest stages, to get the religious community across the country more actively involved in the jobs crisis.
Mr. Laarman and the Rev. Paul Chapman, director of the Employment Project, are trying, among other things, to get the clergy to pay more attention to the pernicious combination of radical corporate downsizing and the flight of government from its responsibility to help people in need.
This combination is likely to insure that as times get tougher, more and more people will be left completely to their own devices.
"This is a moral crisis as well as an economic turning point," said Mr. Laarman. "My hope is that in various ways, partly by means of this project, the churches and synagogues and mosques will assume a degree of responsibility for leadership."
Mr. Laarman acknowledged that some religious organizations are already involved in the struggle against joblessness. But he believes that the clergy, for the most part, has not fulfilled its responsibility to counsel, support and fight on behalf of the millions of Americans for whom decent jobs are a thing of the past.
While the people at the top of the economic pyramid are accumulating unimaginable riches, enormous numbers of workers of all ages and in most economic categories are being discarded like so much rubbish. They are no longer needed. Often they are treated as if they are no longer human.
"Who would have thought it would come to this?" said David Johnson, a Judson Memorial member who twice was thrown out of work. He noted that the unemployed men and women who joined the discussion group at the church had many years of experience and "real technical expertise."
"We were as marketable as you could be," he said. "And yet, there we were -- on the outs, looking in."
The cold truth is that tens of millions of hard-working (or potentially hard-working) men and women have no real economic future in a system that is thoroughly dominated by a ruthless corporate elite and the politicians who do their bidding. As we close in on the 21st century, the ferocious parlay of technological advances, cheap foreign labor and corporate greed is being allowed to lay waste to the American dream.
We are allowing this to happen with little more than a whimper of protest.
Mr. Laarman and Mr. Chapman believe the religious community can and should serve as a crucial resource for workers abandoned by the business community, and increasingly by government as well.
"The loss of jobs is an awfully big issue and one that many people want to hide from," said Mr. Chapman. "But it needs to be faced."

HEADLINE: Governors Try To Find Unity On Medicaid

BYLINE: By ADAM CLYMER 

DATELINE: BURLINGTON, Vt., July 30

 BODY:
While welfare preoccupies Washington, the nation's governors are more worried about what cuts and changes Congress will make in Medicaid, and are searching at their annual summer meeting for a common position on how to alter the health care program for the poor.
Medicaid costs the states six times as much as welfare. So while many governors are reconciled to what Democrats call "cuts" and Republicans call "slower growth" in Federal Medicaid spending, they are arguing intensely about formulas for distributing the money to the states and the need to reduce Federal directives on how it gets spent. 
 While President Clinton and Senator Bob Dole of Kansas, the majority leader, plan to offer competing welfare plans when they speak on Monday at the National Governors' Association's 87th summer meeting here, the governors are focused on the Medicaid fight, which is likely to come to a head on Capitol Hill in late September.
A consensus among the governors, if one can be reached, would make it considerably easier for Congress to decide how Medicaid money should be divided among the states.
Today, much of the talk at the public meetings, in the corridors and in news conferences and interviews was about Medicaid and the governors' fears that Congress will cut their money without reducing the number of ways they are told to spend it.
Governor Howard Dean of Vermont, a Democrat who heads the governors' group, said at a news conference today that the governors' greatest fear was that by the time Congress finished, "We get all the old rules and less money."
Gov. George V. Voinovich of Ohio, a Republican, said, "We could end up having our money cut and having no flexibility."
In addition to cuts in the growth of spending under the program, Republicans who control Congress have proposed changing the individual Federal Medicaid reimbursements for a system of lump-sum annual payments to states, known as block grants.
The Republican Governor of Texas, George W. Bush, said in an interview that he and other governors from Sun Belt states were worried that any block-grant program, by limiting the amount of money to the states, would not take account of their needs caused by rapid population growth.
Senator Pete V. Domenici, the New Mexico Republican who heads the Senate Budget Committee, told the association's first formal meeting today, "We didn't know the formula would be hard to put together." But he asked for help, saying "Get your act together, Democrats and Republicans."
Republicans, who hold the governorship in 30 states, including most of the largest ones, have been trying to agree among themselves about a Medicaid formula before trying to enlist Democratic allies. They met Saturday and again this morning. But their efforts are making only slow progress, said Gov. Michael O. Leavitt of Utah, who heads the Republican Governors Association.
"This stuff is still in midair," said another Republican, Gov. William F. Weld of Massachusetts.
But Gov. Tommy G. Thompson, the Wisconsin Republican who is about to succeed Mr. Dean as head of the national group, said that the governors were running out of time and that it was essential for both parties to agree because "we need a broad bipartisan coalition." The Medicaid issues were discussed, inconclusively, when the 40 or so governors here met in private after hearing Senator Domenici.
Democratic governors, while eager for fewer restrictions, remain skeptical about block grants and opposed to any diminished Federal commitment to Medicaid. Gov. Evan Bayh of Indiana said he feared the block-grant approach. In a national recession that increased the need for Medicaid, he said, an unchanging Federal contribution would force states to turn away the needy or cut other spending, such as for education.
Another Democrat, Gov. Mike Lowry of Washington, insisted that "the cuts are going to be much deeper than the way they are described." He said that by cutting Medicaid more deeply than Medicare, the health care program for the elderly, Republicans were "attacking those who are less able to defend themselves."
Gov. Roy Romer of Colorado told his Democratic colleagues that he thought it was important that states be required to keep spending at least as much as they do now, rather than take advantage of unconditional block grants to save money for other purposes.
Senator Domenici told the governors their complaints sounded like they were saying, "Don't put too much burden on us on Medicaid but we sure would like less strings."
But Mr. Domenici agreed that in general, fewer restrictions and Federal directives were desirable. Still, he asked, "You do expect to be challenged to use it only for the poor, not for highways, don't you?"
The budget resolution adopted by both houses of Congress calls for a reduction in the rate of growth of Federal Medicaid spending, now going up about 7 percent a year, to only 4 percent a year by 1998. That would save the Federal Government $182 billion over seven years. States now pay about half the costs of Medicaid, which amounts to about 20 percent of all state spending.
The larger Medicare program would be slowed to an 8 percent annual increase. That would save $270 billion. Together, these proposed entitlement cuts are essential components of the Republican effort to balance the budget by 2002.
Creating a system of block grants to replace the main welfare program, Aid to Families with Dependent Children, which consumes about 3 percent of state spending, has produced a similar dispute about the proper distribution formula. But Mr. Dole has pleased many governors here with a proposal that no state get less than it does now, and that some states with fast-growing rolls get extra. Gov. John Engler of Michigan, a Republican, said at today's meeting that he hoped that approach could be followed on Medicaid.
For some governors like George E. Pataki of New York, that would not be enough. Mr. Pataki, a Republican, said in an interview, "Protecting the base in and of itself is not adequate." He said a state like his faced higher costs and some health problems, such as AIDS, that other states did not.
But, Mr. Pataki warned, "a fight over the formula" might hinder the governors' ability to get a block-grant program at all. Therefore, he said, there was a "very real political need to find common ground."
Many Democrats want to continue Medicaid as an individual entitlement, and President Clinton has proposed keeping it that way, but capping per-person spending with limits on annual increases. His proposal would save only $54 billion over seven years.
There is near unanimity in favor of fewer restrictions. A favorite target is an amendment to the Social Security Act that governors contend requires them to pay too much to nursing homes that serve the impoverished elderly.
Governor Bush argued today that there should not be Federal mandates on spending. "I wouldn't mind broad guidelines," he said, such as requiring the states not to use the money for the National Guard.

HEADLINE: To Help Keep Mexico Stable, U.S. Soft-Pedaled Drug War

SERIES: The New Drug Lords: Second of two articles.

BYLINE: By TIM GOLDEN 

DATELINE: MEXICO CITY, July 30

 BODY:
Soon after becoming President at the end of 1988, Carlos Salinas de Gortari sent his new drug-enforcement chief to help rescue Mexico's troubled relationship with the United States.
The official, Javier Coello Trejo, quickly formed a 1,200-member task force, seized tons of drugs and put the most-wanted cocaine smuggler in Mexico behind bars. He brought the Government within reach of its broader goal, sufficiently impressing American officials with Mr. Salinas's resolve to dislodge the drug issue as an obstacle to closer ties between the countries. 
 But six years and much American praise later, law-enforcement officials say that Mexico's traffickers have grown more powerful and that their protection in the security forces remains widespread. Mr. Coello, removed from his post in 1990, was merely the first in a new series of Mexican officials to promise a tougher fight against the traffickers and then be accused of taking their bribes.
While the United States' influence in Mexico has risen steadily in recent years, interviews with dozens of current and former officials and a series of American government documents indicate that Washington has treated Mexico's drug problem as a special case.
Concerned for Mexican stability and the fate of the North American Free Trade Agreement, officials said, the United States often exaggerated the Mexican Government's progress in the fight against drugs, playing down corruption and glossing over failures.
Just as American pressure helped to force the Colombian Government's recent crackdown against the Cali cartel, many of these officials said, the United States might have done much more to block the rise of a new generation of Mexican drug lords.
Major Mexican traffickers were sometimes captured and let go without a public word from American diplomats, the officials said. Slow movement by the Mexican Government to stop the laundering of drug profits was accepted almost patiently. Rather than push for the prosecution of the drug-related corruption, they added, American officials usually wrote off such episodes as unavoidable bumps in a long, bumpy road. Washington's approach was in part a result of misplaced trust, bureaucratic infighting and an abiding fear that Mexican leaders would react to perceived meddling in their affairs by curbing American drug enforcement here, officials said.
Above all, though, American officials said they were kept in check by the desire of the Clinton and Bush Administrations to keep problems of drugs and corruption from jeopardizing the trade accord and the new economic partnership it symbolized.
"People desperately wanted drugs not to become a complicating factor for Nafta," said John P. Walters, a senior official for international drug policy in the Bush White House. "There was a degree of illicit activity that was just accepted."
Some American officials argue that the approach was justified by the greater role that the trade accord has given the United States in Mexico's future. Others say that to have applied greater diplomatic pressure on a problem that is rooted fundamentally in Americans' demand for illegal drugs would have endangered the two countries' improving relationship.
Mr. Salinas's successor, Ernesto Zedillo, has made reform of the legal system a central goal for his six-year term, which began in December. During a surge of Congressional attention to the drug issue as Mexico sought billions of dollars in foreign loans last winter, Mr. Zedillo also agreed privately to a handful of new counternarcotics measures sought by the United States.
Some American drug-enforcement officials now say they have never worked more closely with their Mexican counterparts. But even so, many of the United States officials who deal most closely with the drug issue are pessimistic about Washington's focus and Mexico's future.
"Billions of dollars are flooding into Mexico, and it's going to corrupt everything, and that's not even popping up on peoples' radar screens," said a senior counternarcotics official in Washington. "We spend more time talking about Rwanda."
 
Salinas's Start
Despite Gains, Dark Figures Lurk
By comparison, more experienced officials recall, Mr. Salinas's start was even more impressive.
Eager to renegotiate a $100 billion foreign debt and warned by American officials that stronger ties would require stronger Mexican efforts to stop drugs, Mr. Salinas turned to Mr. Coello, a tough-talking former aide to one of the more feared military governors of southern Chiapas state.
As Mexico's first assistant attorney general for drug enforcement, Mr. Coello called on the head of the Drug Enforcement Administration's office in Mexico City, Edward Heath, and they began planning the capture of Mexico's two most notorious traffickers.
One of Mr. Coello's teams was to hunt for Miguel Angel Felix Gallardo, then the reputed godfather of Mexican cocaine trafficking and a fugitive in the slaying four years earlier in Guadalajara of an American drug-enforcement agent, Enrique S. Camarena. Another was to go after Manuel Salcido, a particularly violent Felix Gallardo associate known as El Cochiloco, or the Mad Pig.
It took Mr. Coello's agents barely four months to bring in Mr. Felix Gallardo and raise drug seizures sharply. With the United States Congress debating Mexico's cooperation in anti-drug efforts, Mr. Heath told reporters that "President Salinas de Gortari means business."
Despite the optimism generated in Washington by the notion that young, honest technocrats had taken power in Mexico City, former Bush Administration officials now say they remained concerned about Mr. Salinas's dependence on some of the darker characters of the old regime.
Mr. Salinas's first Attorney General had been Governor of western Jalisco state when Mr. Camarena was kidnapped there by traffickers and state policemen. His Education Minister, Manuel Bartlett Diaz, had been implicated in the case while serving as Interior Minister.
Former officials said the Justice Department at one point considered trying to indict Mr. Bartlett in connection with the murder. But some officials questioned the evidence, which came from wiretaps, confidential informants and two former drug traffickers. Others said any action against Mr. Bartlett could destabilize the Salinas Government. It was also noted, former officials said, that in his previous job, Mr. Bartlett had helped United States intelligence agencies on matters ranging from the activities of leftist groups in Central America to the local operations of the Soviet and Cuban intelligence agencies.
What may have taken some pressure off the Salinas Government was the Medellin Cartel.
With Colombia racked by the terrorism of its cocaine traffickers, Bolivia struggling with its coca growers and Peru threatened by both drug traffickers and leftist guerrillas, the Bush Administration's drug policy had centered on the Andes from the start. Compared with the $2.2 billion it sought for drug programs there, its investment in Mexico was minimal.
"Mexico was an afterthought," one of the senior officials in charge of the strategy said. "We thought that if we could crush the problem in the Andes and reduce demand here, the corruption problem in Mexico would go down as the transit of drugs went down."
According to officials and drug-policy documents released under the Freedom of Information Act to the National Security Archive, a private research group, the goals of American efforts in Mexico were comparatively mundane: to work more closely with Mexican officials, to establish an operation to track drug-smuggling flights across Mexico and to provide helicopters to ferry Mexican police on drug operations.
The tracking operation was seen as a particular success. But in the long list of goals, several American officials complained that more basic aims like arresting big traffickers and cracking down on corrupt officials often seemed to get lost.
"We all kind of got seduced by certain things that Salinas appeared to be doing," a former Pentagon official said. "Their willingness to take helicopters from us -- it was amazing how their acceptance of this was taken as proof that they were taking the drug threat seriously."
 
Arrests
Some Traffickers Were Shielded
Though Mr. Felix Gallardo was captured in 1989, few other kingpins followed him to jail.
Among the more notable drug figures arrested during the next three years was a lieutenant to the Gulf-coast trafficker Juan Garcia Abrego, who was later charged in Texas with paying some $25 million to bribe high-ranking Mexican officials. Testifying in a separate case last year, another aide to Mr. Garcia Abrego said some of the money had gone to Mr. Coello, the drug-enforcement chief.
Despite accusations of human rights abuses against Mr. Coello and his agents, a classified cablegram to Washington from the United States Ambassador to Mexico, John D. Negroponte, reported that he left office in late 1990 "with an excellent record." Other officials said they saw Mr. Coello as part of a pattern in which Mexican commanders would strike at some traffickers while staying away from others who had presumably bought their protection.
Mr. Garcia Abrego seemed to go especially neglected, officials said. Mr. Salcido was finally killed in 1991, and his death exposed an embarrassing disguise: He had been living on a luxurious ranch in the western state of Colima, breeding horses and entertaining his friend the Governor.
In a report to Congress on Mexico's drug efforts in March 1991, the State Department asserted, "All but two of the major drug traffickers in Mexico have been jailed."
Several officials based in Mexico at the time said that no matter how old the list of traffickers from which the report's authors might have been working, the idea was absurd. Mexican and American officials also acknowledged that at least half a dozen top-level traffickers, including the man now considered Mexico's most powerful cocaine smuggler, Amado Carrillo Fuentes, were arrested during the Salinas Government and quietly freed by corrupt judges or the police.
In the case of Mr. Felix Gallardo, for three years after his arrest in 1989, the "godfather" and his associates were allowed cellular telephones and fax machines to run their smuggling operations from a well-appointed suite cells in a Mexico City jail.
 
Revolving Door
Corrupt Officials Merely Retired
While the system of drug corruption largely persisted under Mr. Salinas, its faces often changed. And with each reshuffling, government documents suggest, American hopes were revived.
When Mr. Coello was replaced at the Attorney General's office by a retired army general, Jorge Carrillo Olea, a State Department report predicted that the move "should improve cooperation" between the Mexican military and federal drug agents under the Attorney General.
But soon after the report appeared, American officials found themselves in the midst of one of the more serious drug incidents of the Salinas administration when army soldiers killed seven federal drug agents who had chased a planeload of drugs to a remote airstrip in the eastern state of Veracruz.
A videotape shot by United States Customs pilots who followed the agents to the scene appeared to show that the soldiers had been protecting the shipment. Autopsy reports showed that some of the agents had been shot dead at point-blank range.
Yet even when the Mexican Government initially tried to cover up the episode, American diplomats bent over backwards to avoid contradicting it, motivated by what one former official called concern for "the bigger picture."
In declassified notes from a closed Congressional briefing, one senior State Department official said the incident exposed the dangers of using "fixed-wing aircraft" instead of helicopters in the drug fight. Ambassador Negroponte called the shootings "a regrettable accident."
Other American officials eventually leaked the true story.
As the political fight over the free trade agreement grew more intense in Washington, the weight of "the bigger picture" on drug controversies grew heavier, many current and former officials said.
"Once Bush and Salinas decided to go with Nafta as the No. 1 goal, then everything else had to be made manageable," said a United States official who dealt extensively with the Mexicans. "On the issue of high-level officials being involved in drugs, we said, 'Carlos, as long as we are getting results, we are not going to micromanage.' "
Some former officials said they had argued that Mr. Salinas's pursuit of the trade pact gave the United States a unique opportunity to press Mexico for stronger measures on corruption and drugs, as it ultimately did on labor and the environment.
"They said we could not make drugs part of the debate," the former Customs Commissioner, Carol B. Hallett, recalled, referring to other senior Bush Administration officials. "I think it was a terrible mistake not to tie the two together."
Some officials question whether a more drastic American stance might have had the desired effect. "Say you got them to remove a few corrupt officials," said a former National Security Council official, "then other ones come in?"
But by a similar logic, other officials argued, the persistence of corruption in the Mexican police and military undermined drug-enforcement efforts to the point of almost making them futile. Operations based on D.E.A. intelligence were so often compromised by corrupt Mexican officials, officials said, that D.E.A. agents refused to work with many federal police commanders.
Mr. Salinas's attorneys general said hundreds of Mexican police agents were dismissed for suspected corruption. The attorneys general themselves were replaced four times in six years. Criminal prosecutions were so rare, however, that some American officials came to view the policy as simply a different form of impunity.
"The strategy was to retire people rather than prosecute them, and we told the Mexicans that that concerned us," said William J. Olson, a former senior State Department counternarcotics official. "They would say, 'Give us information that we can use.' But we were very concerned about compromising our sources and methods by turning over solid intelligence information."
 
New President
Rising Hopes And Some Doubts
Clinton Administration officials said they hoped that they had turned an important page on the drug problem in Mexico when Mr. Zedillo was inaugurated as President on Dec. 1.
In a highly unusual action shortly before the inauguration, officials said the American Ambassador, James R. Jones, presented the new Government with a list of about 15 active and former Mexican officials whom the United States suspected of corruption and hoped not to see in the new administration. An American official said none of those on the list joined the new Government.
Still, American officials who interpreted the Zedillo response to the list as a sign of new times might consult Glenn W. MacTaggart, a Federal prosecutor in San Antonio who wrote to Mexican officials this year requesting the removal of the Mexican Attorney General's attache there.
According to a copy of the confidential letter given to a reporter by a Mexican official, Mr. MacTaggart said the attache had socialized with a suspected drug trafficker, withheld information about the sighting of a political-murder suspect and apparently tried to block the extradition of a Mexican lawyer charged with paying off judges to free the convicted rapist of a young girl.
The attache's conduct, Mr. MacTaggart wrote, "has raised serious security concerns and precludes our willingness to conduct business with him on future law-enforcement matters in this district."
The attache was eventually transferred to Guatemala. But even then, the Zedillo Government would not acknowledge that he had done anything wrong.
A senior Mexican official dismissed Mr. MacTaggart's concerns as the product of unspecified "personal differences" that he said the prosecutor had had with the attache. The attache, who has never denied the accusations, could not be reached. Mr. MacTaggart declined to comment on the incident, except to say, "The letter pretty well speaks for itself."

HEADLINE: MICROSOFT'S MOBILIZATION: OVERVIEW;
Windows of Opportunity for Microsoft

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR 

 BODY:
REDMOND, Wash.
The Microspft Corporation plans to begin selling its 272d software title on Aug. 24.
But it is clear that Windows, 95, the latest version of the software that runs some 100 million of the world's personal computers, is much more than just another new product.
Windows 95 is a sign of the times, already creating a stir in markets worldwide, in Washington and on Wall Street, an indication of the inroads the personal comptuer has made into contemporary commerce and culture.
A video producer is churning out five million copies of a Windows 95 tape, convinced that sales of an instructional video on a software program will dwarf those of past best sellers, like the Cindy Crawford workout tapes.
The test versions of the software that Microsoft has been circulating for months have received microscopic scrutiny, not only for technical flaws but even for political slights. One version had a map with a misdrawn Latin American border, bringing fiery protest from Peru, which is in a border dispute with Ecuador.
Meanwhile, the Justice Department is racing to decide whether to stop Microsoft from using Windows 95 as a springboard for the company's new on-line service.
And the surge in stock prices this year has been led by technology companies, partly in anticipation of increased sales of computer hardware and software that analysts expect in the wake of the Windows 95 introduction on Aug. 24. Should sales of the new Microsoft program lag behind expectations, the stock market may well tumble.
The fascination, hype and controversy surrounding Windows 95 stems largely from the fact that PC's are now ubiquitous in the workplace and are fast becoming a mainstream consumer product. The $85- billion-a-year personal computer industry is recognized as a pillar of the American economy. And as a gateway to the Internet, cyberspace and multimedia, the personal computer affects everything from how people communicate with each other to how children are educated.
Even Brad Chase, the 34-year-old architect of Microsoft's marketing strategy for Windows 95, says he is amazed by all the attention the product has received. And he readily admits that the phenomenon is only partly explained by the big-budget marketing campaign he has led for the last year from Microsoft's pine-covered corporate campus here, outside Seattle.
"The breadth of interest in Windows 95 is because of all these things, from the Internet to the Justice Department investigation, coming together, all these stars getting into line at once," Mr. Chase said. "Windows 95 is a lot more than software."
And how is a single software program linked to all these larger trends and events? Simply put, because Windows 95 is a new operating system and because it is Microsoft's. An operating system controls the on-screen look of a personal computer and the keyboard-and-mouse steps required to use it, and more than 80 percent of all PC's use Microsoft's operating software. So when Microsoft overhauls its operating program, it is changing the language of personal computing.
Windows 95, by all accounts, is the most significant change Microsoft has made in its operating program since 1990. That was the year the company introduced its first Windows system, which borrowed an approach from Apple Computer's Macintosh by enabling a user to point and click on graphical icons instead of typing arcane commands.
Since then, Microsoft has improved Windows, notably with its Windows 3.1 version in 1992. But Windows 95 is an entirely new operating system built with 11 million lines of programming code, compared with 3 million lines for Windows 3.1.
All operating systems have their irritating quirks and shortcomings, and Windows 95, priced at less than $100, is no exception. Some existing programs do not yet run smoothly on Windows 95, and it requires more computer hard-disk space and memory to use than the previous generation of Windows. But analysts agree that Windows 95 represents a big step forward in making computers less confusing for someone writing a report, browsing the Internet or installing a new printer.
Technology purists point out that some of the new features of Microsoft's new system, like being able to have lengthy file names instead of the eight-character limit of Windows 3.1, are advantages that rival operating systems -- the Apple Macintosh and I.B.M.'s OS/2 -- have had for years. Yet even with a technical edge, Microsoft's competitors have not been able to challenge the company's dominance of the operating system market, which it attained shortly after I.B.M. chose Microsoft's Disk Operating System for the I.B.M. PC in 1980.
By now, in the PC operating system market, Microsoft has no genuine rival. The company's extraordinary marketing campaign, with an estimated budget of $150 million, is intended less to fend off competition than to move computer companies and users -- quickly -- to the new industry standard, Windows 95.
"For Microsoft, the marketing campaign is essentially a $150 million insurance policy to make sure that nothing happens to prevent Windows 95 from becoming by far the most successful product in the history of the business," said David B. Yoffie, a professor at the Harvard Business School.
If Microsoft makes Windows 95 a success, it will have plenty of help from the rest of the personal computer industry. Dataquest, a market research firm, estimates that in addition to Microsoft's $150 million, other hardware and software companies, retailers and distributors will spend $350 million this year to market Windows 95 and their own Windows 95-related products.
Other software makers, in particular, tend to have a hate-you/need-you relationship with Microsoft. As the dominant producer, Microsoft makes not only operating software but also applications like word processing programs, data bases, spreadsheets, educational software and games. So it is the chief rival of most applications-software companies in the industry.
But because the software makers' products run on the Microsoft operating system, they have been rushing to develop products for the new Windows 95 platform.
A successful rollout of Windows 95 in the early months is vital to Microsoft, both to its corporate pride and its profits. Analysts' sales estimates vary considerably for the last four months of 1995, following the Aug. 24 debut. At the high end, Dataquest is forecasting sales of 29 million copies this year. International Data Corporation, another research firm, predicts 20 million. Another firm, Inteco, estimates it will be only 14 million.
At roughly $50 in revenue to Microsoft for each copy sold, the difference between a torrid and a not-so-fast rollout could make a striking difference in near-term revenues and profits. Selling 29 million copies this year means roughly $1.45 billion in revenue to Microsoft, while 14 million copies would be $700 million.
Responsibility for seeing that the Windows 95 marketing goes quickly and smoothly rests largely on the shoulders of Mr. Chase, the general manager of Microsoft's personal systems division, and seven group product managers. They are all men in their early 30's -- seasoned veterans by Microsoft standards. They are mainly M.B.A.'s from places like Wharton, Harvard and Northwestern. Most started in other fields, then recognized that technology was the future, and headed to Redmond.
Mr. Chase wrote the marketing blueprint one night, shortly before a March 1994 meeting with Bill Gates, the Microsoft chairman. It sketched out what Mr. Chase called the "E-strategy" for Educate, Excite and Engage, and it explained that the personal computer market has broadened so much since the introduction of Windows 3.1 in 1992, nearly tenfold, that an entirely different approach was needed this time.
And different it has been. The company has distributed 400,000 beta, or test, copies of Windows 95, many of them to corporate users licensed to put the software on more than one PC. By Aug. 24, an estimated million people will have used Windows 95 for nearly 75 million hours. This means that even before it officially hits the market, Windows 95 will have received more use than all but a few of the most successful software products receive in their entire several-year product cycles.
For more than a year, Microsoft has been bringing software developers, PC makers, corporate customers and members of the computer press to Redmond for briefing and cajoling. The company presented demonstrations to 50,000 individual customers during a 23-city, three-month tour of the United States..
Microsoft sent the finished code for Windows 95 to factories on July 14. And the company has revved up seven times its normal production capacity, contracting with 12 plants and engaging a fleet of 500 trucks, to insure that the new product will be at 20,000 retail outlets, from CompUSA to Wal-Mart, on Aug. 24. Consumers are expected to move quickly to Windows 95, particularly since it will be preloaded on the machines produced for the Christmas season by manufacturers like Compaq, Packard Bell, Gateway and Dell.
But corporate customers traditionally wait six to nine months to buy new operating systems, allowing the early programming bugs to be eliminated. To try to get companies to move quickly this time, Microsoft and distributors have offered large customers hundreds of free copies as an incentive to buy others. Companies that have agreed to deploy Windows 95 on many desktops by Aug. 24 include Ford, Dow Chemical, Sprint and Deloitte Touche.
Yet it seems doubtful how quickly even the Microsoft marketing arsenal can prompt companies to move to Windows 95. Surveys show most companies would wait until next year to make the transition.
"There's no question about it," Mr. Chase said, "inertia is our biggest competitor."

 CORRECTION:
An article in Business Day last Monday about the Microsoft Corporation's new Windows 95 operating system software misstated the date of the first Windows version. It was 1985, not 1990.
